403 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Held v. State of New York Workers' Comp. Bd.


509642


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


85 A.D.3d 35; 921 N.Y.S.2d 674; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3095; 2011 NY Slip Op 3155; 245 N.Y.L.J. 80



April 21, 2011, Decided 
April 21, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed without opinion by, Leave to appeal denied by Held v. State Workers' Comp. Bd., 17 N.Y.3d 837, 954 N.E.2d 1157, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 2120, 930 N.Y.S.2d 532 (2011)
Subsequent appeal at Matter of Held v. State of N.Y. Workers' Compensation Bd., 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1282 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, Feb. 28, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, members of group self-insured trusts, sued defendants, which included the New York State Workers' Compensation Board, challenging defendants' assessments against them under Workers' Compensation Law § 50(5). The Supreme Court Albany County, New York, granted plaintiffs summary judgment as to their claim that the assessments were an unconstitutional taking and ordered the Board to repay the assessments. Both sides appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs argued that § 50(5) did not authorize the Board to levy administrative expense assessments against them for the unpaid compensation and benefits owed by defaulted group self-insurers. The appellate court held that it was clear that group self-insurers were intended to be included among those to be assessed to provide the funds to cover the defaults of all private self-insurers, including groups, under § 50(5). Section 50(5) did not violate U.S. Const. amend. V's Takings Clause because 1) while the amounts of the assessments might have been unanticipated, their economic effect on plaintiffs did not rise to the level of a taking; 2) levying assessments against group self-insurers was consistent with the other provisions of the Workers' Compensation Law; 3) and plaintiffs knew or should have known of their potential liability for the assessments. Section 50(5) did not violate due process because it was sufficiently clear and, having been enacted before plaintiffs existed, was not retroactively applied to them. As § 50(5) could not violate contracts that did not exist when it was enacted, it did not violate the Contracts Clause of U.S. Const. art I, § 10.


OUTCOME: The judgment partially granting plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment and partially denying defendants' cross motion for summary judgment was reversed, summary judgment was awarded to defendants, and the complaint was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Workers' Compensation -- Coverage -- Assessments against Group Self-Insured Trusts 

1. The Workers' Compensation Board was authorized to levy assessments against plaintiffs, members of group self-insured trusts, for the unpaid compensation and benefits owed by defaulted group self-insured trusts pursuant to Workers' Compensation Law § 50 (5) (former [f]) and (g), as a fair reading of the statute, within the context of the related provisions and legislative history, revealed that group self-insurers were intended to be included among those to be assessed to provide the funds to cover the defaults of all private self-insurers, including groups. Workers' Compensation Law § 50 (3-a) subjects group self-insurers to the same statutory provisions governing individual self-insured employers, and former paragraph (f), which is among the provisions relating to self-insurance deemed applicable to group self-insurance, was intended to apply to all self-insurers as evidenced by its legislative history inasmuch as its purpose was to establish a method for paying claims of an insolvent self-insurer out of administrative assessments to be levied against all private self-insurers. Moreover, the legislative history of the 2008 amendments to the Workers' Compensation Law, which relabeled former paragraph (f) as paragraph (g) and expressly included group self-insurers within the meaning of the term "self-insured employers," made clear that the Board was always authorized to impose assessments with respect to both individual and group self-insurers. Plaintiffs had always been subject to assessments by the Board to cover defaulted individual self-insured employers, and while the Board did not include assessments to cover its payment of the compensation liabilities of insolvent self-insured groups until 2007, self-insured groups had not defaulted prior thereto. Furthermore, allowing assessments against all self-insurers was not inconsistent with the contractual obligation of joint and several liability assumed by the individual members of a group self-insurer.

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Property -- Workers' Compensation -- Assessments against Group Self-Insured Trusts

2. The Workers' Compensation Board's application of Workers' Compensation Law § 50 (5) (former [f]) and (g) to plaintiffs, members of group self-insured trusts, levying assessments for the unpaid compensation and benefits owed by defaulted group self-insured trusts did not violate the Takings Clause of the Fifth Amendment. While the amounts of the assessments may have been unanticipated and plaintiffs may have been deprived of substantial amounts of money to pay them, their economic effect on plaintiffs did not rise to the level of a taking as their liability directly depended on their proportional role in the self-insurance program and the workers' compensation system. Under the statute, the costs associated with defaulting self-insurers are to be assessed against all self-insurers, and plaintiffs were self-insurers. Moreover, the levying of assessments against self-insurers was not inconsistent with other provisions of the Workers' Compensation Law, which employs such a mechanism against employers and insurance carriers to recoup the system's expenses. The application of former paragraph (f) and paragraph (g) also did not interfere with plaintiffs' reasonable investment-backed expectations as the enforcement of the statute was consistent with the legislative scheme which was sufficiently clear from the language of the statute, and thus plaintiffs knew or should have known of their liability for administrative expense assessments. As voluntary participants who elected to exercise the privilege of self-insurance, plaintiffs could not complain that the assessments imposed to administer the self-insurance program consistently with the purpose and goals of the workers' compensation system was an unexpected taking of their property. Furthermore, the character of the government action at issue was a public program adjusting the benefits and burdens of economic life to promote the common good, and, as such, was not generally the type of regulation considered to rise to the level of a taking.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Workers' Compensation -- Assessments against Group Self-Insured Trusts

3. The Workers' Compensation Board's application of Workers' Compensation Law § 50 (5) (former [f]) and (g) to plaintiffs, members of group self-insured trusts, levying assessments for the unpaid compensation and benefits owed by defaulted group self-insured trusts was not unconstitutional under the Due Process Clause of the Fifth Amendment. Former paragraph (f) was sufficiently clear, to a reasonable degree of certainty, so that plaintiffs were not forced to guess at its meaning. Moreover, inasmuch as the statute authorized the assessments at issue since its enactment in 1976, prior to plaintiffs' existence, they had notice of it and it was not retroactively applied to them. The statute also did not violate the Contracts Clause as a statute cannot be said to impair a contract that did not exist at the time of its enactment.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Workers' Compensation -- Continuing Assessments on Inactive Group Self-Insured Trusts

4. The due process challenge by plaintiffs, members of group self-insured trusts, to the 2008 amendments to Workers' Compensation Law § 15 (8) (h) (4) and § 151 (2) (b), which adjusted the formula used to determine continuing assessments, decreasing over time, on inactive self-insurers for the administrative expenses of the Special Disability Fund and the Workers' Compensation Board, was without merit. Legislation readjusting rights and burdens is not unlawful solely because it upsets otherwise settled expectations. Here, the 2008 amendments--which changed the method for determining the amounts owed by inactive group self-insurers from a calculation based on annual payroll for the preceding year, resulting from 2007 amendments, to a calculation based on payroll at the time the individual or group self-insurer ceases to self-insure, reduced by a factor reflecting the reduction in compensation liabilities since becoming inactive--did not impose an unfair exit penalty. Nor did the 2008 amendments improperly alter plaintiffs' liability without notice or justification as there is no requirement to provide notice of enactment of a statute beyond the normal process of enactment, publication and an opportunity for those within the statute's reach to become familiar with its requirements and to comply with them. Moreover, the legislative history of the 2008 amendments revealed that, to the extent the 2007 amendments allowed inactive groups to completely avoid liability for the assessments after one year of inactivity despite their continuing, although decreasing, liability for compensation and participation in the program, the limitation was error, it was promptly corrected and was not a settled expectation. Additionally, plaintiffs were not subjected to double assessments by the 2008 amendments since an inactive group's assessment were expressly reduced over time based on the group's diminishing compensation liabilities, and the assessments were therefore proportional to plaintiffs' compensation liabilities. Furthermore, the 2008 amendments did not effect a taking inasmuch as prior to 2007, inactive groups were responsible for paying continued assessments based on claims that accrued while they were self-insured, and the continuation of that responsibility, albeit by a different calculation, did not interfere with any reasonable investment-backed expectation, nor did they have a disproportionate effect on plaintiffs as assessments are applied to all self-insurers and insurance carriers in the workers' compensation system.

 

404 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of New York Constr. Materials Assn., Inc. v. New York State Dept. of Envtl. Conservation


510593


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


83 A.D.3d 1323; 921 N.Y.S.2d 686; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3109; 2011 NY Slip Op 3165



April 21, 2011, Decided 
April 21, 2011, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant producers and providers brought a combined proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 and action for declaratory judgment, to, among other things, declare ECL 19-0323 to be unconstitutional and annul certain regulations promulgated by respondent Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC). The Supreme Court, Saratoga County (New York), dismissed the application. The producers and the providers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The producers and the providers challenged 6 NYCRR 248-1.1(10), (20), (28) and argued that the DEC's interpretation of the Diesel Emissions Reduction Act (DERA), ECL 19-0323, language imposing diesel emission controls on vehicles operated "on behalf of" the State exceeded its authority. The appellate court found that prior legal usage did not support the DEC's broad construction. The phrase "on behalf of" was legislatively used to connote a direct representative or agency relationship. The DEC's interpretation of the term "on behalf of" to mean a class including but not limited to prime contractors, subcontractors, and sub-subcontractors extended well beyond the phrase's commonly accepted legal meaning. Nothing in the language of the Diesel Emissions Reduction Act, ECL 19-0323, construed as a whole, indicated that the legislature intended it to extend beyond prime contractors. The legislature did not intend to impose the requirements of the Diesel Emissions Reduction Act, ECL 19-0323, on vehicles other than those used by prime contractors under direct contract with state agencies and public authorities, as defined in 6 NYCRR 248-1.1(23).


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified by reversing so much thereof as declared that 6 NYCRR 248-1.1(10), (20), (28) were promulgated in compliance with the Diesel Emissions Reduction Act and dismissed the petition on that ground. The petition was reinstated to that extent and 6 NYCRR 248-1.1(10), (20), (28) were annulled. As so modified, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law--Judicial Review--Regulations Not Entitled to Judicial Deference--Agency's Lack of Authority to Make Policy Determinations as to Scope of Statute's Coverage.--In proceeding seeking to annul regulations interpreting Diesel Emissions Reduction Act (DERA) (L 2006, ch 629, §§ 1, 2; ECL 19-0323), regulations promulgated by Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) imposing law's diesel emission controls on vehicles "on behalf of" state were not entitled to judicial deference; deference is required when technical expertise or specialized knowledge of operational practices is required to interpret statutory language, but no such expertise was required to construe phrase "on behalf of"; further, nothing in DERA indicates that deference should be accorded on ground that Legislature delegated comprehensive, interpretive and subordinate policymaking authority to DEC on subject of diesel emission controls; DERA was not enacted in broad outline, with details left to DEC to arrange, but instead lays out detailed requirements, defines several technical terms and specifies, among other things, precise timetable for compliance, particular grounds on which DEC may grant waivers, and details that agency must include in annual reports; given such specificity, DERA's direction to DEC to promulgate regulations as necessary and appropriate to carry out law's provisions did not confer authority on DEC to make policy determinations as to scope of statute's coverage.

Administrative Law--Judicial Review--Regulations Promulgated in Excess of Agency's Authority Annulled.--Regulations promulgated by Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) interpreting Diesel Emissions Reduction Act (DERA) (L 2006, ch 629, §§ 1, 2; ECL 19-0323) were annulled as in excess of agency's authority; Legislature did not intend to impose DERA's requirements on vehicles other than those used by prime contractors under direct contract with state agencies and public authorities, as defined in 6 NYCRR 248-1.1 (b) (23), but definitions of "on behalf of," "contractor," and "subcontractor" in 6 NYCRR 248-1.1 (b) (10), (20) and (28) imposed DERA's diesel emission controls on broad segment of private sector including subcontractors, sub-subcontractors, and others who did not contract directly with state; regulations did not violate Ex Post Facto Clause of US Constitution, however, as no retroactive enforcement had occurred, and DEC had exercised its enforcement discretion after promulgating regulations by indicating it would not enforce violations or reporting requirements for 2008 and 2009.

 

405 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Hughes


2009-07374


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


83 A.D.3d 960; 921 N.Y.S.2d 300; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3169; 2011 NY Slip Op 3262



April 19, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal granted by People v. Hughes, 19 N.Y.3d 961, 973 N.E.2d 211, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 2445, 950 N.Y.S.2d 113 (2012)
Affirmed by People v. Hughes, 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 3153 (N.Y., Nov. 19, 2013)


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Possession of Weapon--Possession of Firearm in Home of Defendant Previously Convicted of Crime--Constitutionality.--Defendant's convictions of criminal possession of weapon in second and third degrees was affirmed; Penal Law article 265, which criminalizes possession of firearm or loaded firearm, even in home, where defendant has previously been convicted of crime, does not effect absolute ban on possession of firearms and are, therefore, not "severe restriction" improperly infringing upon defendant's Second Amendment rights; rather, statutes represent policy determination by Legislature that illegal weapon is more dangerous in hands of convicted criminal than in possession of novice to criminal justice system; statutory scheme is consistent with United States Supreme Court's determination that, although individuals may have constitutional right to bear arms in home for self-defense, that right is not unlimited and may properly be subject to certain prohibitions; accordingly, challenged statutes do not violate Second Amendment, and they are not unconstitutionally overbroad.

Matter of Corporate Woods 11, LP v. Board of Assessment Review of the Town of Colonie


510768


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


83 A.D.3d 1250; 921 N.Y.S.2d 380; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2930; 2011 NY Slip Op 2989



April 14, 2011, Decided 
April 14, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Matter of Corporate Woods 11, LP v Board of Assessment Review of The Town of Colonie, 17 NY3d 707, 954 NE2d 89, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 2055, 929 NYS2d 798 (N.Y., Sept. 8, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner owner brought a proceeding pursuant to RPTL art. 7 seeking review of a refusal by respondent board to consider the owner's property tax assessment grievance. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), granted the board's motion to dismiss the petition. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner's tenant had earlier brought a tax certiorari proceeding. The board claimed that, based on this earlier proceeding, the three-year repose period of RPTL 727(1) barred the owner's grievance. The owner argued that the tenant was not "aggrieved" within the meaning of RPTL 704(1) and thus lacked standing to commence the prior proceeding. The appellate court found that the record supported the trial court's determination that the owner received actual notice that the tenant was conducting the tax certiorari proceeding while time remained to object to the tenant's standing. The owner's acquiescence and assistance supported the finding that the owner was in privity with the tenant for collateral estoppel purposes. Considering the owner's knowledge of the prior proceeding, its longstanding contractual relationship with the tenant, and the alignment of their interests in reducing their property tax liability, the statutory purposes of RPTL 727(1) were best served by deeming the owner to be in privity with the tenant in the prior proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Judgments--Collateral Estoppel--Full and Fair Opportunity to Litigate--Whether Party Had Actual Notice of Prior Proceeding

Judgments--Collateral Attack--Existence of Privity--Representation of Party's Interests in Prior Proceeding

 

407 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


For The People Theatres of N.Y. Inc. v City of New York


38473848, 121080/02, 121197/02


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


84 A.D.3d 48; 923 N.Y.S.2d 11; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2751; 2011 NY Slip Op 2816



April 7, 2011, Decided 
April 7, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Findings of fact/conclusions of law at For the People Theatres of NY, Inc. v. City of New York, 38 Misc. 3d 663, 954 N.Y.S.2d 801, 2012 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 5275 (Aug. 30, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff adult establishments brought actions against defendant city, challenging the 2001 Amendments to the Zoning Resolution of the City of New York, N.Y (2001 Amendments). Following an earlier appeal, the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), among other things, found that the 2001 Amendments were constitutional with regard to bookstores, video stores, and topless night clubs and bars. The adult establishments appealed.


OVERVIEW: In its decision, the Court of Appeal of New York established a study of the Department of City Planning, as well as other studies and court decisions, as the basis for assessing the claim that "60/40" businesses no longer continued to resemble the kinds of adult uses that were shown to cause negative secondary effects. The appellate court held that the trial court did not elaborate on the criteria by which it determined that the adult establishments' essential nature was similar or dissimilar to the sexually explicit adult uses that were analyzed in those studies and case law. Moreover, it failed to state the facts on which it based its judgment. The trial court's judgment was insufficient to answer the question posed on remittitur. The decision was "manifestly inadequate" and violated CPLR 4213(b). The trial court also erred in finding that the adult establishments' argument that reconstituted 60/40 clubs no longer resembled their pre-1995 forbears was entirely irrelevant. This deprived the adult establishments of their opportunity to be heard. There was no merit to the city's contention that adult establishments' "as-applied" issue was neither raised nor preserved.


OUTCOME: The case was remitted to the trial court for a decision setting forth its findings of fact as to the facial challenge. The finding of constitutionality was vacated.


HEADNOTES

Trial -- Decision -- Failure to State Facts Supporting Decision

1. In related actions challenging a municipal zoning ordinance regulating adult establishments, the Supreme Court's decision violated CPLR 4213 (b) because it did not elaborate on the criteria by which it determined that the essential nature of plaintiffs' business was similar or dissimilar to the sexually explicit adult uses that were analyzed in studies and case law and found to have negative secondary effects on the community. It failed to state the particular facts on which it based its judgment; rather, it simply detailed the municipality's evidence and arrived at legal conclusions. That was insufficient to answer the question posed on remittitur from the Court of Appeals--namely, whether certain establishments were similar in nature to adult establishments that had been shown by means of empirical data to cause negative secondary effects. As Supreme Court did not provide any direction for the parties or appellate courts to adequately review, analyze, or understand the ruling, its decision was manifestly inadequate and violated the dictates of CPLR 4213 (b). Given the scale of the trial record below, which ran into the hundreds of pages, it was impossible to properly review on appeal Supreme Court's conclusions of law without the benefit of established findings of fact.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Zoning Amendments Restricting Adult Establishments

2. In assessing the validity of amendments to a municipal law regulating adult establishments, the trial court, on remand, must compare "self- identified" 60/40 businesses--those which devoted 40% of their area to adult activities--with adult establishments found in a municipal study to create negative secondary effects on the community, so as to determine whether the 60/40 businesses retained a predominant focus on sexually explicit materials. The amendments sought to include as adult establishments, businesses that apparently complied with the 60/40 rule but, because of other factors, retained their character as adult establishments. Supreme Court must determine the particular characteristics associated with the promotion of sexually explicit materials. The negative characteristics identified by the Supreme Court would serve as baseline for adjudicating whether the 60/40 businesses had so transformed their character as to no longer resemble adult businesses that create negative secondary effects. Using that baseline, Supreme Court would then need to determine whether the 60/40 businesses had a similar predominant focus on sexually explicit materials. In using the municipal study to assess the constitutionality of the amendments in question, the court should consider if the 60/40 businesses have a predominant focus on sexually explicit materials.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Zoning Amendments Restricting Adult Establishments

3. In an action challenging the validity of amendments to a municipal law regulating adult establishments, in which the municipality proffered evidence in support of its claim that 60/40 businesses, i.e., those which devote 40% of their area to adult activities, displayed a predominant, ongoing focus on sexually explicit materials or activities, the trial court, on remand, must assess whether that evidence, and any additional evidence that the municipality wishes to adduce, entitle it to a judgment in its favor on a theory that 60/40 businesses retain the character of adult businesses which create negative secondary effects on the community. The municipality's evidence is subject to "intermediate scrutiny." Very little evidence is required to uphold the constitutionality of the amendments, which sought to include as adult establishments businesses that apparently complied with the 60/40 rule but, because of other factors, retained their character as adult establishments. The municipality's evidence must be relevant to the issue of whether the 60/40 businesses have so transformed their character that they no longer resemble adult businesses that created negative secondary effects: it must establish that the materials that formed the basis of its justification for the amendments were indeed of an adult character. Moreover, the municipality's evidence must also present a fair snapshot of the businesses that are the object of its claim.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Zoning Amendments Restricting Adult Establishments

4. In an action challenging the validity of amendments to a municipal law regulating adult establishments, in which the municipality proffered evidence in support of its claim that 60/40 businesses, i.e., those which devote 40% of their area to adult activities, displayed a predominant, ongoing focus on sexually explicit materials or activities, the trial court erred in finding irrelevant and according no weight to plaintiffs' evidence that those reconstituted 60/40 clubs no longer resemble 100% adult businesses which were found to create negative secondary effects on the community. In so doing, it deprived plaintiffs of their opportunity to be heard by depriving them of their ability to cast doubt on the municipality's rationale for its ordinance, which sought to include as adult establishments those that apparently complied with the 60/40 rule but because of other factors retained their character as adult businesses. On remittitur, Supreme Court must therefore address any relevant evidence proffered by the plaintiffs to show that there has been a significant change in the character of 60/40 businesses. However, the court is not to consider evidence that is irrelevant to the question of whether those establishments continue to have a predominant focus on sexually explicit materials.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Zoning Amendments Restricting Adult Establishments -- Evidentiary Standard

5. In assessing the validity of amendments to a municipal law regulating adult establishments, the trial court, on remand, should assess the municipality's evidence in light of a somewhat heightened standard of proof: notwithstanding the simplified nature of proof required of municipalities by the United States Supreme Court and the New York Court of Appeals, imposing a level of intermediate scrutiny requires more conviction of the connection between legislative ends and means than does the rational basis standard, but only in the sense of evidence that is reasonably believed to be relevant to the secondary effects generated by adult businesses.

Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review -- "As Applied" Constitutional Challenge to Municipal Ordinance Regulating Adult Businesses

6. In related actions challenging the constitutionality of a municipal ordinance regulating adult businesses, plaintiffs' "as applied" challenge was preserved for appellate review where plaintiffs specifically raised the issue of such a challenge during a colloquy wherein the court posed the question of what might happen if it decided that some clubs didn't have a dominant sexual purpose in their activities, but others did.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Zoning Amendments Restricting Adult Establishments -- "As Applied" Challenge

7. In judicial challenges to the constitutionality of amendments to a municipal ordinance regulating adult establishments, given the possibility that the amendments could curtail legitimate free speech, an "as applied" challenge would be an appropriate means to challenge them if the facial challenge against the ordinance failed. In upholding the amendments, Supreme Court simply stated that "[m]erely because [plaintiff has] introduced evidence that some topless clubs may not have an ongoing focus on adult activities, does not defeat the pattern established by [the municipality] of topless clubs having an ongoing focus of adult activities." Thus, the court gave no indication of any facts it took into account in arriving at its decision on the "as applied" challenge, or whether it even considered them. Neither its decision nor judgment made any factual findings to help resolve the question of whether only some of the clubs challenging the amendments were found to have a predominantly sexual focus or whether all of them were. The result of the court's decision was that some of the clubs that had aspects of an adult business, but were not primarily devoted to adult activities, could be put out of business by a law that might not apply to them. Accordingly, the matter was remitted for a decision setting forth findings of fact as to plaintiffs' "as applied" challenge.

409 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Ricket v Mahan


511014


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


82 A.D.3d 1565; 919 N.Y.S.2d 588; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2435; 2011 NY Slip Op 2520



March 31, 2011, Decided 
March 31, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended April 15, 2011.
Subsequent appeal at Matter of Ricket v. Mahan, 97 A.D.3d 1062, 949 N.Y.S.2d 272, 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5712 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, July 26, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), among other things, dismissed petitioner resident's application, in a combined proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 and action for declaratory judgment, to, among other things, review two resolutions of respondent town board appointing respondents commissioner and a personnel officer. All parties cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The resident argued that the commissioner's appointment was invalid because he did not reside in the town, nor did he possess the qualifications established for this position by the town in its local law. The appellate court found that, since the local law was silent as to whether the commissioner had to be a town resident, the issue was whether state law served to impose such a requirement. The town, when it enacted the local law, made no reference to any state statute, nor did it identify in the local law any state statute that it intended to supercede. Pursuant to Town Law §§ 20(1)(a), 23(1), the commissioner was a town officer who had to be a town resident. Since the commissioner acknowledged that he was not a town resident when he was appointed, and did not intend to become one in the future, his appointment did not comport with relevant state law and was invalid. As to the personnel officer, Town Law § 64(5) was controlling and, thus, as the trial court found, the personnel officer's appointment had to be limited to the remainder of his predecessor's unexpired term.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified by reversing so much thereof as dismissed that part of the petition/complaint seeking a declaration that the resolution appointing the commissioner was void; the petition was granted to that extent. As so modified, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Public Officers--Eligibility for Office.--Respondent Town Board's appointment of Commissioner of Public Works was invalid because individual appointed did not reside in Town; since local law was silent as to whether Commissioner of Public Works must be town resident, issue was whether state law served to impose such requirement; under Public Officers Law § 3 (1) and Town Law § 23 (1), if town enacts local law creating public or civil office and person appointed to it is town officer, appointee must be town resident; since neither Public Officers Law nor Town Law defines what constitutes public or civil office or who qualifies as town officer, that determination depends upon nature of position, its role in town governance and whether position involved has responsibilities that require high degree of initiative and independent judgment; here, Commissioner of Public Works took oath of office, served two-year term, and according to Town Code, was principal executive officer and administrative head of Department of Public Works; given nature of position, and crucial role it played providing essential services for Town, Commissioner of Public Works was town officer who was required to be town resident.

Constitutional Law--Gift of Public Funds

People v Williams


33 KA 09-01489


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


82 A.D.3d 1576; 919 N.Y.S.2d 608; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2222; 2011 NY Slip Op 2153

411 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


South Liberty Partners, L.P. v Town of Haverstraw


2009-11308


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


82 A.D.3d 956; 918 N.Y.S.2d 563; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1974; 2011 NY Slip Op 1995



March 15, 2011, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, town-A and the town regional sewage board, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Rockland County (New York), which granted a motion by plaintiff limited partnership (LP) to strike defendants' fourth affirmative defense, and denied their motion for summary judgment. Defendant town-B also appealed the grant of the LP's motion to strike various affirmative defenses. The matter involved a sewer unit connection fee.


OVERVIEW: The LOP sought, inter alia, a declaratory judgment that a sewer unit connection fee imposed pursuant to Haverstraw, N.Y., Code §§ 137-21 and 137-22 was unconstitutional and that the adoption of the fee by the board and the enforcement thereof was invalid. Various affirmative defenses asserted by defendants were stricken. The trial court also denied a motion by town-A and the board for summary judgment dismissal of the complaint. On appeal, the court found that the LP could not have raised the challenge to the constitutionality of §§ 137-21 and 137-22 in a CPLR art. 78 proceeding. Such a proceeding was not a proper vehicle to challenge the constitutionality of a legislative enactment. Accordingly, the claims challenging the constitutionality thereof were governed by the six-year limitations period of CPLR 213(1). Further, they were not time-barred based on the accrual date of the claims. However, claims regarding the validity of the adoption and enforcement of the fee were administrative actions that were reviewable in an art. 78 proceeding. Accordingly, those claims were time-barred.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the trial court by substituting provisions that denied the motion to strike the fourth affirmative defense asserted by town-A and the board, granted summary judgment dismissal of the declaratory judgment claims regarding adoption and enforcement of the fee to town-A and the board, and denied the motion to strike one of town-B's affirmative defenses. As modified, the order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions--What Statute Governs--Declaratory Judgment Action.--Since plaintiffs could not have raised their challenge to constitutionality and substance of defendant town's code provisions in CPLR article 78 proceeding, which is unavailable to challenge validity of legislative act except where challenge is directed at procedures followed in its enactment, plaintiffs' claims were subject to six-year limitations period set forth in CPLR 213 (1).

Limitation of Actions--When Cause of Action Accrues

Limitation of Actions--What Statute Governs--Declaratory Judgment Action.--Plaintiffs' claims for judgment declaring that adoption by defendant joint regional sewage board of sewer unit connection fee and enforcement of such fee were invalid should have been brought in CPLR article 78 proceeding, since those were administrative actions; although sewage board derived its authority to adopt charges and fees pursuant to town code, challenged sewer unit connection fee was adopted by means of resolution and applied to plaintiffs by contractual agreements between municipalities; accordingly, claims against sewage board were time-barred.

414 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Aydin v Commissioner of Taxation & Fin.


508772


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 1203; 917 N.Y.S.2d 427; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1333; 2011 NY Slip Op 1384



February 24, 2011, Decided 
February 24, 2011, Entered


HEADNOTES

Taxation--Sales and Use Taxes--When Double Taxation Permissible

Taxation--Sales and Use Taxes--Impermissible Sales Tax Pyramid--What Constitutes.--Calculation of taxable sales of fuel and cigarettes at petitioner's gas stations and convenience stores pursuant to Tax Law § 1111 (h) and (k) did not result in impermissible double taxation or prohibited sales tax pyramid; imposition of double taxation is permissible as long as Legislature expresses clear and distinct intention to do so, and here Legislature's intention to impose double tax is clear from unambiguous language of Tax Law § 1111 (h) and (k), which specify that cigarette excise tax and petroleum business tax are to be included in respective receipts subject to sales tax; further, same statutory provisions do not create impermissible pyramid, since pyramiding of sales taxes occurs only when businesses are taxed upon purchase of component materials or equipment used to create products that are taxed when sold to consumer; Tax Law § 1111 (h) and (k) are not unconstitutional, as Legislature has nearly unconstrained authority in design of taxing measures unless they are utterly unreasonable or arbitrary.

 

415 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Town of Oyster Bay v Kirkland


2009-11092, 2010-02720


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 812; 917 N.Y.S.2d 236; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1259; 2011 NY Slip Op 1265



February 15, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Town of Oyster Bay v. Kirkland, 17 N.Y.3d 778, 952 N.E.2d 1073, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 1679, 929 N.Y.S.2d 79 (2011)
Leave to appeal granted by Town of Oyster Bay v. Kirkland, 17 N.Y.3d 716, 958 N.E.2d 553, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3232, 934 N.Y.S.2d 374 (2011)
Affirmed by Town of Oyster Bay v. Kirkland, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 2725 (N.Y., Oct. 18, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which denied its motion for a preliminary injunction and dismissed the amended complaint, seeking to enjoin defendant, the Division of Human Rights (DHR), from proceeding with an administrative complaint against the town, alleging violations of the Human Rights Law. The town also appealed an order denying its motion for reargument.


OVERVIEW: The town amended the zoning code to create new zoning districts for building below-market rate senior communities and below-market, price-controlled housing for first time homebuyers. The code contained required residency preferences and accompanying restrictive covenants. The DHR filed an administrative complaint against the town and others connected to developments constructed under the housing programs, charging them with unlawful discriminatory practices in housing, in violation of the Human Rights Law, Executive Law § 296(6). The town filed suit, seeking to enjoin the administrative action and related proceedings. The trial court dismissed the complaint on the merits and for failure to exhaust administrative remedies. On appeal, the court found that the town's as-applied constitutional challenge was subject to the exhaustion doctrine in order to establish the necessary factual record. The trial court properly determined on the merits that DHR had authority to file a complaint on its own initiative under Executive Law § 295(6)(b). The facial constitutional challenge, based on DHR's combined investigative and adjudicative functions, failed on the merits.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by adding provisions that declared that DHR acted within its authority by initiating the administrative complaint on its own, and that various provisions were not unconstitutional. As so modified, the judgment was affirmed. The order of the trial court was also affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law--Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies.--Cause of action seeking declaration that town was not proper respondent and was not subject to provisions of Human Rights Law it was charged with violating in Division of Human Rights (DHR) complaint was properly dismissed for failure to exhaust administrative remedies; claim went to merits of any future finding that town's action violated charged provisions, and did not implicate DHR's broad jurisdiction to determine discriminatory practices in first instance.

Administrative Law--Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies.--Cause of action seeking declaration that Division of Human Rights complaint was void because it constituted reverse discrimination against residents of town was properly dismissed for failure to exhaust administrative remedies; claim did not facially challenge constitutionality of Human Rights Law or any specific provisions thereof, but challenged possible application of charged provisions to town's housing programs, and required resolution of factual issues at administrative level.

Civil Rights--State Division of Human Rights--Constitutionality of Investigative and Adjudicative Powers.--Cause of action seeking declaration that Division of Human Rights (DHR) complaint was void because Executive Law § 295 (6) (a) and (b), which collectively authorize Division of Human Rights to both file and pass upon complaints, was unconstitutional as deprivation of due process rights under federal and state constitutions, was without merit; combination of investigative and adjudicative functions in single administrative agency or officer is not, ipso facto, denial of due process; here, procedures provide that, should matter proceed to administrative hearing, it will be heard by administrative law judge who has not previously been involved in investigation, engaged in conciliation proceeding or caused notice of hearing to be issued (see Executive Law § 297 [4] [a]; 9 NYCRR 465.12 [d] [1]); moreover, party charged with housing discrimination, as was case here, has option, within 20 days of finding of probable cause, to elect to have court action commenced (see Executive Law § 297 [9]); accordingly, combined functions of DHR do not violate due process.

 

416 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Carrie B. v Josephine B.


508906


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 1009; 916 N.Y.S.2d 275; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 595; 2011 NY Slip Op 589



February 3, 2011, Decided 
February 3, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed without opinion by Carrie B. v. Josephine B., 17 NY3d 773, 952 NE2d 1054, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 1662, 929 NYS2d 62 (N.Y., June 23, 2011)


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child--Visitation--No Standing after Termination of Parental Rights

Parent and Child--Visitation--Standing of Sibling.--Petitioner biological mother, who was also adoptive sibling of children, lacked standing to seek visitation as children's sister pursuant to Domestic Relations Law § 71; statute does not confer automatic standing on siblings; brother or sister may apply for visitation only "[w]here circumstances show that conditions exist which equity would see fit to intervene" (Domestic Relations Law § 71); equitable circumstances did not exist that would provide standing where petitioner's parental rights had been terminated based upon her permanent neglect of children.

 

417 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Concourse Rehabilitation & Nursing Ctr., Inc. v Novello


4074, 20201/03


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


80 A.D.3d 507; 915 N.Y.S.2d 252; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 314; 2011 NY Slip Op 271



January 20, 2011, Decided 
January 20, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Concourse Rehabilitation & Nursing Ctr., Inc. v. Novello, 17 N.Y.3d 717, 958 N.E.2d 1202, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3262, 935 N.Y.S.2d 287 (2011)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Concourse Rehab. & Nursing Ctr. v. Novello, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 2578 (U.S., Apr. 2, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff health care facility brought sued defendant department of health (DOH), challenging the DOH's method for applying funds recouped for unpaid gross receipts toward assessment delinquencies for Public Health Law § 2807-d taxes. The Supreme Court, Bronx County (New York), inter alia, granted the facility's cross motion for summary judgment declaring that the penalties were unconstitutional only to a certain extent. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared that the penalties were unconstitutional to the extent of declaring that the facility was entitled to have 50 percent of the subject interest and penalties waived. The appellate court agreed with the trial court's finding that the facility was not entitled to the benefit of the first amnesty granted by the legislature because it did not pay the outstanding assessments by the March 31, 2001 deadline. However, contrary to the trial court's finding, the record did not show that the facility was owed substantially more than the outstanding assessments by the DOH as of March 31, 2001. Thus, the facility's claim that it was owed monies as of March 31, 2001 referred to retroactive rate increases that it anticipated but that had not yet been calculated by the DOH or approved for payment. The fact that the DOH eventually paid the various sums did not support the facility's argument that those sums were owed as of March 31, 2001. The amount owed to the facility was insufficient to pay the more than $ 2 million in unpaid assessments that the facility owed for the period of April 1997 through December 1999, which was the period at issue here.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified, the DOH's motion for summary judgment was granted, and the declaration sought by the cross motion was vacated. Otherwise, the judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Health--Nursing Homes--Gross Receipts Tax--Department of Health's Collection of Deficiency by Withholding of Funds.--In action by plaintiff nursing home challenging Department of Health's (DOH) method of applying funds recouped for unpaid gross receipts toward assessment delinquencies (see Public Health Law § 2807-d), alleging that recouped funds were wrongfully applied to each delinquent month's principal, interest and penalties in chronological order, and that withheld funds should have been treated as credits toward assessments, defendants' motion to dismiss complaint was granted; plaintiff was not entitled to benefit of first amnesty provision enacted by Legislature because it did not pay outstanding assessments by that provision's deadline of March 31, 2001, and plaintiff's claim that it was owed monies as of March 31, 2001 referred to retroactive rate increases that it anticipated but that had not yet been calculated by DOH or approved for payment; as to second and third amnesty provisions, DOH reduced by 50% funds it attributed to interest and penalties that it had not recouped as of April 1, 2004 effective date of second amnesty provision; thus, plaintiff was afforded benefit of second and third amnesty provisions; DOH's determination to apply funds to earliest month's principal, interest, and penalties before proceeding to next month was rational; while as general rule, debtor has right to specify to which debt debtor wishes payment to be applied, there was no evidence that plaintiff so specified before DOH had allocated funds.

 

418 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Sullivan v Paterson


510143


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


80 A.D.3d 1051; 915 N.Y.S.2d 403; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 310; 2011 NY Slip Op 327; 190 L.R.R.M. 2150



January 20, 2011, Decided 
January 20, 2011, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association sued respondent governor, challenging the constitutionality of L 2010, ch. 45, which permitted certain members of respondent retirement system to retire early without penalty. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), among other things, granted cross motions by the governor and the retirement system for summary judgment dismissing the complaint and declaring the statute constitutional. The association appealed.


OVERVIEW: The statute allowed individuals who had attained the age of 55 and completed at least 25 years of service to retire without suffering the reduction in benefits that would otherwise have resulted by operation of Retirement and Social Security Law § 440 et seq. and Retirement and Social Security Law § 600 et seq. To be eligible, an employee had to hold a position represented by collective bargaining units affiliated with the New York state united teachers employee organization (NYSUT). The association argued that L 2010, ch. 45 violated equal protection. The governor and the retirement system proffered, as a rational basis for the distinction between NYSUT members and nonmembers, that the eligibility exclusion provided a legitimate means to target individuals who, if encouraged to retire, would have provided the most cost savings-namely, teachers and teachers assistants that made up most of the individuals represented by NYSUT, as opposed to the supervisory and administrative personnel represented by the association. The appellate court found that a similarly drawn distinction had been held rational. The proffered financial basis used here was also rational.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties-- Standing--Organizational Standing--Action Challenging Constitutionality of Early Retirement Legislation.--Plaintiffs, president of organization that represented bargaining units composed of professional school employees, had organizational standing to challenge legislation permitting certain members of New York State and Local Employees' Retirement System and defendant New York State Teachers' Retirement System to retire early without penalty (L 2010, ch 45); by demonstrating that its member organizations represented individual administrators who would have retired during open period if they would suffer no penalty, plaintiff essentially demonstrated that its members who represent those individuals would have organizational standing to challenge legislation. 

Constitutional Law--Equal Protection of Laws--Challenge to Early Retirement Legislation--Rational Basis Established.--Defendants, New York State and Local Employees' Retirement System and New York State Teachers' Retirement System, proffered rational basis for distinction between members and nonmembers of New York State United Teachers Employee Organization in legislation permitting certain their members to retire early without penalty (L 2010, ch 45); eligibility exclusion provided legitimate means to target group of individuals who, if encouraged to retire, would provide most cost savings, since replacing administrators and supervisors is not as advantageous from cost-savings perspective as replacing older classroom teachers, because supervisors and administrators are usually replaced by someone closer in seniority than classroom teacher's replacement.

 

419 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Hussein v. State of New York


509778


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 132; 914 N.Y.S.2d 464; 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 114; 2011 NY Slip Op 140



January 13, 2011, Decided 
January 13, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, in part Hussein v. State of New York, 17 N.Y.3d 871, 956 N.E.2d 1267, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 3006, 932 N.Y.S.2d 422 (Oct. 13, 2011)
Affirmed by, Certified question answered by Hussein v. State of New York, 19 N.Y.3d 899, 973 N.E.2d 752, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 1788, 950 N.Y.S.2d 342 (June 26, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, parents and students, sought injunctive relief to compel defendant State to establish and maintain an education aid and funding system as to all public schools. The State moved to dismiss the action pursuant to CPLR 3211 on the basis that the claims were not ripe for review and/or were moot. The State appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The parents and students commenced the declaratory judgment action alleging that students were being deprived of the opportunity of a sound basic education, in violation of NY Const. art. XI, § 1, because the school districts where the students attended school were substantially underfunded. The State argued that the claims were not ripe for review because they were based upon data obtained before the enactment of education aid reform legislation. The State argued that since the aid had not yet been fully implemented, the factual record was incomplete and the effects of the legislation could not be measured. As the complaint was replete with detailed data allegedly demonstrating, among other things, inadequate teacher qualifications, building standards and equipment, which illustrate glaring deficiencies in the current quality of the schools and a substantial need for increased aid, the reviewing court held that it was compelled to affirm. If the parents and students proved their allegations that such funding would not remedy an existing unconstitutional deprivation of the opportunity for a sound education, a judicial determination of their claims would have an immediate effect.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Ripeness Doctrine -- Right to Sound Basic Education

1. In a declaratory judgment action in which parents of minor students alleged that their children were being deprived of a sound basic education in violation of NY Constitution, article XI, §1 because the school districts where the students attended school were substantially underfunded, Supreme Court properly denied defendant State of New York's motion to dismiss. The constitutionality of particular levels of education funding are a proper matter for consideration by the courts, and the fact that the court may be required to determine the rights of the parties upon the happening of a future event does not mean that the declaratory judgment will be merely advisory. Here, in addition to allegations of deficiencies in the quality of schools in plaintiffs' districts and a substantial need for increased aid, plaintiffs also claimed that, even with the increases anticipated by the enactment of education aid reform legislation (Foundation Aid), their districts would still be substantially short of the funding levels needed to provide a constitutionally sound basic education. Thus, plaintiffs' claims were ripe for review despite being related to a future event, the implementation of Foundation Aid and the increases in funding encompassed therein, since such event was controlled by defendant and was likely to occur. Moreover, as a judicial determination of their claims would have an immediate and practical effect on the rights and actions of the parties if plaintiffs were successful in proving their allegations that such funding would not remedy the existing unconstitutional deprivation of the opportunity to obtain a sound basic education, and inasmuch as the hardship that may be suffered if consideration of the claims was not permitted to go forward was not insignificant, remote or contingent, the matter was justiciable.

Appeal -- Academic and Moot Questions -- Right to Sound Basic Education

2. In a declaratory judgment action in which parents of minor students alleged that their children were being deprived of a sound basic education in violation of NY Constitution, article XI, §1 because the school districts where the students attended school were substantially underfunded, plaintiffs' claims were not rendered moot by the Legislature's enactment of education aid reform legislation (Foundation Aid) as it was possible that plaintiffs would successfully demonstrate, as alleged in their complaint and based on available data, that even the planned increases in aid were not sufficient to enable the school districts to provide a constitutionally-guaranteed sound basic education. Moreover, the constitutionality of particular levels of education funding is a proper matter for consideration by the court and plaintiffs' rights would be directly affected by a determination of their claims.

People v Knapp


1587 KA 09-01704


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


79 A.D.3d 1805; 913 N.Y.S.2d 470; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9819; 2010 NY Slip Op 9807



December 30, 2010, Decided 
December 30, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at People v. Gano, 81 A.D.3d 1378, 916 N.Y.S.2d 419, 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 990 (N.Y. App. Div. 4th Dep't, 2011)

Dead Bodies--Disposition of Remains--Body Stealing.--In prosecution for body stealing and other related crimes, court properly denied request to charge jury on "good faith" defense set forth in Public Health Law § 4306 (3); evidence established that defendant and employees under her supervision removed tissue and/or bone from decedents without consent from donors or their next of kin, that defendant instructed employees to sign blank consent forms as witnesses for use in future recoveries, and that those forms were subsequently filled out with false information.

 

421 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Chwick v Mulvey


2009-01468


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 161; 915 N.Y.S.2d 578; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9817; 2010 NY Slip Op 9911



December 28, 2010, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensees brought a hybrid CPLR art. 78 and declaratory judgment proceeding against respondent county, and others, challenging Nassau County, N.Y., Ordinance No. 5-2008, as amended by Nassau County, N.Y., Ordinance 9-2008 (Ordinance), and seeking to enjoin enforcement of the Ordinance. The Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), denied the petition and dismissed the proceeding. The licensees appealed.


OVERVIEW: The principal issue on appeal was whether the Ordinance, which banned the possession of "deceptively colored" firearms, was preempted by Penal Law § 400.00. The appellate court found that, contrary to the county's contention, far from affecting mere possession, the Ordinance interfered with the licensing provisions set forth in Penal Law § 265.00 et seq. and Penal Law § 400.00 et seq. by making it illegal for an individual to possess a deceptively colored handgun in Nassau County even though such individual held a valid firearms license under state law. Further, the appellate court rejected the county's argument that § 400.00 referred only to firearm licensing procedures and the qualifications needed for an individual to be granted a license, and did not refer to types of weapons. Indeed, § 400.00(2) specifically precluded from licensing assault weapons and disguised guns. Conflict preemption did not apply. However, in light of the comprehensive and detailed regulatory language and scheme of § 400.00, which demonstrated the legislature's intent to preempt the field of firearm regulation, § 400.00 preempted the Ordinance.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified by granting the petition insofar as asserted by one of the licensees and declaring that Nassau County, N.Y., Ordinance No. 5-2008, as amended by Nassau County, N.Y., Ordinance 9-2008 was preempted by Penal Law § 400.00; as so modified, the judgment was affirmed insofar as appealed from.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Challenge to Local Firearms Ordinance

1. In a proceeding challenging a county ordinance (County of Nassau, Miscellaneous Laws, tit 69 [Local Law No. 5-2008, as amended by Local Law No. 9-2008]) prohibiting the possession of "deceptively colored" firearms, a petitioner lacked standing because his Glock model 20 pistol fell outside the definition of "deceptively colored" in the amended ordinance and, therefore, was exempt from the amended ordinance.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Gun Control

2. A county ordinance (County of Nassau, Miscellaneous Laws, tit 69 [Local Law No. 5-2008, as amended by Local Law No. 9-2008]) which banned the possession of "deceptively colored" firearms interfered with the licensing provisions set forth in Penal Law § 265.00 et seq. and § 400.00 et seq. The ordinance did not regulate mere possession of certain handguns; it made illegal the possession of such a handgun even though an individual holds a valid firearms license under state law.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Gun Control -- Conflict Preemption

3. A county ordinance (County of Nassau, Miscellaneous Laws, tit 69 [Local Law No. 5-2008, as amended by Local Law No. 9-2008]) which criminalized the possession of a "deceptively colored" firearm, regardless of whether one possesses a valid state firearms license, was not subject to conflict preemption by state law because Penal Law § 265.20 (a) (3) and § 400.00 do not prohibit the possession of firearms that are deceptively colored as defined by the amended ordinance. Although, under current state law, possession of "deceptively colored" firearms is implicitly allowed, there was no conflict preemption between the county ordinance and state law since there was no "head-on collision" between the two.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Gun Control -- Express Field Preemption

4. The doctrine of express field preemption, whereby an express statement in a state statute explicitly avers that it preempts all local laws on the same subject matter, did not invalidate a county ordinance (County of Nassau, Miscellaneous Laws, tit 69 [Local Law No. 5-2008, as amended by Local Law No. 9-2008]) which criminalized the possession of a "deceptively colored" firearm, regardless of whether one possessed a valid state firearms license, since nowhere in the state statutory scheme was there a provision explicitly preempting local laws on the subject matter of firearm possession or licensing.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Gun Control -- Policy Field Preemption

5. The doctrine of policy field preemption, whereby a declaration of state policy evinces the intent of the Legislature to preempt local laws on the same subject matter, did not invalidate a county ordinance (County of Nassau, Miscellaneous Laws, tit 69 [Local Law No. 5-2008, as amended by Local Law No. 9-2008]) which criminalized the possession of a "deceptively colored" firearm, regardless of whether one possessed a valid state firearms license, since no such intention may be found in the pertinent state statutes or legislative histories.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Gun Control -- Implicit Field Preemption

6. A county ordinance (County of Nassau, Miscellaneous Laws, tit 69 [Local Law No. 5-2008, as amended by Local Law No. 9-2008]) which prohibited the possession of "deceptively colored" firearms was preempted by Penal Law § 400.00, which contains detailed and comprehensive provisions for issuing firearms licenses in the state. The state statute evinced an intent to set forth a uniform system of licensing and provided that it was the "exclusive statutory mechanism" for such licensing. As such, no locality may supplant that licensing requirement, since doing so would undermine the uniformity of the system, whereby a license issued in any county is valid in any other county (save New York City). The county ordinance interrupted that uniformity by imposing additional requirements for lawful firearms possession, and if each county enacted additional restrictions, the uniformity of the scheme would be destroyed. The state statute, by its detailed nature, left no room for local ordinances to operate. Thus, when the Legislature demonstrated its intent to preempt the field, all local ordinances, such as the one in question, were preempted, regardless of whether they actually conflicted with the state law.

 

422 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Columbus 95th St., LLC v New York State Div. of Hous. & Community Renewal


113148/07, 3627


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 269; 916 N.Y.S.2d 2; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9658; 2010 NY Slip Op 9569



December 28, 2010, Decided 
December 28, 2010, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord sued respondent New York State Division of Housing and Community Renewal (DHCR), seeking a declaration that Rent Stabilization Code (9 NYCRR) § 2522.3(f)(4), a regulation clarifying N.Y. City, N.Y., Rent Stab. Law 26-513(a), was invalid, or, in the alternative, an order prohibiting its retroactive application. The Supreme Court, New York County, New York, denied the requested relief; the landlord appealed.


OVERVIEW: On leaving the Mitchell-Lama program, the landlord's rents were regulated by N.Y. City, N.Y., Rent Stab. Law § 26-512(b)(3). After it filed applications for rent increases, the Division enacted § 2522.3(f)(4), which provided that any change in economic circumstances due to termination of a prior regulation of rent would not justify a rent adjustment without a showing of "hardship" under Rent Stabilization Code (9 NYCRR) § 2522.4(b) or (c). The landlord argued that § 2522.3(f)(4) was invalid because it contravened § 26-513(a), which provided former Mitchell-Lama buildings with an absolute right to a rent increase. The appellate court disagreed. The landlord did not meet its burden to establish that § 2522.3(f)(4) was inconsistent with §§ 26-512(b)(3) and 26-513(a), as there was no evidence the legislature meant to permit former Mitchell-Lama owners to charge market rents on exiting the program. DHCR, as a government agency, was not estopped from taking the position it did on appeal. It did not act in bad faith by delaying its decision on the landlord's application so it could enact § 2522.3(f)(4), as the regulation did not change the law to the landlord's detriment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Building Emerging from Mitchell-Lama Program -- Initial Legal Regulated Rent

1. Rent Stabilization Code (9 NYCRR) § 2522.3 (f) (4), which provides that the previous regulation of an apartment building by virtue of any state or federal program shall not, in and of itself, constitute a "unique and peculiar circumstance" entitling it to an adjustment of its units' initial legal regulated rent, is not inconsistent with either Rent Stabilization Law (Administrative Code of City of NY) § 26-512 (b) (3), which provides that for housing accommodations not subject to either the Emergency Tenant Protection Act of 1974 (ETPA) or the Vacancy Decontrol Law of 1971 (VDL) the initial legal regulated rent shall be the rent reserved in the last effective lease or other rental agreement, or section 26-513 (a), which provides that the initial legal regulated rent may be adjusted in the presence of "unique or peculiar circumstances." According to the plain language of section 26-512 (b) (3), the initial rent for all apartments that become regulated for any reason other than because they were previously deregulated by the VDL or were previously rent controlled but then vacated is not the market rent but the rent reserved in the previous lease. Accordingly, apartments in a building emerging from the Mitchell-Lama subsidized housing program, i.e., one not previously covered by either the ETPA or the VDL, may not have their initial legal regulated rent set at the market rate: to do so would contravene the stated goal of the Rent Stabilization Law as it would cause a drastic increase in the rents of existing tenants who had no control over their landlord's decision to opt out of Mitchell-Lama.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Building Emerging from Mitchell-Lama Program -- Initial Legal Regulated Rent

2. Apartments in a building emerging from the Mitchell-Lama subsidized housing program were not entitled to have their initial legal regulated rent set at the market rate on the ground that the regulated rents set under that program constituted "unique or peculiar circumstances" pursuant to Rent Stabilization Law (Administrative Code of City of NY) § 26-513 (a). Although there may be relatively few such buildings when compared to all of the other types of multiple dwellings found throughout New York City, that fact does not permit the conclusion that the Legislature meant to afford a step-up to market rents based on the simple fact that a building had left the Mitchell-Lama program. Rather, the phrase "unique or peculiar" applies to situations which would not have been reasonably foreseeable to a landlord when forming its assumptions as to the initial rent it could charge under rent stabilization. It cannot be said that emerging from the Mitchell-Lama program was a "unique" or "peculiar" circumstance when a landlord's reasonable expectation, based on the clear language of Rent Stabilization Law § 26-512 (b) (3), should have been that upon its emergence the initial regulated rent would be "the rent reserved in the last effective lease or other rental agreement."

Estoppel -- Estoppel against Government -- New York City Rent Regulation

3. In a proceeding to invalidate a regulation of the Division of Housing and Community Renewal (DHCR) providing that previous regulation of apartments in a multiple dwelling, in and of itself, shall not constitute "a unique and peculiar circumstance" thereby entitling the landlord to an adjustment of the initial legal regulated rent, DHCR was not estopped from taking the position that the emergence of a building from the Mitchell-Lama subsidized housing program did not constitute a unique or peculiar circumstance. Estoppel cannot serve to bar a governmental agency from exercising its governmental functions, and, in any event, a 1994 letter from a DHCR official which discussed the possibility that a former Mitchell-Lama building could argue that the very fact of past regulation constituted a "unique or peculiar circumstance," was inconclusive. That letter expressly recognized the hypothetical and "amorphous" nature of the discussion and stated that it "should not be construed as a[n] official statement of DHCR's position in any particular case." In the absence of proof of any official policy by DHCR, the agency should not be bound to what can at best be expressed as musings in response to requests for its position on the setting of initial rents.

Administrative Law -- Validity of Regulation -- Bad Faith of Agency

4. In determining the request of a landlord for initial legal regulated rents for apartments in a building that was emerging from the Mitchell-Lama subsidized housing program, the Division of Housing and Community Renewal (DHCR) did not act in bad faith by delaying consideration of the request until after DHCR adopted a regulation which provided that previous regulation of apartments in a multiple dwelling, in and of itself, did not constitute "a unique and peculiar circumstance" that would entitle the landlord to an adjustment of the initial legal regulated rent. The regulation did not change existing law; it merely clarified it. Accordingly, retroactive application of the regulation was not unexpected and indefensible by reference to the law as it then existed.

 

423 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Coleman v Daines


1923, 108435/08


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


79 A.D.3d 554; 913 N.Y.S.2d 83; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9449; 2010 NY Slip Op 9349



December 16, 2010, Decided 
December 16, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Coleman v. Daines, 17 N.Y.3d 826, 954 N.E.2d 79, 2011 N.Y. LEXIS 2054, 929 N.Y.S.2d 789 (2011)
Motion granted by Coleman v. Daines, 19 N.Y.3d 980, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 1952 (2012)
Affirmed by, Certified question answered by Coleman v. Daines, 2012 N.Y. LEXIS 3263 (N.Y., Oct. 30, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Medicaid recipient sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied her petition against respondent state health officials and dismissed a hybrid proceeding under CPLR art. 78 and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, seeking class certification, declaratory and injunctive relief, and damages and fees. The matter arose from the recipient's request for personal care attendant (PCA) services.


OVERVIEW: The recipient, who suffered from many maladies, submitted an application for Medicaid home care services in the form of sleep-in PCAs. She was not given notice of the availability of temporary Medicaid in the form of PCA services. The recipient moved in temporarily with her sister, who was overburdened, and the recipient submitted a request under Social Services Law § 133 and N.Y. Const. art. 17, § 1 for temporary Medicaid. She received notification that she was eligible for Medicaid retroactively, but there was no indication of the number of hours of PCA services that the recipient was entitled to. Suit was filed. The trial court dismissed the matter on the grounds of mootness and failure to exhaust administrative remedies (EAR). On appeal, the court found that dismissal on both grounds was error. All of the elements of the "likely to recur" exception to the mootness doctrine were present. As the dispute turned on the construction of the relevant constitutional, statutory, and regulatory framework, the matter was within the exceptions to the EAR doctrine. Resort to a fair hearing would have been futile, as established agency policy under § 133 would have been followed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, reinstated the petition, and remitted the matter for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Appeal--Academic and Moot Questions--Failure of City and State Agencies to Fulfill Constitutional, Statutory and Regulatory Duties with Respect to Medicaid Applicants.--In proceeding seeking class certification, alleging that respondent Commissioner of Department of Health and respondent Commissioner of New York City Human Resources Administration violated various constitutional, statutory and regulatory duties with respect to Medicaid applicants applying for personal care attendant services, all of elements of "likely to recur" exception to mootness doctrine were present and proceeding should not have been dismissed on that basis; first, petition challenged respondents' policy not to notify Medicaid applicants of availability of temporary Medicaid in form of personal care attendants, not to provide or pay for temporary Medicaid benefits in form of personal care attendants, and not to render decision on requests for temporary medical assistance in form of personal care attendants within 48 hours of such request and to provide temporary Medicaid assistance next day; since that policy applied to other similarly situated Medicaid applicants and recipients, it was "likely to recur"; second, issues were substantial and of public importance; respondents' policy regarding notice and availability of temporary services could negatively impact on health and welfare of substantial number of Medicaid applicants in need of personal care attendants; third, issues were capable of evading review since applicants could receive determination on their ultimate eligibility for Medicaid in form of personal care attendants under Social Service Law § 133 before issue of temporary eligibility came before court.

Administrative Law--Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies--Exceptions to Doctrine.--Proceeding in which petitioner argued that she was entitled, pursuant to rights granted by Federal and State Constitutions and Social Services Law § 133, to notice of right to temporary Medicaid benefits and to benefits themselves should not have been dismissed on ground of failure to exhaust administrative remedies; because dispute turned on construction of relevant constitutional, statutory and regulatory framework, rather than substantive factual dispute between parties relating to extent of personal care that petitioner required or was entitled to, matter fell within exceptions to exhaustion of administrative remedies doctrine; resort to fair hearing would also have been futile inasmuch as hearing officer would have been required to follow established agency policy that petitioner sought to challenge, which did not provide for notice of right to temporary Medicaid benefits in form of personal care attendant services or for right to those benefits themselves under Social Services Law § 133, as it existed as of date petition was filed.
