Matter of Hamil Stratten Props., LLC v New York State Dept. of Envtl. Conservation


2009-10372


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


79 A.D.3d 747; 913 N.Y.S.2d 282; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9226; 2010 NY Slip Op 9134



December 7, 2010, Decided


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation--Hazardous Waste--Termination of Brownfield Site Cleanup Agreement by Department of Environmental Conservation.--Respondent Department of Environmental Conservation's (DEC) termination of brownfield site cleanup agreement entered into between petitioners and DEC, based on determination that petitioners had failed to substantially comply with agreement, was not arbitrary, capricious, or abuse of discretion; DEC is authorized to terminate such agreement "at any time during its implementation if the applicant fails to comply substantially with such agreement's terms and conditions" (ECL 27-1409 [12]), provision mirrored in parties' agreement; petitioners' contention that they did not receive proper notice of termination of agreement and refusal to reinstate agreement was without merit, since notices were sent to petitioners' attorney, who acted as their agent in matter; requirements with regard to notice of termination of brownfield site cleanup agreements contained in 6 NYCRR 375-3.5 (c) (1) did not apply since that regulation applies only to agreements entered into after effective date of regulations; additionally, DEC reasonably determined that termination and refusal were not issues subject to formal dispute resolution under agreement, and that, in any event, petitioners failed to timely request formal dispute resolution pursuant to agreement.

Constitutional Law--Due Process of Law--Termination of Brownfield Site Cleanup Agreement by Department of Environmental Conservation.--Petitioners constitutional rights were not violated when respondent Department of Environmental Conservation terminated brownfield site cleanup agreement without hearing; due process is satisfied if opportunity for hearing is given, regardless of whether property owner takes advantage of that opportunity; here, petitioners had opportunity for pre-deprivation hearing, in form of dispute resolution, but petitioners did not timely pursue that option; moreover, CPLR article 78 proceeding provided adequate post-deprivation process even in absence of administrative hearing.

Constitutional Law--Equal Protection of Laws

 

426 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Hudson Val. Mar., Inc. v Town of Cortlandt


2009-10058


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


79 A.D.3d 700; 912 N.Y.S.2d 623; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9212; 2010 NY Slip Op 9104



December 7, 2010, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner of a marina sued defendants town and employees, seeking damages from allegedly improper proceedings brought against the owner. The Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), entered summary judgment dismissing the causes of action to recover damages for malicious prosecution, abuse of process, negligent training and supervision, and violations of 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1983, 1985(3). The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner alleged that the town's issuance of a stop work order, the bringing of an action seeking a permanent injunction, and criminal charges caused the owner, among other things, a loss of income. However, the appellate court found that the town and the employees established their prima facie entitlement to judgment as to the abuse of process claim by showing that the stop work order, appearance tickets, and the civil action were brought to accomplish the lawful purpose of requiring the owner to comply with the town code and not to obtain a collateral objective. The owner failed to raise a triable fact issue. Further, the owner's claims relying on alleged negligence did not support an abuse of process claim since it was an intentional tort. The denial of the town's motion for a preliminary injunction did not show that the civil action was ended in its favor, since that denial was not an adjudication on the merits. Also, that action was discontinued without prejudice by agreement of the parties. The town also presented evidence sufficient to establish its prima facie burden of entitlement to judgment as a matter of law dismissing the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed insofar as appealed from.


HEADNOTES

Process--Abuse of Process--What Constitutes.--Defendants, town and its code enforcement officer and fire inspector, were entitled to summary judgment dismissing causes of action alleging abuse of process; defendants demonstrated that they issued stop work order and appearance tickets, and commenced civil action seeking injunction, to accomplish lawful purpose of requiring plaintiff to comply with town code and not to obtain collateral objective; in opposition, plaintiff failed to raise triable issue, and its reliance on defendants' alleged negligent conduct could not support claim for abuse of process since it is intentional tort.

Torts--Malicious Prosecution--Termination of Proceeding in Favor of Accused--What Constitutes.--In action alleging malicious prosecution based upon defendant town's commencement of underlying civil action against plaintiff seeking permanent injunction barring plaintiff from continuing to engage in certain construction work until plaintiff obtained requisite approvals and permits from town, denial of town's motion for preliminary injunction in underlying civil action did not demonstrate that civil action was terminated in plaintiff's favor, since that denial was not adjudication on merits; additionally, underlying civil action was discontinued without prejudice by agreement entered into by parties; thus, there was no termination of action favorable to plaintiff to support action for malicious prosecution.

Civil Rights--Federal Civil Rights Claim.--Defendants, town and its code enforcement officer and fire inspector, were entitled to dismissal of causes of action alleging violations of 42 USC §§1983 and 1985 (3); town demonstrated that criminal charges brought against plaintiff, which plaintiff alleged violated its rights under 42 USC §1983, did not result from policy, regulation, or custom of town; as for 42 USC §1983 claims asserted against code enforcement officer and fire inspector, they were entitled to qualified immunity since it was objectively reasonable for them to believe that their acts did not violate clearly established constitutional or statutory rights regardless of whether they made mistakes of law or fact in applying town code; as for 42 USC §1985 (3) claim, plaintiff failed to allege that defendants harbored class-based invidious discriminatory animus against particular group of individuals and that that prejudice motivated alleged wrongful conduct against plaintiff.

428 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Shields v Carbone


508485


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


78 A.D.3d 1440; 913 N.Y.S.2d 354; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8761; 2010 NY Slip Op 8661



November 24, 2010, Decided 
November 24, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Shields v. Carbone, 99 A.D.3d 1055, 952 N.Y.S.2d 649, 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6973 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff treasurer brought an interpleader suit against defendants client, girlfriend, and lawyers, seeking a determination as to who should receive bail funds. The client and the girlfriend counterclaimed. The Supreme Court, Delaware County (New York), inter alia, dismissed the counterclaims and directed the treasurer to release to the lawyers the bail money posted by the client. The client and the girlfriend appealed.


OVERVIEW: The client and the girlfriend alleged that treasurer violated their Fourth Amendment rights by unreasonably withholding their money without authority. However, the appellate court found that the Fourth Amendment did not address unreasonably withholding property once a citizen voluntarily gave that property to the government, such as happened here, and those counterclaims were properly dismissed. The questions regarding the lawyers' intent required denial of summary judgment on the Judiciary Law § 487 cause of action. As there was evidence that the lawyers' sent their letters in an effort to obtain payment of legal fees, they were entitled to summary judgment dismissing the prima facie tort cross claim because malevolence was not the lawyers' sole motive. However, the trial court erred in ordering the treasurer to release bail proceeds directly to the lawyers. Under General Municipal Law § 99-m(1), bail was to be refunded to the person who originally posted it. The lawyers did not provide proof of a perfected transaction between the client and the lawyers through which the client intended to vest in the lawyers a present right to his bail proceeds.


OUTCOME: The order was modified by, inter alia, reversing the orders (1) denying the lawyers' cross motion for dismissal of the cross claims for prima facie tort and legal malpractice, (2) directing the treasurer to release bail proceeds to the lawyers, and (3) granting the lawyers' cross motion for summary judgment on its breach of contract claim against the client. The treasurer was directed to release the bail proceeds to the client.


HEADNOTES

Bail--Exoneration of Bail--Unreasonable Withholding of Bail Does Not Constitute Unconstitutional Search and Seizure.--Counterclaims of defendants alleging that plaintiff county treasurer violated their US Constitution 4th Amendment rights by unreasonably withholding bail money they posted were dismissed; Fourth Amendment prohibits unreasonable searches and seizures of property but does not address unreasonably withholding property once citizen voluntarily gives that property to government, as plaintiffs did when they posted bail.

Bail--Exoneration of Bail--Assignment of Cash Bail to Satisfy Attorney's Fee.--Court erred in ordering plaintiff county treasurer to release bail proceeds directly to attorneys who represented defendant in underlying criminal matter; by statute, when bail is exonerated, it is refunded to person who originally deposited such money (General Municipal Law §99-m [1]); while person who posts bail can assign right to receive bail proceeds, attorneys did not provide proof of perfected transaction with defendant through which he intended to vest in them present right to bail proceeds; retainer agreement provided that defendant agreed to sign cash bail over to attorneys, but there was no written assignment and defendant did not endorse bail receipt over to attorneys; without proof of assignment, court should have directed plaintiff to release bail proceeds to defendant, original depositor or those funds.

Attorney and Client--Compensation--Ambiguous Fee Agreement.--Defendant attorneys were not entitled to summary judgment of breach of contract cause of action against defendant they represented in underlying criminal matter; pursuant to fee agreement, fee was to be paid at time of disposition of case if firm obtained "disposition or resolution"; attorneys represented defendant through entry of guilty plea, but defendant discharged them after plea and retained new counsel prior to sentencing; phrase "disposition or resolution" was ambiguous, as it could refer to guilty plea, which constitutes conviction, or sentencing, which results in judgment of conviction; ambiguity created question of fact concerning whether attorneys obtained "disposition or resolution" of defendant's criminal case so as to entitle them to minimum fee, or whether defendant discharged attorneys prior to disposition or resolution such that they could only recover fee on hourly basis.

New York State Socy. of Professional Engrs., Inc. v City of New York


3615, 101179/09


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


78 A.D.3d 477; 911 N.Y.S.2d 303; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8476; 2010 NY Slip Op 8352



November 16, 2010, Decided 
November 16, 2010, Entered


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law--Validity of Statute--Facial Unconstitutionality--Total Invalidity Required.--Plaintiffs failed to prove that Local Law 39, amending New York City Charter section 641 to require either Commissioner of Buildings or First Deputy Commissioner to be licensed professional engineer or registered architect, was unconstitutional on its face on grounds that it was inconsistent with and preempted by Title VIII of Education Law, which sets forth licensing requirements for professional engineers and registered architects; plaintiffs claimed that by no longer requiring Commissioner to be licensed professional engineer or registered architect, City permitted official to engage in practice of engineering without license; however, for statute to be declared unconstitutional on its face, plaintiff must demonstrate that law is invalid in toto, i.e., that under no circumstances would this law be valid; express power, by unlicensed Commissioner, to delegate any duties that involve practice of engineering or architecture to properly licensed First Deputy Commissioner, validates Local Law 39; in addition, local enactment did not permit Commissioner of Buildings or anyone else to regulate practice of engineering within New York City or to practice engineering or architecture without license, in conflict with state law.

 

430 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Prometheus Realty Corp. v City of New York


111132/08, 3057


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


80 A.D.3d 206; 911 N.Y.S.2d 299; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8495; 2010 NY Slip Op 8373



November 16, 2010, Decided 
November 16, 2010, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners, and others, brought an action seeking a judgment declaring that New York City, New York, Tenant Protection Act, Local Law No. 7 of 2008 (Local Law 7), was unconstitutional and enjoining defendant city from enforcing the law. All parties moved for summary judgment. The Supreme Court, New York County (New York), denied the owners' motion and granted the city's motion, dismissing the complaint. The owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owners argued that Local Law 7 improperly granted a type of authority to the Housing Part of New York City Civil Court that may only have been granted by the state legislature. The appellate court disagreed. Local Law 7 did not exceed the authority previously accorded to the Housing Part, but fell within the previously-defined jurisdiction of the Housing Part entitling it to enforce laws to establish and maintain housing standards. Having concluded that Local Law 7 did not impermissibly expand the jurisdiction of the Housing Part to hear claims of harassment against landlords, the appellate court also rejected the parallel argument that it impermissibly expanded the Housing Part's equitable jurisdiction. Local Law 7 was rationally related to a legitimate State objective, namely, maintaining rent-regulated housing in New York City. Preventing landlords from forcing tenants out by such means as refusing to make repairs, so they could have deregulated their buildings, was also rationally related to this goal. Moreover, Local Law 7 did not lack procedural safeguards for protecting landlords' due process rights.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Constitutionality of Anti-Harassment Statute -- Expansion of Jurisdiction of Housing Part of New York City Civil Court 

1. Local Law No. 7 (2008) of the City of New York, which provides legal remedies for tenants experiencing harassment by landlords attempting to force them out, does not violate the State Constitution or usurp the authority of the State Legislature by modifying the jurisdiction of the Housing Part of the New York City Civil Court. The Housing Part has jurisdiction over the maintenance of "housing standards" (CCA 110), and although the CCA does not include a definition of that term, the creation of remedies for harassment of tenants by landlords is a matter that falls squarely within the concept of "housing standards" as the term has been understood and applied since the Act was enacted. Since such issues as whether a tenant's conduct was objectionable or constituted a nuisance have already been established to be within the jurisdiction of the Housing Part, and therefore necessarily an issue of "housing standards," the equivalent issue of whether a particular landlord's conduct constitutes harassment must similarly be recognized as an issue of "housing standards" within the previously established jurisdiction of the Housing Part. "Housing standards" contemplates more than simply standards for the physical plant or condition of buildings.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Constitutionality of Anti-Harassment Statute -- Expansion of Equitable Jurisdiction of Housing Part of New York City Civil Court

2. Inasmuch as Local Law No. 7 (2008) of the City of New York, which provides legal remedies for tenants experiencing harassment by landlords attempting to force them out, does not violate the State Constitution or usurp the authority of the State Legislature by modifying the jurisdiction of the Housing Part of the New York City Civil Court, it also does not impermissibly expand the Housing Part's equitable jurisdiction. CCA 110 (a) (4) authorizes the Housing Part to issue equitable relief such as restraining orders and injunctions in order to enforce "housing standards," broadly construed. That authority was no more limited to proceedings involving the physical condition of buildings than the remainder of the authority granted by section 110 (a) to hear actions in order to enforce housing standards.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Constitutionality of Anti-Harassment Statute -- Relationship with Housing Maintenance Code

3. The fact that Local Law No. 7 (2008) of the City of New York, which provides legal remedies for tenants experiencing harassment by landlords attempting to force them out, was incorporated into the Housing Maintenance Code did not render it improper or unconstitutional. The Code has not been strictly limited to governing matters of building structure, but was intended to protect a tenant's actual occupancy.

Municipal Corporations -- Home Rule Powers -- New York City Anti-Harassment Statute 

4. In enacting Local Law No. 7 (2008) of the City of New York, which provides legal remedies for tenants experiencing harassment by landlords attempting to force them out, the City Council did not exceed its authority "to adopt and amend local laws not inconsistent with the provisions of the constitution or not inconsistent with any general law" (Municipal Home Rule Law § 10 [1] [i]), since there was no inconsistency.

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation -- Constitutionality of Anti-Harassment Statute -- Due Process

5. Local Law No. 7 (2008) of the City of New York, which provides legal remedies for tenants experiencing harassment by landlords attempting to force them out, does not violate plaintiff landlords' substantive and procedural due process rights. It is rationally related to a legitimate State objective, namely, maintaining rent-regulated housing in New York City. Preventing landlords from forcing tenants out by such means as refusing to make repairs, so they can deregulate their buildings, is also rationally related to that goal. Moreover, Local Law No. 7 does not lack procedural safeguards for protecting landlords' due process rights.

 

431 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Morales


2210/04, 1142


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 1; 911 N.Y.S.2d 21; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8105; 2010 NY Slip Op 8012



November 9, 2010, Decided 
November 9, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Recalled by, Vacated by, Remitted by People v. Morales, 86 AD3d 147, 924 NYS2d 62, 2011 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4459 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, May 31, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prosecution brought in the Supreme Court, Bronx County, New York, under the terrorism statute, Penal Law § 490.25, defendant was convicted by a jury of first-degree manslaughter as a crime of terrorism, attempted second-degree murder as a crime of terrorism, second degree criminal possession of a weapon as a crime of terrorism, and second-degree conspiracy. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: In the course of a fight among members of rival gangs, defendant shot a child and a young man; the child died and the man became paralyzed. The appellate court held the evidence was insufficient to prove defendant committed his crimes with the intent to intimidate or coerce a "civilian population" generally, as opposed to members of rival gangs. The evidence showed that the shootings were intended to assert the gang's dominance over rival gangs and to pursue a vendetta against a specific gang. Members of other Mexican-American gangs in the area did not qualify as a "civilian population" under § 490.25(1), and there was no evidence defendant intended to intimidate the broader community. His own statements sufficiently corroborated the testimony of his accomplice to satisfy CPL 60.22(1). Defense counsel, who obtained an acquittal on a second-degree murder charge, was not ineffective. Defendant's motion to suppress his statements to police at his apartment was properly denied, as police had consent to enter, and as he was not in custody, he had not been entitled to Miranda warnings. The conditions of his detention were not so unreasonable as to render his statements involuntary.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reduced the convictions for crimes of terrorism to the corresponding specified crimes as lesser included offenses and remitted the case to the trial court for resentencing on those counts.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Terrorism - Intimidation of "Civilian Population"

1. In a prosecution arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, the evidence was not legally sufficient to establish that defendant acted with the requisite intent to render his offenses crimes of terrorism. Even assuming that residents of a certain ethnicity in a particular area constituted "a civilian population" under Penal Law § 490.25 (1), the evidence was insufficient to support a finding that defendant committed his crimes with the intent to intimidate or coerce that "civilian population" generally, as opposed to the much more limited category of members of rival gangs. The only purposes of defendant's actions were those of asserting his gang's dominance over rival gangs in general and pursuing a vendetta against a particular rival gang. While defendant's gang sometimes preyed on area residents who were not gang members, there was nothing to suggest that defendant's actions were motivated by the desire to intimidate a particular ethnic community in a certain geographic area. The crime of terrorism is not established unless the alleged terroristic intent is connected to the particular specified offense underlying the charge, and the "civilian population" to be intimidated must be some group other than the direct victims of the crime.

Crimes -- Terrorism -- Intimidation of "Civilian Population"

2. In a prosecution arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, the evidence was not legally sufficient to establish that defendant acted with the requisite intent to render his offenses crimes of terrorism. Members of other rival gangs in the area in which defendant's gang operated did not qualify as "a civilian population" within the meaning of Penal Law § 490.25 (1). The context of the Anti-Terrorism Act weighs against stretching the meaning of its language to cover such a narrowly defined subcategory of individuals. Rather, the Legislature intended to address extraordinary criminal acts perpetrated for the purpose of intimidating a broad range of people, not a narrowly defined group of particular individuals whom the criminal actor happens to regard as adversaries. A minimum size of "a civilian population" need not be defined where the legislative findings implied an intent to create a pervasively terrorizing effect on people living in a given area, directed either to all of those residents or those residents who are members of some broadly defined class, such as a gender, race, nationality, ethnicity or religion.

Crimes -- Corroboration of Accomplice Testimony -- Evidence Tending to Connect Defendant with Crime

3. Defendant was properly convicted of first-degree manslaughter, second-degree attempted murder, criminal possession of a weapon and conspiracy arising from his shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl based on the testimony an accomplice who was a leader of defendant's gang, because that testimony found support in "corroborative evidence tending to connect the defendant with the commission of [the] offense[s]" (CPL 60.22 [1]). The chief corroborating evidence was defendant's own statements he gave to the police in which he admitted his membership in the gang, placed himself at the scene of the crime and admitted having held a gun there.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Judge's Remarks at Voir Dire

4. In a prosecution for terrorism arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, defendant was not deprived of a fair trial by the manner in which the court referred to the terrorist attack of September 11, 2001 (9/11). While it would have been preferable for the court to forgo the reference to jury service as a way to "speak back" to the 9/11 terrorists, it was highly unlikely that the jurors misinterpreted that hortatory rhetoric as an invitation to vindicate their own rage at the 9/11 terrorists by their treatment of defendant's case, thereby undermining the impartiality of the proceedings. Nothing in the court's remarks likened defendant to the 9/11 terrorists; on the contrary, the court specifically explained that defendant was not being charged with politically motivated terrorism. Significantly, the trial took place a full six years after 9/11, and defendant did not claim that anything the jurors learned of his background might have caused them to connect him to the 9/11 terrorists. Further, given the vast scale of the 9/11 catastrophe, the distinction between those attacks and the crimes charged was unmistakable.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review - Strategic Waiver

5. Defendant was not entitled to a new trial in the interest of justice based on the introduction of numerous hearsay statements at his trial on charges stemming from his shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl. Defendant not only failed to raise any objections, but also affirmatively waived them and sought to use the evidence in question for his own strategic ends. Accordingly, defendant, through counsel, intelligently and knowingly waived his Confrontation Clause claims.

Crimes -- Right to Counsel -- Effective Representation

6. In a prosecution for terrorism arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, the performance of defendant's lead trial counsel was not so deficient as to deny him effective assistance of counsel. In the absence of a complete record developed by a motion to vacate the judgment pursuant to CPL 440.10, it may be presumed that counsel exercised professional judgment and strategic discretion in determining how to conduct the defense. The existing record reflected that counsel followed a coherent strategy that sought to show that defendant committed no crime beyond weapon possession, a charge that he was unlikely to defeat given the denial of his suppression motion. Further, counsel competently attacked the credibility of the People's main witness, and brought out the inconsistencies between his testimony and that of other witnesses. Ultimately, counsel obtained an acquittal on the second-degree murder charge, the most serious count of the indictment.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review - Inconsistent Verdict

7. In a prosecution for terrorism arising out of defendant's shooting of a supposed rival gang member and a 10-year-old girl, defendant's claim that the verdict was irreconcilably inconsistent insofar as he was convicted of attempted second-degree murder with respect to the supposed rival gang member who was paralyzed, while he was acquitted of the second-degree murder charge with respect to the girl, who was killed, was unpreserved as defendant failed to raise it before the jury was discharged. The failure to object to the verdict as inconsistent at the appropriate time may well have been a conscious tactical choice by defense counsel, since resubmitting the case to the jury to cure the inconsistency could have resulted in the acquittal on the murder charge being changed to a conviction. Accordingly, the claim was not reviewed in the interest of justice.

Crimes -- Confession -- Noncustodial Statements

8. In a prosecution arising out of defendant's shooting of two individuals, the police did not act improperly in entering defendant's apartment when they first approached him for questioning, since they entered the apartment with the implicit consent of an elderly man (apparently, defendant's stepfather) who met them at the door. The police did not have to give defendant Miranda warnings when they began to interview him after he voluntarily accompanied them to the precinct, since a reasonable innocent person in defendant's situation would have believed, at the inception of the interview, that the police, who never displayed their weapons, were still in the process of gathering information about the alleged incident. Accordingly, the suppression court properly concluded that defendant was not in custody when the interview began and that the police were not required to read the Miranda warnings at that point.

 

432 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Amazon.com, LLC v New York State Dept. of Taxation & Fin.


1534, 1535, 601247/08, 107581/08


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


81 A.D.3d 183; 913 N.Y.S.2d 129; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7943; 2010 NY Slip Op 7823



November 4, 2010, Decided 
November 4, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Overstock.com, Inc. v. New York State Dept. of Taxation & Fin., 2013 N.Y. LEXIS 542 (N.Y., Mar. 28, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff on-line retailers (OLRs) appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed their complaints, seeking a declaratory judgment that challenged the constitutionality of Tax Law § 1101(b)(8)(vi), as amended on April 23, 2008, on commerce clause, due process, and equal protection grounds.


OVERVIEW: The amendment to § 1101(b)(8)(vi) required the OLRs to collect a sales and use tax on purchases made by New York residents. The trial court granted dismissal of the complaints under CPLR 3211(a)(2) and (7). On appeal, the court found that the facial challenges failed to state a cause of action. The court found no validity to a facial challenge based on the Commerce Clause under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8(3), as the tax collection obligation was only imposed on an out-of-state vendor where it entered into a business-referral agreement with a New York State resident, and only when that resident received a commission based on a sale in New York. Accordingly, the nexus requirement was satisfied. Due process facial challenges also failed, as the presumption created was not irrational and irrebuttable, and § 1101(b)(8)(vi) was not void for vagueness with respect to various terms used therein. The as-applied challenges were ripe for review. However, issues of fact existed as to some of the as-applied challenges, including under the commerce and due process clauses. There was no showing of a viable equal protection claim under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court judgment by declaring the statute constitutional on its face, and constitutional on its face and as applied with respect to the Equal Protection Clause. The matter was remanded for further proceedings with respect to the as-applied challenges under the due process and commerce clauses. The remainder of the trial court judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Presumption of Constitutionality -- Facial Challenge to Statute 

1. Facial challenges to a statute's constitutionality are disfavored. A plaintiff can only succeed in a facial challenge by establishing that no set of circumstances exists under which the act would be valid, i.e., that the law is unconstitutional in all of its applications. Although language in a footnote in City of Chicago v Morales (527 US 41, 55 n 22 119 S Ct 1849, 144 L Ed 2d 67 [1999]) might be interpreted to the effect that the "no set of circumstances" standard is not the appropriate test, in the absence of any unequivocal holding to the contrary, continued reliance on the "no set of circumstances" standard is warranted.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Collection of Sales Tax by Out-of-State Internet Vendors -- Nexus with Taxing State

2. On its face Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, does not violate the Commerce Clause of the Federal Constitution. It imposes a tax collection obligation on an out-of-state vendor only where the vendor enters into a business-referral agreement with a New York State resident, and only when that resident receives a commission based on a sale in New York. The statute does not target the out-of-state vendor's sales through agents who are not New York residents. Thus, the requirement that there be a substantial nexus with the taxing state is satisfied. Moreover, the statute requires that there be solicitation, not mere passive advertising by the in-state resident.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Collection of Sales Tax by Out-of-State Internet Vendors -- Solicitation of Customers -- Presumption

3. On its face Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, does not violate the Commerce Clause of the Federal Constitution where the statute requires that there be solicitation, not mere passive advertising by the in-state resident. While the statute creates the presumption that the in-state agent will solicit on behalf of the vendor, it provides the out-of-state vendor with a "safe harbor": the vendor merely has to include in its contract with the in-state vendor a provision prohibiting the in-state representative from "engaging in any solicitation activities in New York State that refer potential customers to the seller," and the in-state representative must provide an annual certification that it has not engaged in any prohibited solicitation activities. However, the State has a legitimate basis to conclude that many in-state representatives will engage in direct solicitation, rather than mere advertising.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Collection of Sales Tax by Out-of-State Internet Vendors -- Facial Challenge

4. A facial challenge by plaintiff, an out-of-state Internet vendor, to the constitutionality of Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi) as violative of the Commerce Clause of the Federal Constitution failed inasmuch as there was a set of circumstances under which the statute would be valid. The challenged statute, which provides that out-of-state vendors must collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, requires a substantial nexus between the vendor and the taxing state and a solicitation of business rather than mere passive advertising on the in-state residents' Web site. A program by plaintiff whereby commissions were given to in-state residents who directed business to it was not designed for the passive advertiser, but sought representatives looking to solicit business. Thus, until such time as the out-of-state vendor produced a certification from every one of its New York representatives that they had not engaged in solicitation, thereby bringing it within the "safe harbor" provision of the statute, the facial challenge based upon the Commerce Clause must fail. The statute would be valid where a New York representative used some form of proactive solicitation which resulted in a sale by plaintiff and a commission to the representative, and the representative had an in-state presence sufficient to satisfy the substantial nexus test.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Collection of Sales Tax by Out-of-State Internet Vendors -- Presumption of In-State Solicitation

5. Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, creates a not irrational presumption that in-state solicitation occurs when an in-state representative is paid a commission on a per sale basis after a New York purchaser accesses its Web site and "clicks" through to make a purchase at the out-of-state vendor's Web site. Solicitation in varying forms is an extremely plausible and likely avenue by which any competent businessperson would seek to improve revenues. In the event, however, the in-state representative wishes to chance success merely on luck and good fortune, without expending initiative, the statute permits it to offer proof that it did not engage in solicitation, and an implementing regulation provides that the proof can come in the form of a certification from the in-state representative that it did not engage in solicitation.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Collection of Sales Tax by Out-of-State Internet Vendors -- Due Process Vagueness Claim

6. Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, is not unconstitutionally vague. The words "or indirectly," used in referring to commissions paid by the out-of-state vendor, do not present any confusion. Since an in-state buyer can be referred by the in-state representative "directly" to the out-of-state vendor by a click on its Web site, the words "or indirectly" may mean referral by a manner other than a direct click, perhaps just by providing an e-mail address of the out-of-state vendor. In either case, the result is the same: a buyer has been routed to a seller by an intermediary. Neither are the words "or other consideration" in reference to commissions paid to the in-state resident vague. The statute simply provides that if there is some type of remuneration to the in-state representative other than a direct payment, the transaction will still be encompassed by the statute. Finally, the word "solicitation" is not so imprecise as to be unconstitutionally vague. While the Internet represents a significant change in communication, that fact does not require new definitions of terms that previously had a clear meaning: the maintenance of a Web site which the visitor must reach on his or her own initiative is not, under the statute or the advisory opinions of the Department of Taxation and Finance, a solicitation.

Courts -- Ripeness Doctrine -- Challenge to Tax Statute

7. A challenge to the constitutionality of Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, was ripe for judicial review where the Department of Taxation and Finance made clear its position that the statute applied to the activities of New York-based representatives of plaintiff Internet vendors taken on their behalf, and its intention to enforce the statute against them. Thus, the threat of harm to plaintiffs was "direct and immediate."

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Exhaustion of Administrative Remedies -- Tax Statute

8. A challenge to the constitutionality of Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, was not barred on the ground that plaintiffs, out-of-state Internet vendors subject to the statute, failed to exhaust their administrative remedies. There is an exception to the exhaustion requirement where a party is challenging the constitutionality or the basic applicability of a statute or regulation, and although that exception only applies where the issues are purely legal and do not require the resolution of factual issues, the circumstances herein were exigent enough to warrant review: plaintiffs were conducting an on-going business and required finality and clarity as to the extent of their present obligations.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- As-Applied Commerce Clause Challenge to Statute Requiring Out-of-State Internet Vendors to Collect Sales and Use Taxes

9. Where plaintiffs, out-of-state Internet vendors, challenged the constitutionality of Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, their claim that the statute, as applied, violated the Commerce Clause on the ground that plaintiffs' representatives did nothing more than advertise on New York-based Web sites could not be resolved because the Court could not conclude as a matter of law that plaintiffs' in-state representatives were engaged in sufficiently meaningful activity so as to implicate the State's taxing powers. Thus, given the limited, if nonexistent, discovery on the question, plaintiffs were afforded the opportunity to develop a record establishing whether their in-state representatives were soliciting business or merely advertising on their behalf. The Department of Taxation and Finance could find that plaintiffs' in-state representatives may have certified that they did not solicit business on plaintiffs' behalf, thereby coming within a "safe harbor" provision of the statute, yet engaged in activities that did constitute solicitation. The record did not permit a determination of whether the in-state representatives were engaged in activities which were "significantly associated" with the out-of-state vendors' ability to do business in New York.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- As-Applied Due Process Challenge to Statute Requiring Out-of-State Internet Vendors to Collect Sales and Use Taxes

10. In two declaratory judgment actions challenging the constitutionality of Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, dismissal of the entire complaints was premature where plaintiffs raised an "as applied" due process challenge based on their contention that the statute was both irrational and irrebuttable as applied to them. Further discovery was warranted so that plaintiffs could make their record that all their in-state representatives did was advertise on New York-based Web sites. The determining factor was whether it was irrational to conclude that plaintiffs' agreements with New York-based Web sites, by which they compensated the New York-based Web sites in exchange for those Web sites' referral of customers to plaintiffs through Web links, were by their nature sufficient to establish that the New York-based Web sites would engage in other activity that went beyond mere advertising and amounted to solicitation--to such a level that would satisfy the Commerce Clause 's substantial nexus requirement--of New York business on plaintiffs' behalf. Moreover, it would be premature to find that even as applied the due process challenges were unavailing, whether because the statute created an illegal and irrebuttable presumption, or because its language was so vague that plaintiffs could not ascertain which transactions gave rise to their obligations to collect the sales tax.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- As-Applied Equal Protection Challenge to Statute Requiring Out-of-State Internet Vendors to Collect Sales and Use Taxes

11. In a declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of Tax Law § 1101 (b) (8) (vi), which requires out-of-state vendors to collect sales or use taxes where they use in-state residents to solicit business from New York residents through Internet Web sites, plaintiff, an out-of-state Internet vendor, failed to establish the existence of a viable as-applied equal protection claim. Plaintiff could not claim that it was being exclusively targeted since it was being treated exactly the same as another out-of-state Internet vendor which brought its own declaratory judgment action having a similar business program. Moreover, plaintiff could not claim that it was treated differently from those out-of-state retailers which do not have a business program like its own, since those retailers were not similarly situated. Thus, there was no proof that plaintiff was improperly singled out.

Matter of Velez v DiBella

SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT

435 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Greenfield v Town of Babylon Dept. of Assessment


2009-06646


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


76 A.D.3d 1071; 908 N.Y.S.2d 251; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6873; 2010 NY Slip Op 6798



September 28, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Matter of Greenfield v. Board of Assessment Review for Town of Babylon, 2013 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3402 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, May 15, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner appealed a judgment by the Suffolk County Supreme Court (New York) that, inter alia, denied his hybrid CPLR art. 78 petition to declare that the method of tax assessment imposed by defendants, town department of assessment and assessor, was invalid and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: When the department assessed the owner's property at the sum of $ 9,590 for the 2008/2009 tax year, the owner filed a grievance with the town's board of assessment review, alleging an excessive and unequal assessment, and seeking reduction of the assessment. After his grievance was denied, the owner commenced a small claims assessment review (SCAR) proceeding, which was denied for failure to prove that the assessed valuation exceeded the full value of his property. The trial court found that the owner's challenges were untimely, that a village was a necessary party, and that the determination of the hearing officer was supported by a rational basis. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the owner's challenges were timely and that the village was not a necessary party. However, the owner failed to establish that the full market value of his property, multiplied by the applicable equalization rate or residential assessment ratio, was less than the assessed valuation of his property, as required by RPTL 729(2)(a), 732(2). Therefore, the owner was not entitled to art. 78 relief.


OUTCOME: The judgment was modified by deleting the provision thereof dismissing the cause of action seeking a judgment declaring that the method of tax assessment imposed was invalid and unconstitutional; and, as so modified, the judgment was affirmed, and the matter was remitted for further proceedings on the owner's constitutional challenge to the assessor's methodology.


HEADNOTES

Taxation--Assessment--Judicial Review.--Appellant timely commenced hybrid proceeding to review determination which denied his application to reduce tax assessment of his real property for tax year 2008/2009 and action for judgment declaring that method of tax assessment imposed was invalid and unconstitutional; court should have disregarded reference in petition/complaint to incorrect tax year as it was obvious mistake, and to disregard it would not prejudice substantial right of any party; moreover, appellant asserted timely challenges to assessment for 2008/2009 tax year at each stage of process, and then timely challenged Assessor's methodology in this proceeding; in any event, appellant also asserted claim pursuant to 42 USC § 1983, which has three-year statute of limitations.

Parties--Necessary Parties--Assessment Review.--Village was not necessary party in hybrid proceeding to review determination which denied appellant's application to reduce tax assessment of his real property and action for judgment declaring that method of tax assessment imposed was invalid and unconstitutional; appellant did not seek any relief from Village and only commenced small claims assessment review proceeding against Town Department of Assessment; in addition, no evidence was submitted to establish that Village's interest would be affected by outcome of proceeding requiring dismissal for failing to name it as party.

Taxation--Assessment.--Court dismissed so much of petition/complaint as sought to annul hearing officer's determination in small claims assessment review proceeding; evidence, which included comparable recent sales and appellant's listing of property for sale at price in excess of full market value as determined by Assessor, provided rational basis for hearing officer's determination that appellant failed to establish that assessed valuation of his property exceeded its full value; although appellant submitted applicable residential assessment ratio (RAR), which is median percentage of value applied to residential property by assessing unit during preceding year, appellant failed to establish that full market value of his property, multiplied by applicable RAR, was less than assessed valuation of his property.

 

436 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Stoffer v. Department of Pub. Safety of the Town of Huntington


2009-00896


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


77 A.D.3d 305; 907 N.Y.S.2d 38; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6537; 2010 NY Slip Op 6425



August 17, 2010, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent town department of public safety (the agency) appealed a judgment by the Suffolk County Supreme Court (New York) that granted petitioner owners' CPLR art. 78 petition and annulled the agency's revocation of the owners' accessory apartment permit; the owners claimed, inter alia, that the town's administrative tribunal lacked jurisdiction to adjudicate the matter.


OVERVIEW: After the owners obtained a permit to maintain an accessory apartment, in accordance with the permit, the agency issued them a notice of violation for the non-certified use and operation of a kennel on the premises. Following a hearing, the owners' accessory apartment permit was revoked for failure to comply with the town code, which required them to permit town officials to enter their property to determine whether the accessory apartment was in compliance with various codes. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the issue of whether the administrative tribunal had jurisdiction to adjudicate violations of the town code was a prerequisite to any additional determination as to the constitutionality of the accessory apartment law's warrantless search provisions. Since the area had been preempted by N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 3(a)(2) and Town Law § 135(1), the administrative tribunal lacked jurisdiction to adjudicate a violation of the town's zoning code. Accordingly, the owners were entitled to art. 78 relief.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- Appellate Division -- Reviewability 

1. In petitioners' CPLR article 78 proceeding to review the determination revoking their accessory apartment permit, in which the Supreme Court declined to reach the issue of whether the Town's Accessory Apartment Bureau (AAB) had jurisdiction to hear violations of the Town Code and elected to address the issue of whether the warrantless search provision of the Town Code's Accessory Apartment Law was unconstitutional, the issue of whether the AAB had jurisdiction to adjudicate violations of the Town Code was a prerequisite to any additional determination as to the constitutionality of the Accessory Apartment Law's warrantless search provisions which had to be addressed. Notwithstanding the contention that the issue of whether the AAB had jurisdiction to adjudicate violations of the Town Code was not properly before the Appellate Division, the Appellate Division could review petitioner's alternate basis for annulling the Hearing Officer's determination even though petitioners did not cross appeal from the judgment. Petitioners could not cross appeal from the judgment because they were not aggrieved thereby, since their petition was granted (see CPLR 5511). Moreover, the Appellate Division can review the alternate ground for relief alleged in the petition as a basis for affirmance. In addition, the issue presented related to the lack of subject matter jurisdiction of the AAB, a ground for relief that could not be waived and could be raised at any stage of a proceeding.

Local Laws -- Preemption by State -- Town Administrative Tribunal Lacked Jurisdiction to Adjudicate Zoning Code Violations

2. Respondent Town's Accessory Apartment Bureau (AAB), a quasi-judicial tribunal, lacked jurisdiction to determine whether petitioners' accessory apartment permit should be revoked due to their failure to consent to an inspection of their property for the purpose of determining whether the accessory apartment was in compliance with various codes as required by the Town's Accessory Apartment Law as a condition for the use of an accessory apartment. The administrative tribunal was an unconstitutional usurpation of the judicial function. The State has preempted local governments in municipalities of a certain size from adjudicating zoning code violations in an administrative tribunal, which would abrogate or supersede the jurisdictional framework created by the Legislature. The Legislature, acting in accordance with its constitutional authority to regulate the jurisdiction, practice and procedure of the courts (see NY Const, art VI, § 30) and by deeming zoning code violations to be offenses and misdemeanors (see Town Law § 135 [1]), conferred jurisdiction over civil actions to enforce ordinance violations upon the District Courts (see UDCA 203 [a]), and conferred jurisdiction over criminal enforcement actions upon the superior and local criminal courts (see CPL 10.10 [3]; 10.20 [1]; 10.30 [1]). Furthermore, the Town, by virtue of its population, was not otherwise authorized to create its own tribunal for the purposes of code enforcement (see General Municipal Law § 380). Moreover, since the AAB was without jurisdiction to adjudicate a violation of the Town's zoning code and to revoke petitioner's accessory apartment permit, petitioners' constitutional challenge to the provision of the law requiring them to consent to warrantless searches of their property was rendered academic.

Parties -- Necessary Parties -- Legislative Body Necessary Party in Proceeding Challenging Constitutionality of Legislative Act

3. Petitioners, whose accessory apartment permit was revoked by respondent Town's Accessory Apartment Bureau due to petitioners' failure to consent to an inspection of their property as required by the Town's Accessory Apartment Law as a condition for the use of an accessory apartment, were not entitled to declaratory relief in their CPLR article 78 declaring the provision of the law requiring warrantless searches to be unconstitutional, due to petitioners' failure to join the Town Board as a necessary party to the proceeding. In a matter seeking a declaratory judgment challenging a legislative act, the legislative body that enacted the challenged law or ordinance is a necessary party. Since the Town Board was not named as a party or joined, the Appellate Division could not exercise its authority pursuant to CPLR 103 (c) to convert the article 78 proceeding challenging the legislative act to a declaratory judgment action.

 

437 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Aides At Home, Inc. v State of New York Workers' Compensation Bd.


508375


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


76 A.D.3d 727; 907 N.Y.S.2d 84; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6435; 2010 NY Slip Op 6330



August 5, 2010, Decided 
August 5, 2010, Entered


HEADNOTES

Workers' Compensation--Coverage--Deficiency Assessment.--Workers' Compensation Board properly imposed assessment against petitioner, member of workers' compensation group self-insured trust--although petitioner was not member of Trust at time that assessment was levied, petitioner remained jointly and severally liable for liabilities of Trust that were incurred during petitioner's membership until those liabilities were satisfied (see Workers' Compensation Law § 50 [3-a] [former (2), (3)]); however, petitioner's share of Trust deficiency for year 2000 was properly limited and was based only on its contributions to Trust through date on which petitioner left Trust--amount of assessment was not unreasonable or excessive; assessment amount was supported by detailed actuarial analysis, which took into account both Trust's known liabilities and claims incurred but not reported, and was calculated using widely-accepted allocation methodology.

 

438 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v Strawbridge


101212


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


76 A.D.3d 115; 905 N.Y.S.2d 687; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6274; 2010 NY Slip Op 6181; 244 N.Y.L.J. 22; 244 N.Y.L.J. 23



July 22, 2010, Decided 
July 22, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v. Strawbridge, 15 N.Y.3d 895, 938 N.E.2d 1019, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 3427, 912 N.Y.S.2d 584 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: defendant appealed an order by the Albany County Supreme Court (New York) that denied her CPL 440.10 motion to vacate her second-degree murder conviction without a hearing; defendant claimed that the conviction was obtained in violation of her due process rights, that the evidence was insufficient, and that she was entitled to retroactive application of the decisions regarding depraved indifference.


OVERVIEW: After giving birth unassisted at home in 1997, defendant put her newborn baby in a plastic bag and then placed the bag in a dumpster. She was convicted of depraved indifference murder under Penal Law § 125.25(4). The appellate court found, inter alia, that at the time of defendant's trial, throughout her direct appeal, and until the time her conviction became final, the controlling case law provided that the phrase "under circumstances evincing a depraved indifference to human life" constituted a "factual setting" in which the risk-creating conduct occurred, focusing "upon an objective assessment of the degree of risk presented by defendant's reckless conduct. None changes in the depraved indifference murder standards applied as defendant's conviction became final before the changes in the law even began. In addition, the People proved all of the elements of the crime beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant's remaining contentions were considered and found to be unpersuasive. Consequently, defendant's CPL 440.10 motion to vacate was properly denied without a hearing.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Appeal -- Finality of Conviction 

1. Defendant's conviction for depraved indifference murder of a child became final 90 days after the Court of Appeals denied defendant's motion for leave to appeal the Appellate Division order affirming her conviction, as that was the date that defendant's time expired for filing a petition for a writ of certiorari in the United States Supreme Court. Defendant's application to the New York Court of Appeals for reconsideration did not extend the time in which to petition for certiorari.

Courts -- Retroactive Application of Judicial Decision -- Depraved Indifference Murder

2. The Court of Appeals established new law governing depraved indifference murder beginning in 2003, and while those new standards were formulated in cases involving convictions under Penal Law § 125.25 (2), since the pertinent statutory language is the same in both subdivisions (2) and (4), i.e., "under circumstances evincing a depraved indifference to human life," the Court of Appeals' formulation of the new standards governing that language and its retroactivity were equally applicable to convictions under Penal Law § 125.25 (4)--depraved indifference murder of a child.

Courts -- Retroactive Application of Judicial Decision -- Depraved Indifference Murder 

3. Defendant's motion to vacate the judgment convicting her of depraved indifference murder of a child under Penal Law § 125.25 (4) was properly denied. None of the changes in depraved indifference murder enunciated in People v Sanchez (98 NY2d 373, 777 NE2d 204, 748 NYS2d 312 [2002]) applied retroactively to defendant, whose conviction became final prior to those changes. The Court of Appeals has unambiguously stated that the new law should not be applied on collateral review to defendants whose convictions became final prior to the new interpretation of the law of depraved indifference murder. The language of CPL 440.10 (2) (a), which precludes collateral review of points previously determined on appeal except where there has been a retroactively effective change in the law controlling the issue, does not have a meaning independent of and greater than under common-law retroactivity principles. It is ultimately for the Court of Appeals to determine whether and to what extent a new state judicial precedent operates retroactively. Moreover, given the circumscribed purpose of the new rules, nonretroactivity posed no danger of a miscarriage of justice, and defendant did not demonstrate that federal constitutional principles required retroactive application to her case.

Crimes -- Murder -- Depraved Indifference Murder -- Collateral Attack on Judgment 

4. On a motion to vacate the judgment convicting defendant of depraved indifference murder, the Appellate Division did not employ a deficient standard of review in affirming defendant's conviction on direct appeal. The Court determined that the People had proven all of the elements of the crime beyond a reasonable doubt, notwithstanding that it did not parrot all aspects of the legal sufficiency review standards and analysis. Defendant was not entitled, in a collateral proceeding, to reargue legal sufficiency claims considered and rejected on her direct appeal under then-controlling precedents. Moreover, her challenge to Penal Law § 125.25 (4) as unconstitutionally vague as applied to her was not raised before Supreme Court, was unpreserved for appellate review and lacked merit.

 

439 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Morrissey v Apostol


509141


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


75 A.D.3d 993; 906 N.Y.S.2d 639; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6270; 2010 NY Slip Op 6214



July 22, 2010, Decided 
July 22, 2010, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), among other things, dismissed petitioner owner's application, in a combined proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 and action for declaratory judgment, to, among other things, review a determination of respondent zoning board denying the owner's application for a variance. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner challenged Albany, N.Y., City Code § 375-65, which restricted occupancy of his premises to a single family. He also sought a use variance allowing six unrelated individuals to live in his house. The appellate court found that the ordinance was not unconstitutional, as the terms "family" and "functional equivalent of a traditional family" were not so vague as to confound a person of ordinary intelligence and, thus, was not susceptible to arbitrary enforcement. The meaning of those terms was readily ascertainable given the body of case law-specific to the zoning realm-interpreting the term "family." Further, the owner did not satisfy the criteria required for a variance. In addition to presenting questionable financial data, the owner made no effort to show that he could neither increase the rent nor sell the property at a profit-either as a one-family or two-family home, and failed to demonstrate that the property was significantly distinct from other residences in the neighborhood. Further, inasmuch as the occupancy restriction on the premises had long been in existence when the owner bought the property, he failed to demonstrate that any hardship was not self-created.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations--Zoning.--Petitioner, who rented premises to six unrelated college students in violation of local zoning ordinance which restricted occupancy of premises to single family, was not entitled to use variance permitting six unrelated individuals to reside in dwelling--definition of "family" found in ordinance was not unconstitutional; pursuant to ordinance, "family" means "[o]ne, two or three persons occupying a dwelling unit ... or ... [f]our or more persons occupying a dwelling unit and living together as a traditional family or the functional equivalent of a traditional family," and it is presumed that four or more unrelated persons living in single dwelling unit are not functional equivalent of traditional family--terms "family" and "functional equivalent of a traditional family" were not so vague as to confound person of ordinary intelligence and, thus, ordinance was not susceptible to arbitrary enforcement.

Municipal Corporations--Zoning--Variance.--Petitioner, who rented premises to six unrelated college students in violation of local zoning ordinance which restricted occupancy of premises to single family, was not entitled to use variance permitting six unrelated individuals to reside in dwelling--in addition to presenting questionable financial data, petitioner made no effort to show that he could neither increase rent nor sell property at profit, either as one-family or two-family home, and failed to demonstrate that property was significantly distinct from other residences in neighborhood; furthermore, inasmuch as occupancy restriction on premises had long been in existence when petitioner purchased property, he failed to demonstrate that any hardship was not self-created.

 

440 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


State of New York v. Campany


493 CA 09-00375


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


77 A.D.3d 92; 905 N.Y.S.2d 419; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6127; 2010 NY Slip Op 6041



July 9, 2010, Decided 
July 9, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by, Motion dismissed by State v. Campany, 15 NY3d 713, 938 NE2d 1012, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 3246, 912 NYS2d 577 (N.Y., Oct. 26, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a Mental Hygiene Law art. 10 proceeding brought by respondent State, following a jury verdict, the Supreme Court, Lewis County (New York), determined that respondent inmate was a dangerous sex offender requiring confinement and committed him to a secure treatment facility. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmate argued that he had a right to effective assistance of counsel and was denied that right. The appellate court found that, although this was a civil action, the fact that a commitment may have been indefinite and involuntary was an "extraordinary circumstance" affording the right to effective assistance of counsel. However, the inmate did not receive ineffective assistance. Counsel was not ineffective in stipulating that neither he nor the State's attorney would observe an exam conducted by the psychiatric examiner for the other party. The underlying issue was not resolved until after the stipulation was entered, and the inmate's attorney was not required to anticipate a change in the law. The inmate's claim that counsel failed to investigate his case was based on matters outside the record and thus was not before the appellate court. The inmate's claim that his attorney was ineffective for failing to argue that the report of the psychiatric examiner appointed for him should have remained private was meritless. Counsel was not ineffective in using a peremptory challenge to exclude a prospective juror who had known the inmate, rather than challenging him for cause.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sex Offenders -- Civil Commitment or Supervision -- Right to Effective Assistance of Counsel

A proceeding pursuant to Mental Hygiene Law article 10 to determine whether a respondent is a dangerous sex offender requiring confinement, although civil in nature in view of the legislative intent to treat rather than punish offenders (see Mental Hygiene Law §10.01), nonetheless requires that the respondent receive effective assistance of counsel. In the context of civil litigation, a contention concerning ineffective assistance of counsel will not be considered absent "extraordinary circumstances." The potential of indefinite (see Mental Hygiene Law §10.09 [b], [f]) and involuntary civil confinement constitutes an "extraordinary circumstance." Here, respondent, a repeat sex offender found by the court to be a dangerous sex offender requiring confinement in a secure facility following a jury verdict finding that he had a mental abnormality that predisposed him to commit further sex offenses (see Mental Hygiene Law §10.03 [i]; §10.07 [d]), was not denied meaningful representation, viewing the evidence, the law and circumstances of the case as a whole and as of the time of the representation. Respondent's attorney was not ineffective in stipulating that neither party would observe any examination conducted by the other party's psychiatric examiner. The issue whether petitioner is permitted to attend the psychiatric examination of a respondent conducted on respondent's behalf in a Mental Hygiene Law article 10 proceeding was not resolved until after the subject stipulation was entered, and respondent's attorney was not required to anticipate a change in the law.

Ziegler v Serrano


508592


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


74 A.D.3d 1610; 905 N.Y.S.2d 297; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5205; 2010 NY Slip Op 5314; 243 N.Y.L.J. 120



June 17, 2010, Decided 
June 17, 2010, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by Ziegler v. Serrano, 15 NY3d 714, 939 NE2d 145, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 3450, 913 NYS2d 128 (N.Y., Nov. 23, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant ex-wife appealed the grant of summary judgment by the Supreme Court (Ulster County, New York) in favor of plaintiff homeowners' on their quiet title action by adverse possession or, alternatively, on res judicata and collateral estoppel grounds. The trial court found that the dismissal of the ex-wife's earlier action for failure to prosecute was entitled to res judicata and that the homeowners showed adverse possession.


OVERVIEW: The ex-wife and her husband purchased the property at issue. She left without informing her husband that she was leaving or where she was going. He thereafter was granted a divorce upon her default. A subsequent action for partition of the marital residence likewise resulted in a default judgment in his favor and he was awarded fee title to the property. The following month, he conveyed the property by deed to the property owners. The ex-wife later challenged their title to the property. That action was dismissed. The homeowners continued to occupy the property and use it exclusively as their residence without interference for another 14 years. The appellate court held the trial court erred in granting summary judgment on res judicata grounds. Nevertheless, the homeowners were entitled to judgment quieting title to the land based on adverse possession. It was undisputed that they continuously possessed and exclusively occupied the property in question since the land was conveyed to them 23 years prior to the filing of the action. Throughout that time, they undertook numerous acts that were consistent with those of a property owner and sufficient to put defendant on notice.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Adverse Possession--What Constitutes.--Plaintiffs were entitled to judgment quieting title to land based on adverse possession; plaintiffs continuously possessed and exclusively occupied property in question since it was conveyed to them in 1985, during which time they undertook numerous acts consistent with those of property owner and sufficient to put defendant on notice; plaintiffs' continued possession under 1985 deed and dismissal of defendant's 1992 action challenging their title gave them reasonable basis to believe they owned property under claim of right; defendant failed to show that plaintiffs had knowledge of purported fraudulent conveyance at time it was made, and deed by one purporting to have authority to convey provides requisite color of title to support adverse possession claim.

443 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Central Hudson Gas & Elec. Corp. v Assessor of Town of Newburgh


2009-01985, 2009-02021


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


73 A.D.3d 1046; 904 N.Y.S.2d 429; 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4325; 2010 NY Slip Op 4381



May 18, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal dismissed by Cent. Hudson Gas & Elec. Corp. v. Assessor of Newburgh, 15 NY3d 865, 936 NE2d 913, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 2966, 910 NYS2d 31 (N.Y., Oct. 19, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent town appealed and petitioner taxpayer cross-appealed an order and judgment by the Orange County Supreme Court (New York) that granted the taxpayer's motion to strike a portion of the town's trial appraisal report concerning certain easements, reduced the tax assessments for its property, and directed that any overpayments be refunded with interest.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer's RPTL art. 7 petitions alleged that the town's assessments of its properties were illegal, unequal, and unconstitutional, resulting in overvaluation of its properties. The appellate court found, inter alia, that the opinion of the taxpayer's expert appraiser as to depreciation was reached by a recognized appraisal method based upon sound theory that was employed using objective data, which yielded substantial evidence of overvaluation. The taxpayer presented the testimony of a professional engineer who had the requisite knowledge with respect to analyzing reproduction cost. The trial court erred, however, in granting the taxpayer's motion to strike a portion of the town's trial appraisal report since the easements in question were not real property under RPTL 102(12) and, thus, were not separately assessable for tax purposes. While the easements were not subject to tax as real property, the costs of acquiring the easements were properly considered in calculating the reproduction cost of the subject transmission lines. The trial court also erred in assessing three tax parcels at a value less than that requested in the petition, in violation of RPTL 720(1)(b).


OUTCOME: The cross-appeal was dismissed as abandoned, the appeal from the order was dismissed, the judgment was reversed, the motion to strike was denied, the order was modified accordingly, and the matter was remitted for further proceedings, and thereafter for a new determination on the petitions.


HEADNOTES

Taxation--Assessment.--In proceedings to review assessments of parcels consisting of gas and electric transmission lines, substations and switching station, petitioner rebutted presumption of validity of assessments through opinion of its expert appraiser as to depreciation, which was reached by (1) applying age-life approach using average service lives independently calculated after review of regional and national depreciation statistics databases, petitioner's own reported average services lives contained in submissions to Federal Energy Regulatory Commission and opinion of expert professional engineer who inspected subject properties as to useful lives of subject properties, and (2) deducting for negative net salvage.

Taxation--Assessment.--In proceedings to review assessments of parcels consisting of gas and electric transmission lines, substations and switching station, it was error to grant motion to strike portion of Town's trial appraisal report concerning easements petitioner possessed in land in which transmission lines were placed since easements are not defined as real property (see RPTL 102 [12]) and are not separately assessable for tax purposes; nonetheless, appropriate method of valuation of subject transmission lines was reproduction cost less depreciation method; while easements were not subject to tax as real property, costs of acquiring easements, which were costs necessary to re-creation of functioning transmission lines, were properly considered in calculating reproduction cost of subject transmission lines.

Taxation--Assessment.--Court erred in assessing certain tax parcels at value less than that requested in petition, in violation of RPTL 720 (1) (b).

People v Taylor


102079


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT

Thereafter, defendant pleaded guilty to second-degree assault and agreed to be sentenced as a mandatory persistent violent felony offender to a prison term of 12 years to life. Defendant's remaining contentions were barred by his waiver of his right to appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Appeal--Waiver of Right to Appeal.--Defendant's valid waiver of his right to appeal precluded him from challenging procedure used in sentencing him as persistent violent felony offender.

Morgenthau v Khalil
