Larabee v. Governor of the State of New York


112301/07, 47614761A


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


65 A.D.3d 74; 880 N.Y.S.2d 256; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4126; 2009 NY Slip Op 4296



June 2, 2009, Decided 
June 2, 2009, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Chief Judge of the State of New York v Governor of the State of New York, 65 AD3d 898, 884 NYS2d 862, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6307 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, 2009)
Motion granted by Larabee v Governor of N.Y., 13 NY3d 890, 921 NE2d 604, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4513, 893 NYS2d 836 (2009)
Motion granted by Larabee v Governor of N.Y., 13 NY3d 889, 921 NE2d 603, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4511, 893 NYS2d 835 (2009)
Motion granted by Larabee v Governor of N.Y., 13 NY3d 889, 921 NE2d 603, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4506, 893 NYS2d 836 (2009)
Motion granted by Larabee v Governor of N.Y., 13 NY3d 890, 921 NE2d 604, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4497, 893 NYS2d 836 (2009)
Motion granted by Larabee v Governor of N.Y., 13 NY3d 917, 922 NE2d 883, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 117, 895 NYS2d 296 (2010)
Modified by, Sub nomine at Matter of Maron v Silver, 14 NY3d 230, 925 NE2d 899, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 39, 899 NYS2d 97 (N.Y., Feb. 23, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judges sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted a motion by defendants, the New York Governor and other State officials, to the extent of dismissing the complaint regarding judge compensation against the Governor and dismissing a claim under N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 25(a) entirely. Defendants appealed the grant of summary judgment to the judges on a separation of powers claim.


OVERVIEW: The judges sought declaratory and injunctive relief based on the failure to enact any enhancement in compensation for members of the state judiciary within the prior 10-year period. The lack of increase, despite multiple efforts to do so, allegedly violated § 25(a). Further, the judges claimed that defendants engaged in "linkage" when legislatively considering judicial pay raises. The trial court dismissed all claims against the Governor, as he acted within his authority, it dismissed the claim under § 25(a), and it granted summary judgment on the separation of powers claim. On appeal, the court agreed that the Governor had acted appropriately and had no liability. Further, inflation and the escalation in the cost of living did not "diminish" judicial compensation for purposes of § 25(a) in the face of legislative inaction. Accordingly, dismissal of the Compensation Clause claim was proper. The Speech or Debate Clause, N.Y. Const. art. III, § 11, did not shield defendants from review of the separation of power claim on the issue of linkage. The legislature's linkage of the judicial compensation issue with compensation for legislators violated the doctrine of separation of powers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Governor -- Powers of Governor -- Challenge to Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Compensation

1. The Governor was not properly part of an action challenging the Legislature's failure to increase judicial compensation due to political conflicts between the executive and legislative branches of state government involving the linkage of salary increases for judges to increases for legislators. There was no evidence that the Governor exceeded his authority, or thwarted either the enactment of judicial pay raise legislation or the expenditure of appropriated funds for judicial compensation. Rather, the Governor facilitated the process by bringing the issue of judicial salaries to the Legislature by means of submitting the Judiciary's budget request to the Legislature unchanged, as he was required to do, and with his approval. The fact that the Legislature reacted by refusing to enact a judicial pay increase could not be attributed to the Governor.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Compensation -- Compensation Clause

2. The failure of the Legislature to increase judicial compensation due to political conflicts between the executive and legislative branches of state government involving the linkage of salary increases for judges to increases for legislators did not violate the constitutional prohibition against the diminution of judicial compensation (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [a]). The loss of purchasing power due to inflation since the last judicial salary adjustment did not "diminish" judicial compensation by reducing wages or benefits in any direct fashion within the meaning of the constitutional prohibition. The indirect impact of inflation on judicial salaries is not unique to, or targeted at, the Judiciary. The Compensation Clause neither offers complete protection of the purchasing power of judicial salaries nor mandates cost of living adjustments to offset inflation.

Legislature -- Immunity from Prosecution -- Immunity under Speech or Debate Clause Not Available in Action Challenging Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Compensation 

3. Legislative immunity by operation of the Speech or Debate Clause (NY Const, art III, § 11) was unavailable to shield defendants from plaintiffs' separation of powers claim in an action challenging the Legislature's failure to increase judicial compensation due to political conflicts between the executive and legislative branches of state government involving the linkage of salary increases for judges to increases for legislators. While individual statements of legislators or legitimate legislative acts may be immunized, it does not automatically follow that the manner in which legislative decisions are made is similarly protected. The practice of linkage is not an inherently legislative function protected from a judicial challenge to the manner in which it is employed. Rather, linkage as applied was merely a legislative custom that served no legitimate legislative purpose other than to facilitate the personal remunerative goals of the Legislature's members. The intragovernmental dispute regarding judicial and legislative compensation went beyond the boundaries of the Legislature's internal communications, debates, committee work, investigations and other similar functions which rest within the legislative sphere. Under these circumstances, it could not be concluded that judicial review of this constitutional challenge was barred by operation of the Speech or Debate Clause.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Compensation -- Separation of Powers Doctrine

4. The failure of the Legislature to increase judicial compensation due to political conflicts between the executive and legislative branches of state government involving the linkage of salary increases for judges to increases for legislators violated the doctrine of the separation of powers. The legislative action in making a judicial salary increase contingent on achieving a legislative pay increase threatened the independence of the judicial branch of government. The political branches of government must discharge the responsibility of considering, and acting upon, an enhancement in judicial salaries on its objective merit. Linkage, as applied in these circumstances, manifested an abandonment of any pretense to an objective consideration of judicial compensation unimpeded by extraneous political considerations. These acts and their ramifications necessarily undermined New York's constitutional framework of government, which prohibits the subordination of the judicial branch to other branches of government either in practice or in principle. Moreover, evidence of a present impairment of the judicial system was not a prerequisite to the viability of the separation of powers claim. The constitutional defect was predicated on the manner in which the linkage distorted the relationship among the branches of government.

 

471 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of H.M. v. E.T.


2007-09323 (Docket No. U-110-07)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


65 A.D.3d 119; 881 N.Y.S.2d 113; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4087; 2009 NY Slip Op 4240



May 26, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Matter of H.M. v E.T., 13 NY3d 917, 922 NE2d 883, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 194, 895 NYS2d 296 (2010)
Motion granted by H.M. v E.T., 13 NY3d 928, 922 NE2d 892, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 122, 895 NYS2d 304 (2010)
Reversed by, Remitted by Matter of H.M. v E.T., 14 NY3d 521, 930 NE2d 206, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 621, 904 NYS2d 285 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the birth mother of a 12-year-old child, filed an action seeking to have appellant, the mother's former same-sex partner, adjudicated a parent of the child, and an award of child support retroactive to the child's birth. A support magistrate granted the partner's motion to dismiss the action. The Family Court, Rockland County, New York, granted the mother's objections to the magistrate's order. The partner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother alleged that she and her partner had agreed that the mother would become impregnated by anonymous sperm donation; that they would raise the child together; that after the child was born, the partner nurtured and cared for him for a few months; that the partner then ended the parties' relationship; and that she refused all requests for child support. The appellate court noted that the family court's order was not an order of disposition and, hence, not appealable as of right; however, under the circumstances, it treated the notice of appeal as an application for leave to appeal, and granted leave. The magistrate properly granted the partner's motion to dismiss the petition on the ground that the family court lacked subject matter jurisdiction to entertain the mother's application. The family court only had jurisdiction under Family Ct Act art. 5 to resolve controversies concerning a male's fatherhood of a child. As the family court had no constitutional or statutory to determine a female's paternity of a child, and had no power to grant equitable relief, it could not apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel to grant the mother the relief she sought.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the family court's order and reinstated the order of the magistrate dismissing the petition.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Family Court -- Subject Matter Jurisdiction -- Proceeding to Have Mother's Former Same-Sex Partner Declared Parent

Family Court lacked subject matter jurisdiction to entertain the petition of a child's birth mother pursuant to the Uniform Interstate Family Support Act (Family Ct Act art 5-B [UIFSA]) which sought to have her former same-sex partner, who lacked legal ties to her and lacked biological and legal ties to the child, adjudicated a parent of the child and required to pay child support. Family Court, a court of limited subject matter jurisdiction, is only competent to entertain such applications as the Constitution or a statute specifically enumerates. UIFSA authorizes a proceeding for a determination of "parentage" (Family Ct Act § 580-301 [b] [6]; § 580-701), and provides that in deciding such a proceeding, the Family Court is required to apply the procedural and substantive law generally applicable to a "similar" proceeding originating in this state, and may only exercise whatever "powers" and provide whatever "remedies" that are "available" in such a proceeding (Family Ct Act § 580-303 [1]). The only proceeding in this state similar to a proceeding for a determination of parentage is a paternity proceeding pursuant to Family Court Act article 5, which only provides a vehicle for resolving controversies concerning a male's fatherhood of a child. Further, although the doctrine of equitable estoppel can be applied in a proceeding pursuant to Family Court Act article 5, Family Court lacks the power to grant equitable relief; its application of the doctrine in article 5 proceedings is merely to preclude a party from denying a certain fact because of equitable considerations. Here, petitioner demanded certain relief the Family Court was not specifically authorized by the Constitution or statute to grant, and Family Court could not apply the doctrine.

 

472 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Goldstein v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp.


2008-07064


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


64 A.D.3d 168; 879 N.Y.S.2d 524; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 3754; 2009 NY Slip Op 3903



May 12, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Goldstein v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp., 13 NY3d 770, 915 NE2d 1167, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3492, 886 NYS2d 870 (2009)
Motion granted by Goldstein v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp., 13 NY3d 778, 915 NE2d 1174, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3530, 886 NYS2d 876 (2009)
Motion granted by Goldstein v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp., 13 NY3d 779, 915 NE2d 1175, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3537, 886 NYS2d 876 (2009)
Motion granted by Goldstein v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp., 13 NY3d 778, 915 NE2d 1174, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3543, 886 NYS2d 875 (2009)
Affirmed by Matter of Goldstein v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp., 13 NY3d 511, 921 NE2d 164, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4090, 893 NYS2d 472 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner owners of impacted property brought a proceeding pursuant to EDPL 207, seeking review of a determination of respondent New York State Urban Development Corporation, d/b/a Empire State Development Corporation (ESDC), which authorized the condemnation of real property. The owners asserted that the proposed taking of their properties violated N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 6, 7, 11, and N.Y. Const. art. XVIII, § 6.


OVERVIEW: ESDC initially argued that the proceeding was time barred under EDPL 207(A). However, the appellate court held that, as the owners first brought a timely federal court action, they were entitled to tolling under CPLR 205(a), and this action was timely. The federal courts' rulings did not preclude the owners' state-law claims. However, EDPL 207(C)(4) authorized the appellate court to consider whether a public use, benefit, or purpose was to have been served by the acquisition. The owners failed to show that the ESDC's determination was not rationally related to a legitimate public purpose. Over half of the project site was formally designated as blighted and in need of development more than 40 earlier. A blight study provided an adequate foundation for ESDC's conclusion that the land was substandard. The owners' argument that some of the public benefits may never actually have been realized was conclusory and speculative. Any incidental profit that may have inured to the private developer did not undercut the public purpose of the condemnation. The project's public benefits were not incidental or pretextual in comparison with benefits to particular, favored private entities.


OUTCOME: The determination was confirmed, the petition was denied, and the proceeding was dismissed on the merits.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- Commencement of Action after Termination of Prior Action -- Judicial Review of Eminent Domain Determination

1. A proceeding pursuant to EDPL 207 (A) challenging the condemnation of petitioners' properties, commenced within six months of the dismissal of petitioners' related state-law claims in a federal action, was not time-barred by the statute's 30-day time period within which to seek review of a condemnation determination. CPLR 205 (a), which provides a six-month grace period to recommence certain previously dismissed actions or proceedings, was applicable since petitioners timely asserted their state-law claims under the EDPL in the federal action, the state-law claims were dismissed by the federal court without an adjudication on the merits and the proceeding was commenced within six months after the date upon which the determination in the federal action became final. Petitioners did not forfeit their right to seek review of their state-law claims by electing to challenge the constitutionality of the condemnation in federal court rather than immediately commencing an EDPL proceeding in state court. The 30-day time period set forth in EDPL 207 (A) operates as a procedural limitation on the remedy, and not as a substantive condition precedent to judicial review which would have rendered the toll of CPLR 205 (a) inapplicable.

Judgments -- Collateral Estoppel -- Judicial Review of Eminent Domain Determination -- Effect of Dismissal of State-Law Claims in Prior Federal Action

2. Petitioners were not collaterally estopped from maintaining a proceeding pursuant to EDPL 207 (A) challenging the condemnation of their properties, where their state-law claims had been raised in a federal action and dismissed without an adjudication on the merits. The only issue before the federal courts was whether petitioners were able to state a claim establishing an unconstitutional taking under the United States Constitution. The primary issue in an EDPL proceeding is whether there was a foundation in the administrative record for the determination to acquire property through condemnation. Since collateral estoppel effect will only be given to matters actually litigated and determined in a prior action, the determinations made by the federal courts did not preclude petitioners from obtaining a review of the administrative record and litigating their state-law claims, as contemplated by the condemnation review scheme.

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Property -- Urban Redevelopment Project -- "Public Use" Requirement

3. The condemnation of petitioners' homes and businesses in order to facilitate advancement of an urban redevelopment project, including the construction of a publicly-owned sports arena and the creation of new housing, in an underutilized and blighted area, did not violate the Public Use Clause of the New York Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 7 [a]). The Public Use Clause, which provides that "[p]rivate property shall not be taken for public use without just compensation," is not to be read restrictively to allow the State to exercise its eminent domain power only where the condemned property is to be held open for use by all members of the public. A literal interpretation of the phrase "public use" is inconsistent with the more expansive formulation of "public use" set forth in EDPL 207 (C) (4), which expressly authorizes a reviewing court to consider whether a "public use, benefit or purpose will be served by the proposed acquisition." Petitioners otherwise failed to show that the challenged condemnation determination was not rationally related to a legitimate public purpose. It could not be said that the public benefits that the project was expected to yield were incidental or pretextual in comparison to the benefit that would be bestowed upon the project's private developer. Moreover, in light of evidence that much of the land to be acquired was substandard, and that the taking was rationally related to the purpose of remedying these substandard conditions, any incidental profit that might inure to the private developer from the remediation of the blighted project site would not undercut the public purpose of the condemnation of the substandard land.

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Property -- Urban Redevelopment Project -- Construction of Housing Not Restricted to Persons of Low Income

4. The condemnation of property in an underutilized and blighted area in order to facilitate advancement of an urban redevelopment project that would include new housing, did not contravene the Housing Article of the State Constitution (art XVIII, § 6) by allowing state funds to be used for the construction of housing without restricting occupancy of the new housing units to low-income persons. Section 6 provides for both the construction of low-income housing and the clearance and rehabilitation of substandard areas. Rehabilitation of substandard land may be achieved through the exercise of eminent domain without the simultaneous construction of a public housing project restricted to low-income occupants. It is only where the power of eminent domain is to be used to implement the separate purpose of providing low-income housing that state funding must be linked to the imposition of a restriction on occupancy to low-income persons. Since the core purpose of the project was to rehabilitate substandard land through improvements, including construction of a sports arena, publicly-accessible open space and community facilities, it could not be deemed a housing project within the ambit of the constitutional provision.

Constitutional Law -- Taking of Property -- Urban Redevelopment Project -- Due Process -- Equal Protection

5. The condemnation of petitioners' property in order to facilitate advancement of an urban redevelopment project did not violate petitioners' due process rights. Respondent substantially complied with the necessary procedural requirements by conducting a public hearing at which those in attendance were given a reasonable opportunity to present an oral or written statement and to submit other documents concerning the proposed public project (see EDPL 203). Although not every interested person who attended the hearing had an opportunity to speak because of the large turnout, respondent accepted written comments for more than one month after the close of the hearing, and conducted two community forums to allow area residents to express their views. Petitioners' equal protection claim similarly lacked merit in the absence of evidence that they had been intentionally treated differently from others similarly situated and that there was no rational basis for the difference in treatment.

People v. Bowe


102109


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


61 A.D.3d 1185; 876 N.Y.S.2d 762; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2827; 2009 NY Slip Op 2863



April 16, 2009, Decided 
April 16, 2009, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v Bowe, 912 NE2d 1085, 2009 NY LEXIS 3269, 884 NYS2d 704 (NY, July 13, 2009)


HEADNOTES

Animals--Cruelty to Animals.--Judgment convicting defendant of cruelty to animals in violation of Agriculture and Markets Law § 353 stemming from discovery of dead and emaciated horses on his property was affirmed--defendant's claim that statute was unconstitutionally vague because it fails to define term "necessary sustenance" was not considered; charges to which defendant pleaded guilty alleged that he failed to provide horses with "necessary sustenance" and that he failed to provide them with "sufficient food or drink"; statute specifically provides that failure to provide animal with "food or drink" constitutes violation thereof; thus, specifications of charges to which defendant pleaded guilty provided independent basis for finding violation of statute, irrespective of whether or not he failed to provide "necessary sustenance"--assertion that court erroneously construed Agriculture and Markets Law § 353 as strict liability statute was not substantiated by record.

 

474 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Barbara N. v. James H. N.


4399


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


62 A.D.3d 178; 877 N.Y.S.2d 16; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 2507; 2009 NY Slip Op 2478



March 31, 2009, Decided 
March 31, 2009, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought review of an order of the Family Court, New York County (New York), which, inter alia, denied her objection to a support magistrate's order that dismissed without prejudice the mother's petition seeking to vacate a 1992 child support agreement, to direct a new hearing, and to replace the child support agreement entered into with respondent father, whom she had never married, with a new schedule of support.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after the child's birth, the parties negotiated a child support agreement in which the father was to pay a total of $126,050 over a period of nine years to cover the child's expenses until she reached the age of 21. The agreement was submitted to the family court for approval as required by Family Ct Act § 516(a). Even though the parties' financial disclosure affidavits resulted in the father's annual child support obligation under the guidelines of the Child Support Standards Act (CSSA) being calculated at $21,052, the mother waived any right to future child support under the CSSA. The examiner made a brief inquiry as to whether the parties understood their obligations under the agreement and then approved it. On appeal, the court reversed the dismissal of the mother's motion to vacate the prior order. While the father had discharged his obligations under the agreement, the examiner failed to comply with the requirements in § 516(a) because an insufficient inquiry was made to protect the best interests of the child; the lump sum payment averaged out to about $6,000 a year for 21 years, and the gross disparity with the CSSA figure should have prompted further inquiry.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the family court's order and granted the mother's petition to the extent of remanding the matter for a hearing on the application for modification of the child support agreement.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Support - Nonmarital Child -- Modification of Completely Performed Support Agreement 

Petitioner mother was not precluded by Family Court Act § 516 from seeking to modify the terms of a completely performed 1992 negotiated child support agreement for her nonmarital child where the Hearing Examiner failed to comply with the requirements of the statute in approving the agreement without determining whether the agreement provided adequate support for the child for 21 years. The agreement required respondent father, in lieu of the support provisions required by the Child Support Standards Act (CSSA) (Family Ct Act § 413), to make a lump-sum payment of $ 126,050 to be paid over a period of nine years, even though CSSA guidelines would have provided for $ 21,054 in annual basic child support, without factoring in any statutory add-ons. The gross disparity between the two figures should at least have prompted the Hearing Examiner to conduct further inquiry as to whether the agreement was in the best interests of the child. Consequently, the matter was remanded for a new hearing at which petitioner will bear the burden to establish that the 1992 support obligations no longer provide for the best interests of the child.

People v. Mojica


2007-04084


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


62 A.D.3d 100; 874 N.Y.S.2d 195; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1499; 2009 NY Slip Op 1507; 241 N.Y.L.J. 44



February 24, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 15, 2010.
Appeal denied by In re Mojica, 12 NY3d 856, 909 NE2d 591, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 2801, 881 NYS2d 668 (N.Y., May 14, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the County Court, Dutchess County (New York), which convicted him of vehicular assault in the second degree (two counts) under Penal Law § 120.03(1) and operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol (two counts) under Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192(2) and (3). A motion to suppress the results of a blood alcohol test (BAT) had previously been denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged, as violative of due process, a rebuttable presumption created under § 120.03(1) that proof of operation of a motor vehicle while impaired or intoxicated and proof of serious physical injury to another person gave rise to a rebuttable presumption that the serious injury was the result of such intoxication. The evidence presented at the suppression hearing indicated that defendant drove through a red light and struck a marked patrol car driven by a city police officer, who suffered head injuries as a result. While defendant was treated for possible head injuries, he allegedly gave consent at the hospital to a BAT, which revealed that his blood alcohol content was more than twice the legal limit. On appeal, the court affirmed. The rebuttable presumption in § 120.03(1) was not unconstitutionally vague as it would not arise if a driver's operation of a vehicle could not be deemed a proximate cause of the accident at issue. At any rate, the People's evidence at trial, including eyewitness testimony that opposing traffic had a green light, was clearly sufficient to give rise to the rebuttable presumption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Application of Rebuttable Presumption in Vehicular Assault Statute 

1. In the prosecution of defendant for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [2], [3]) and vehicular assault in the second degree (Penal Law § 120.03 [1]), the application of the rebuttable presumption in Penal Law § 120.03 (1) that the serious injuries sustained by the victim were the result of defendant's intoxication did not violate defendant's due process rights. Penal Law § 120.03 (1) provides that the rebuttable presumption that a defendant's intoxication caused the subject accident arises only "[i]f it is established that the person operating such motor vehicle . . . caused such serious physical injury while unlawfully intoxicated or impaired by the use of alcohol or a drug." If a driver's operation of a vehicle cannot be deemed a proximate cause of the subject accident, then the rebuttable presumption would not arise, but here the evidence at trial was sufficient to give rise to the rebuttable presumption.

Crimes -- Vehicular Assault -- Rebuttable Presumption That Serious Injuries Were Caused by Driving while Intoxicated or Impaired 

2. In the prosecution of defendant for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [2], [3]) and vehicular assault in the second degree (Penal Law § 120.03 [1]), the evidence at trial was sufficient to give rise to the rebuttable presumption in Penal Law § 120.03 (1) that the serious injuries sustained by the victim were the result of defendant's intoxication. Defendant admitted that he had consumed a mixed alcoholic drink prior to the accident, and his blood alcohol content tested at .18% approximately two hours after the accident. Two eyewitnesses testified that defendant had a red light as he approached the intersection, and neither witness indicated that defendant slowed down as he approached the intersection. The electrician responsible for maintaining the traffic signals at the intersection testified that they were fully operational. In addition, a collision reconstructionist testified that his examination of the roadway and the two vehicles indicated that defendant did not brake prior to impact and there was no malfunction in the steering wheel or brakes of defendant's truck.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Vehicular Assault Statute Not Void for Vagueness 

3. Penal Law § 120.03 (1), which prohibits operating a vehicle in a manner that causes serious physical injury and while intoxicated and/or impaired by drugs in violation of Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 (2), (3), (4) or (4-a), is not unconstitutionally vague. The statutory language conveys sufficiently definite warning as to the proscribed conduct when measured by common understanding and practices. Specifically, the phrase "as a result of such intoxication" would be understood to mean that "by virtue of" or "flowing from" a driver's voluntary consumption of alcohol to the extent that the driver is incapable of employing the physical and mental abilities which he or she is expected to possess in order to operate a vehicle as a reasonable and prudent driver, serious physical injury occurs. Similarly, the statute provides clear guidelines to law enforcement personnel, as a person may be arrested for violating Penal Law § 120.03 based upon probable cause to believe that the defendant was driving while intoxicated and in doing so, operated a vehicle in a manner that caused serious physical injury.

Crimes -- Vehicular Assault -- Weight of Evidence 

4. Defendant's conviction of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [2], [3]) and vehicular assault in the second degree (Penal Law § 120.03 [1]) was not against the weight of the evidence. Defendant admitted that he had consumed a mixed alcoholic drink prior to the accident, and his blood alcohol content tested at .18% approximately two hours after the accident. Two eyewitnesses testified that defendant had a red light as he approached the intersection, and neither witness indicated that defendant slowed down as he approached the intersection. The electrician responsible for maintaining the traffic signals at the intersection testified that they were fully operational. In addition, a collision reconstructionist testified that his examination of the roadway and the two vehicles indicated that defendant did not brake prior to impact and there was no malfunction in the steering wheel or brakes of defendant's truck. The jury verdict demonstrated that the jury credited the testimony of the eyewitnesses regarding the red light, which, coupled with the electrician's testimony, supported the jury's implicit finding that the defendant disregarded a red light. Accordingly, defendant failed to rebut the presumption that his act of driving while intoxicated caused the accident.

Crimes -- Vehicular Assault -- Failure of Victim to Wear Seatbelt 

5. In the prosecution of defendant for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [2], [3]) and vehicular assault in the second degree (Penal Law § 120.03 [1]), it was not error to bar defendant from contending that the failure of his victim, an on-duty police officer, to wear a seat belt was an intervening cause of the victim's serious physical injuries. Defendant's contention on that point was speculative, as he had no expert witness available who could quantify or specify which of the victim's injuries would have been mitigated had he worn his seat belt. In any event, the victim, as an operator of an authorized emergency vehicle, was exempt from the requirement that he wear a seat belt (see Vehicle and Traffic Law §§ 101, 1229-c [3], [4]).

Motor Vehicles -- Chemical Tests -- Voluntary Consent to Blood Draw 

6. In the prosecution of defendant for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of alcohol (Vehicle and Traffic Law § 1192 [2], [3]) and vehicular assault in the second degree (Penal Law § 120.03 [1]), the suppression court properly determined that defendant voluntarily consented to the blood draw for testing his blood alcohol content. The arresting officer had probable cause to arrest the defendant for driving while intoxicated based on the totality of the circumstances, which included the information communicated to the arresting officer by the officer who had been on the accident scene and had spoken with the defendant, the arresting officer's own observations at the hospital of defendant's bloodshot and glassy eyes and detection of alcohol on defendant's breath, and the results of the Alco-Sensor test that showed alcohol in the defendant's system. Moreover, although defendant sustained a head wound during the accident, the evidence demonstrated that defendant was conscious, coherent and capable of exercising his rights. There was no evidence that the arresting officer, in obtaining defendant's consent, engaged in coercion, illegality or deception.

People v. Hernandez

Crimes--Sentence--Resentence--Postrelease Supervision.--Defendant's resentencing to postrelease supervision (PRS) pursuant to Correction Law § 601-d, which provides for identification and resentencing of defendants whose convictions required mandatory PRS component that had not been imposed by sentencing court, was not violation of his double jeopardy or due process protections; defendant had no legitimate expectation of finality with respect to determinate sentence with no PRS component; he understood that PRS was component of his sentence, as he had actually served three years of administratively-imposed PRS at time of resentencing; fact that administratively-imposed PRS was nullity did not render it irrelevant to his expectation of finality; resentencing was fundamentally fair, as he was resentenced only to originally promised determinate term, along with required term of PRS.

Lewis v. New York State Dept. of Civ. Serv.


504900


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


60 A.D.3d 216; 872 N.Y.S.2d 578; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 415; 2009 NY Slip Op 283; 45 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2377; 105 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 544



January 22, 2009, Decided 
January 22, 2009, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended January 23, 2009.
Appeal granted by Lewis v. New York State Dep't of Civ. Serv., 12 NY3d 705, 906 NE2d 1086, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 324, 879 NYS2d 52 (2009)
Motion granted by Lewis v. New York State Dep't of Civ. Serv., 13 NY3d 780, 915 NE2d 1176, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3541, 886 NYS2d 877 (2009)
Motion granted by Lewis v. New York State Dep't of Civ. Serv., 13 NYd781, 915 NE2d 1178, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3550, 886 NYS2d 879 (2009)
Motion granted by Lewis v. New York State Dep't of Civ. Serv., 13 NY3d 780, 915 NE2d 1176, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3551, 886 NYS2d 877 (2009)
Motion granted by Lewis v. New York State Dep't of Civ. Serv., 13 NY3d 781, 915 NE2d 1177, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3559, 886 NYS2d 878 (2009)
Motion granted by Lewis v. New York State Dep't of Civ. Serv., 13 NY3d 780, 915 NE2d 1177, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3560, 886 NYS2d 878 (2009)
Motion granted by Lewis v. New York State Dep't of Civ. Serv., 13 NY3d 781, 915 NE2d 1177, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3567, 886 NYS2d 878 (2009)
Affirmed by Godfrey v. Spano, 13 NY3d 358, 920 NE2d 328, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4050, 892 NYS2d 272 (N.Y., Nov. 19, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action involving a decision by defendant, the Department of Civil Service, to recognize the parties to a same-sex marriage as spouses under certain circumstances, plaintiff taxpayers sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which, among other things, granted summary judgment in favor of the Department and other defendants and dismissed the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The Department's policy, which recognized same-sex couples as spouses if their marriage was valid in the jurisdiction where it was solemnized, allowed such spouses of state employees access to the benefits provided under the state health insurance program provided by Civil Service Law §§ 161(1), 164(1). The taxpayers sought a declaration that the Department's policy was illegal, unconstitutional, and resulted in the unlawful disbursement of public funds. The court affirmed. An out-of-state same-sex marriage could be recognized under the marriage recognition rule because while provisions of the Domestic Relations Law limited marriages solemnized in New York to opposite-sex couples, those statutes had not been read by the state's highest court as invalidating same-sex marriages solemnized in other jurisdictions. Same-sex marriage was not abhorrent to public policy as New York had not enacted legislation to deny full faith and credit to out-of-state same-sex marriages as permitted under the Defense of Marriage Act, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1738C. Once such a marriage was recognized in New York, each party would be a party to a marriage and thus a legal spouse under Civil Service Law art. 11.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision.


HEADNOTES

Marriage -- Same-Sex Marriage -- Recognition of Valid Out-of-State Same-Sex Marriage for Purposes of Spousal Health Care Benefits -- Application of Marriage Recognition Rule 

1. The marriage recognition rule, which recognizes as valid a marriage considered valid in the place where celebrated, was applicable in an action seeking a declaration regarding the legality of the New York State Department of Civil Service's recognition of the parties to a valid out-of-state same-sex marriage as spouses for the purpose of allowing such spouses of state employees access to health insurance benefits, and warranted dismissal of the cause of action alleging that the recognition of such marriages was an unlawful disbursement of public funds. No New York statute expressly precludes recognition of a same-sex marriage solemnized elsewhere, and although the Court of Appeals has held that the Domestic Relations Law limits marriages solemnized in New York to opposite-sex couples (see Hernandez v Robles, 7 NY3d 338, 357, 855 NE2d 1, 821 NYS2d 770 [2006]), it did not hold that same-sex marriages solemnized elsewhere would not be defined as marriages in New York. Nor does New York public policy abhor the recognition of out-of-state same-sex marriages.

Marriage -- Same-Sex Marriage -- Recognition of Valid Out-of-State Same-Sex Marriage for Purposes of Spousal Health Care Benefits -- Civil Service Law Definition of Spouse 

2. The parties to a valid out-of-state same-sex marriage are spouses as defined under Civil Service Law article XI for the purpose of allowing such spouses of state employees access to the benefits provided under the New York State Health Insurance Program (see Civil Service Law § 161 [1]; § 164 [1]).

Constitutional Law -- Separation of Powers -- Recognition by Department of Civil Service of Valid Out-of-State Same-Sex Marriage for Purposes of Spousal Health Care Benefits 

3. In recognizing the parties to a valid out-of-state same-sex marriage as spouses for the purpose of allowing such spouses of state employees access to health insurance benefits, the New York State Department of Civil Service did not usurp the Legislature's authority in violation of the separation of powers doctrine. The Department's recognition of same-sex spouses fell squarely within the scope of the policy expressed in Civil Service Law §§ 161 and 164 to provide benefits to the spouses and dependent children of state employees. Moreover, in recognizing those marriages, the Department has not usurped the Legislature's power to subsequently determine by positive legislation that out-of-state same-sex marriages cannot be recognized in New York.

Constitutional Law -- Gift of Public Funds -- Recognition by Department of Civil Service of Valid Out-of-State Same-Sex Marriage for Purposes of Spousal Health Care Benefits

4. The policy of the New York State Department of Civil Service in recognizing the parties to a valid out-of-state same-sex marriage as spouses for the purpose of allowing such spouses of state employees access to health insurance benefits did not constitute the use of public funds to promote a private undertaking, the personal goal of a former Governor to create civil marriage equality in New York, in violation of NY Constitution, article VII, § 8 (1). Rather, the policy furthered a valid governmental purpose to benefit public employees.

Administrative Law -- Rule Making -- Recognition by Department of Civil Service of Valid Out-of-State Same-Sex Marriage for Purposes of Spousal Health Care Benefits

5. The determination of the New York State Department of Civil Service to recognize the parties to a valid out-of-state same-sex marriage as spouses for the purpose of allowing such spouses of state employees access to health insurance benefits was not invalid for the Department's failure to comply with the formal rulemaking procedures of the State Administrative Procedure Act, as the determination was an interpretative statement that was merely explanatory (see State Administrative Procedure Act § 102 [2] [b] [iv]). The Department's expansion of the definition of the term "spouse" was a reasonable interpretation of existing Department regulations that define the term "dependent" as "includ[ing] the spouse of an employee or retired employee" (4 NYCRR 73.1 [h]).
