484 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of MHC Greenwood Vil. NY, LLC v. County of Suffolk


2007-11588


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


58 A.D.3d 735; 874 N.Y.S.2d 135; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 362; 2009 NY Slip Op 388



January 20, 2009, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner retirement community operator appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which declared Suffolk County, N.Y., Loc. Law No. 1534 (2006), valid in all respects. The operator had brought a proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 to enjoin respondent county from enforcing Local Law No. 1534, and it sought a declaration that it was unconstitutional under the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The retirement community consisted of homes owned by individuals who were age 55 and over, but situated on land leased from the operator. Additionally, each resident had executed a residency agreement that required payment of a charge that was, in effect, a monthly land lease payment. At a public hearing, some residents complained that the common areas were in a state of disrepair and that the monthly charges were disparate and unreasonably raised when a home was sold. As a result of the hearing, Local Law No. 1534 was passed. The trial court determined that Local Law No. 1534 was valid. On appeal, the court found that as there was a rational basis for it, Local Law No. 1534 was not violative of the Equal Protection Clause. Further, there was no federal or state preemption. However, it found that several portions of Local Law No. 1534 should have been declared invalid because the county legislature lacked the constitutional or statutory power to enact those portions pursuant to NY Const. art IX, § 2(c)(ii)(10) and Municipal Home Rule Law § 10(1)(a). Such provisions interfered with the operator's right of enjoyment of its property and were unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the trial court by declaring various portions of the law invalid, and severing and striking them. As so modified, the court affirmed the order of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws--Validity--Regulation of Planned Retirement Communities.--Local Law enacted in response to certain policies of petitioner's planned retirement community, which sought to comprehensively regulate operations of such communities by extending protections to residents of those communities, did not violate Equal Protection Clause; even though petitioner's development was only one of its kind in County, Local Law did not intentionally treat petitioner differently than others similarly situated; Local Law regulates planned retirement communities, and County Legislature adopted Local Law with view that as population of County aged, it was anticipated that additional planned retirement communities would be established in County; thus, County Legislature had rational basis for adopting Local Law--Local Law was not preempted by state or federal legislation--however, several portions of Local Law should have been declared invalid, as County Legislature did not have constitutional or statutory power to enact those portions of Local Law.

 

485 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of New York Charter Schools Assn., Inc. v. DiNapoli


504682


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


60 A.D.3d 119; 871 N.Y.S.2d 497; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 154; 2009 NY Slip Op 158



January 15, 2009, Decided 
January 15, 2009, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected April 1, 2009.
Motion granted by N.Y. Charter Sch. Ass'n v. DiNapoli, 12 NY3d 840, 908 NE2d 921, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 911, 881 NYS2d 13 (N.Y., May 7, 2009)
Reversed by, Judgment entered by Matter of New York Charter Schools Assn., Inc. v. DiNapoli, 13 NY3d 120, 914 NE2d 991, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 2547, 886 NYS2d 74 (N.Y., June 25, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent charter school association filed suit seeking a declaration that General Municipal Law § 33(2) and Education Law § 2854(1)(c) were unconstitutional to the extent they authorized appellant New York State Comptroller to audit charter schools. The Supreme Court, Albany County, New York, held that the statutes violated N.Y, Const. art. V, § 1, and permanently enjoined the Comptroller from auditing charter schools. The Comptroller appealed.


OVERVIEW: General Municipal Law § 33(2) and Education Law § 2854(1)(c) directed the Comptroller to audit each charter school in New York State. The appellate court noted that it was undisputed that public school districts were subject to audit by the Comptroller, since they were considered political subdivisions of the State. Charter schools, while operating independently of existing school districts, retained many of the quintessential characteristics of public school districts and were part of the State's public school system. The significant public funding they received was linked to and had an impact upon public school districts. In light of the legislature's broad delegatory authority regarding the Comptroller, and the enormous public funding involved which implicated the Comptroller's fundamental duty under N.Y. Const. art. V, § 1 to supervise state fiscal matters, as well as the strong presumption of the constitutionality of statutes, the appellate court held that General Municipal Law § 33(2) and Education Law § 2854(1)(c) did not violate N.Y. Const. art. V, § 1.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment, dismissed the association's complaint, and declared that the challenged statutes were constitutional.


HEADNOTES

State -- Comptroller -- Authority to Audit Charter Schools

The Legislature did not violate NY Constitution, article V, § 1 when it directed audits of charter schools by the State Comptroller (see L 2005, ch 267; Education Law § 2854 [1] [c]; General Municipal Law § 33 [2]). Audits of charter schools are "incidental" to the Comptroller's authority to supervise the accounts of public school districts, and thus do not violate NY Constitution, art V, § 1. That provision, in setting forth the Comptroller's broad responsibility to supervise state fiscal matters, expressly limits the Legislature's power to assign to the Comptroller any administrative duties except as may be "incidental" to the performance of the Comptroller's constitutionally authorized functions. Charter schools, although operating independently of existing school districts, nevertheless maintain a statutorily established relationship with public school districts, especially with respect to funding (see Education Law § 2856). Charter schools, like public school districts, perform an important public function, and the significant public funding they receive is linked to and has an impact upon public school districts.

 

486 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Vinluan v. Doyle


2008-02568


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


60 A.D.3d 237; 873 N.Y.S.2d 72; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 199; 2009 NY Slip Op 219



January 13, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended July 23, 2009.
Amended by, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Matter of Vinluan v. Doyle, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5917 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, July 21, 2009)
Related proceeding at Anilao v. Spota, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 34919 (E.D.N.Y., Mar. 31, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to CPLR art. 78, petitioners, an attorney and 10 nurses from the Philippines, sought a writ of prohibition against respondent district attorney to prohibit the district attorney from prosecuting them under an indictment and to prohibit respondent judge from presiding over the criminal matter. Petitioners were charged with conspiracy and endangering the welfare of a child and of a physically-disabled person.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners' prosecution arose from the nurses simultaneously resigning from their jobs at a nursing home after their shifts ended as a result of failing to receive the pay they were promised and for other conditions of their employment that were not met. The indictment centered around the allegations that the attorney advised them to resign and that such resignations endangered the welfare of the patients at the nursing home, who were disabled children. Petitioners asserted that the prosecution violated their right against involuntary servitude as well as violated the attorney's First Amendment rights to advise his clients. The court agreed with petitioners and determined that the indictment explicitly made the nurses' conduct in resigning a component of each of the crimes charged, which impermissibly placed them in the position of being required to remain in the nursing home's employment, or be subject to criminal sanction. Furthermore, the prosecution impermissibly violated the attorney's constitutionally protect rights of expression and association in violation of the First and Fourteenth Amendments since he had a constitutional right to provide legal advice.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition for a writ of prohibition and ordered the district attorney prohibited from prosecuting petitioners under the indictment and prohibited the judge from presiding over the matter.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Prohibition -- When Remedy Available--Challenge to Constitutionality of Pending Criminal Proceeding

1. A proceeding for a writ of prohibition was an appropriate vehicle in which to raise a challenge to the constitutionality of a pending criminal proceeding against petitioners, nurses indicted for various crimes in connection with their simultaneous resignations from positions at a nursing home and the attorney who provided them with legal advice. Although not all constitutional claims are cognizable by way of prohibition, the presentation of an arguable and substantial claim which implicates a fundamental constitutional right generally results in the availability of a proceeding in the nature of prohibition. Here, petitioners claimed that the prosecution contravened the Thirteenth Amendment proscription against involuntary servitude by seeking to impose criminal sanctions upon the nurses for resigning their positions, and attempting to punish their attorney for exercising his First Amendment right of free speech in providing them with legal advice. If the prosecution impermissibly infringed upon those constitutional rights, the act of prosecuting the petitioners would be an act in excess in power, rather than a mere error of law, and prohibition would be an available remedy.

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Prohibition - When Remedy Available--Challenge to Constitutionality of Pending Criminal Proceeding -- Prosecution of Nurses Who Resigned 

2. Petitioner nurses were entitled to a writ of prohibition barring their prosecution on charges of conspiracy in the sixth degree, endangering the welfare of a child and endangering the welfare of a physically-disabled person in connection with their simultaneous resignations from their positions at a nursing home. Although the Penal Law provisions relating to endangerment of children and the physically disabled do not on their face infringe upon the Thirteenth Amendment right to be free from involuntary service by making the failure to perform labor or services an element of a crime, the indictment handed down against petitioners explicitly made their conduct in resigning their positions a component of each of the crimes charged. The prosecution had the practical effect of exposing petitioners to criminal penalty for exercising their right to leave their employment at will, and the imposition of such a limitation upon their ability to freely exercise their right to resign from the service of an employer who allegedly failed to fulfill the promises and commitments made to them was the antithesis of the free and voluntary system of labor envisioned by the framers of the Thirteenth Amendment. Although an employee's abandonment of his or her post in an "extreme case" may constitute an exceptional circumstance which warrants infringement upon the right to freely leave employment, respondent proffered no reason why this was an extreme case.

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Prohibition -- When Remedy Available--Challenge to Constitutionality of Pending Criminal Proceeding -- Prosecution of Attorney Arising Out of Advice to Client

3. Petitioner attorney was entitled to a writ of prohibition barring his prosecution on charges of conspiracy in the sixth degree, endangering the welfare of a child, endangering the welfare of a physically-disabled person and criminal solicitation in the fifth degree in connection with his representation of 10 nurses who simultaneously resigned from their positions at a nursing home. The prosecution impermissibly violated petitioner's constitutionally protected rights of expression and association in violation of the First and Fourteenth Amendments. The indictment asserted that petitioner committed the charged offenses by counseling the nurses to resign immediately, and by filing a discrimination claim on their behalf, and it affirmatively sought to punish petitioner for providing legal advice given in good faith. Petitioner did not advise the nurses to commit a crime, as the nurses' conduct in resigning could not, under the circumstances of the case, subject them to criminal prosecution. Regardless of whether petitioner's legal assessment was accurate, it was objectively reasonable, and an attorney who advises a client to take an action that he or she, in good faith, believes to be legal does not lose the protection of the First Amendment if his or her advice is later determined to be incorrect.

 

487 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Myles


101446, 101447


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


58 A.D.3d 889; 870 N.Y.S.2d 598; 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 55; 2009 NY Slip Op 65



January 8, 2009, Decided 
January 8, 2009, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by People v. Myles, 14 NY3d 890, 929 NE2d 1014, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 1860, 903 NYS2d 779 (N.Y., May 13, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted in the County Court of Schoharie County, New York, of falsifying business records in the first degree (Penal Law § 175.10), petit larceny, theft of services (Penal Law § 165.15(6)), and criminal tampering in the second degree (Penal Law § 145.15)). They appealed. The State conceded on appeal that the evidence had been insufficient to convict defendant two.


OVERVIEW: An electric company employee found jumper cables at a meter box at defendants' home that caused 10 percent of the current to bypass the meter. Defendant one testified he had no knowledge of the cables. The appellate court held that evidence that the meter had been altered by the cables; that defendant one had the skills, tools, materials, and opportunity to open the meter box without disturbing its seal and place the cables within; and that the cables allowed him use some electricity--"property" under Penal Law § 155.00(1)--without its being recorded, supported his larceny conviction. There was sufficient evidence that the cables were placed in the box without the company's consent to support defendant one's conviction of second degree criminal tampering. As the meter recorded the amount of electricity used, the meter itself contained the "business record" at issue as defined by Penal Law § 175.00(2), satisfying that element of the crime of falsifying business records. The theft of services statute allowed the jury to presume that the person receiving the services did the tampering with the intent to avoid payment for the services, and those presumptions were constitutional.


OUTCOME: Defendant one's convictions were affirmed; defendant two's convictions were reversed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Larceny.--In prosecution in which it was alleged that jumper cables had diverted some of electricity around meter at defendant's home, jury could rationally conclude that defendant placed jumper cables; there was evidence that electric meter had been altered by placement of jumper cables, and that defendant had skills, tools, materials, opportunity and motive to open meter box without disturbing its seal and place cables within; there was also evidence that jumper cables allowed defendant to use 10% of electricity without it being recorded by meter--from this evidence and fact that "property" is defined as including electricity (see Penal Law § 155.00 [1]), jury could readily infer that defendant tampered with electric meter and took property to which he was not entitled with requisite intent.

Crimes--Criminal Tampering.--In prosecution in which it was alleged that jumper cables placed by defendant had diverted some of electricity around meter at his home, conviction of criminal tampering (see Penal Law § 145.15) was supported by evidence; security investigator for electric power company testified that jumper cables did not belong in meter box and that he turned over investigation of matter to police; it could be inferred from this testimony that defendant had no right to tamper with company's equipment.

Crimes--Falsifying Business Records.--In prosecution in which it was alleged that jumper cables placed by defendant had diverted some of electricity around meter at his home, conviction of falsifying business records was supported by evidence; business records are defined as "any writing or article, including computer data or a computer program, kept or maintained by an enterprise for the purpose of evidencing or reflecting its condition or activity" (Penal Law § 175.00 [2]); evidence showed that meter itself recorded amount of electricity used and that presence of jumper cables caused it to record 10% less electricity than actually consumed; thus, meter itself contained business record.

Crimes--Theft of Services.--In prosecution in which it was alleged that jumper cables placed by defendant had diverted some of electricity around meter at his home, application of presumptions contained in statute defining crime of theft of services (see Penal Law § 165.15 [6]) did not violate defendant's due process rights; presumptions that person receiving service did tampering and that his or her intent was to avoid payment for that service are not unconstitutional; defendant cited no unique circumstance that would break obvious connection between his use of electricity and inference that he intentionally caused diversion.

 

488 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Craft


1324 KA 06-00785


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


57 A.D.3d 1388; 870 N.Y.S.2d 211; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10031; 2008 NY Slip Op 10270



December 31, 2008, Decided 
December 31, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Craft, 12 N.Y.3d 757, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 1344, 876 N.Y.S.2d 708 (2009)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed, Stay denied by Craft v. Kirkpatrick, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 71913 (W.D.N.Y., July 5, 2011)


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Gang Assault.--In prosecution of defendant for gang assault in first degree (Penal Law §120.07), contention that Penal Law §120.07 is unconstitutional lacked merit; phrase "aided by two or more persons actually present" contained in statute has plain meaning that excludes constructive presence, and statute was not vague as applied to defendant.

Crimes--Corroboration of Accomplice Testimony.--In prosecution for gang assault, testimony of defendant's accomplices was adequately corroborated (see CPL 60.22 [1]) by evidence that defendant and his accomplices sought confrontation, that defendant was wounded when he returned from scene of assault and that defendant's blood was found at scene of assault.

Crimes--Confession.--Suppression of defendant's statements to police was not warranted--police lawfully stopped defendant's vehicle, and defendant's statements to police at that time were not in response to custodial interrogation; statements were made in response to inquiry necessary for providing for defendant's physical condition and needs; defendant's subsequent statements were made after he waived his Miranda rights.

 

489 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wrotten


1984


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


60 A.D.3d 165; 871 N.Y.S.2d 28; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9965; 2008 NY Slip Op 10226



December 30, 2008, Decided 
December 30, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by People v. Wrotten, 12 NY3d 773, 906 NE2d 1063, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 235, 879 NYS2d 30 (2009)
Motion granted by People v. Wrotten, 13 NY3d 826, 918 NE2d 952, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3968, 890 NYS2d 439 (2009)
Reversed by, Remitted by People v. Wrotten, 14 NY3d 33, 923 NE2d 1099, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4472, 896 NYS2d 711 (2009)
On remand at People v. Wrotten, 73 AD3d 637, 901 NYS2d 265, 2010 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4425 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, May 27, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of assault in the second degree. She appealed, contending that the Supreme Court, Bronx County, New York, erred in permitting the complainant to give televised testimony.


OVERVIEW: The complainant accused defendant of hitting him with a hammer and stealing his money. The trial court held that the State established by clear and convincing evidence that the elderly complainant was unable to travel from his home in California to New York without seriously endangering his health. It found him unavailable to testify and permitted the People to present his testimony by a live, two-way television conference. Defendant argued that the use of televised testimony violated her Sixth Amendment right of confrontation and was not statutorily authorized. The appellate court agreed with the latter contention and did not reach the constitutional issue. Defendant's objection to the procedure, and the trial court's ruling thereon, preserved the issue for review. CPL art. 65 authorized trial courts to admit televised testimony only by child witnesses in certain sex crime cases and under carefully specified circumstances. The trial court did not have the authority under Judiciary Law § 2-b(3) to in effect revise art. 65 by expanding its scope. Separation of powers principles precluded trial courts from allowing televised testimony in circumstances not authorized by art. 65.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Witnesses -- Unauthorized Admission of Witness's Televised Testimony 

1. Defendant was entitled to a new trial due to the unauthorized admission at trial of the complainant's live, two-way televised testimony from outside the state. It was error to allow the televised testimony based on the finding that the People established by clear and convincing evidence that complainant was unable to travel to New York without seriously endangering his health and was thus unavailable to testify. In the absence of any express legislative authorization, the court lacked the inherent authority pursuant to Judiciary Law § 2-b (3) to permit the televised testimony. The statute limits the authority of the court to adopt "new process and forms of proceedings" to those that are "necessary to carry into effect the powers and jurisdiction possessed by it," and is only concerned with the authority of courts over matters of procedure. The receipt of the televised testimony of an absent witness in a criminal case necessarily requires a substantive policy decision that must be legislative in nature. The expanded use of televised testimony in this case was inconsistent with the comprehensive legislative scheme contained in CPL article 65 authorizing the televised testimony of a witness only in certain sex crime cases and only with respect to certain child witnesses. It could be inferred that the expression of authority to permit televised testimony under specific limited circumstances indicated an exclusion of authority under other circumstances. Consequently, even if admission of the televised testimony of defendant's principal accuser did not violate defendant's Sixth Amendment right of confrontation, the receipt of such testimony denied her a valuable component of that right and tainted the fairness of the trial.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Review -- Unauthorized Admission of Witness's Televised Testimony 

2. Defendant was entitled to a new trial due to the unauthorized admission at trial of the complainant's live, two-way televised testimony from out of state. The question of the authority of the trial court to admit the televised testimony was preserved for appellate review by defendant's timely and specific protest that the procedure sought by the People to obtain the witness's testimony at trial was not authorized, as well as by the motion court's express ruling on that protest that the inherent power of the courts pursuant to Judiciary Law § 2-b (3) was sufficient to authorize it (CPL 470.05 [2]). Even though defendant did not specifically address the issue of the trial court's lack of authority on appeal, the issue presented a pure question of law that would merit review by the Court of Appeals. In any event, even if defendant's Sixth Amendment right of confrontation was not violated, she was denied a valuable component of that right. In the absence of express legislative authorization, depriving defendant of a face-to-face meeting with her principal accuser tainted the fairness of the trial.

 

490 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Greene


2003-09567


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


57 A.D.3d 1004; 871 N.Y.S.2d 323; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10237; 2008 NY Slip Op 10636



December 30, 2008, Decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Possession of Forged Instrument.--Defendant's right to equal protection was not violated when he was charged with criminal possession of forged instrument in second degree (see Penal Law § 170.25) rather than criminal possession of forged instrument in third degree (see Penal Law § 170.20); prosecutor has broad discretion to decide what crimes to charge, and "overlapping" criminal statutes do not violate defendant's constitutional rights--"forged instrument" that defendant allegedly possessed was forged credit card; thus, defendant's possession of card fell squarely within conduct proscribed by Penal Law § 170.25.

 

491 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


McLean v. City of Kingston


504356, 505256


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


57 A.D.3d 1269; 869 N.Y.S.2d 685; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9810; 2008 NY Slip Op 10088



December 24, 2008, Decided 
December 24, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by McLean v. City of Kingston, 12 NY3d 848, 909 NE2d 85, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 966, 881 NYS2d 392 (N.Y., May 12, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner sued defendant city, seeking a judgment declaring Kingston, N.Y., Code ch. 332 unconstitutional under the New York and United States Constitutions, an injunction preventing enforcement of the law, and judgment for damages under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The Supreme Court, Ulster County (New York), denied the owner's motion and granted the city's cross motion for summary judgment, dismissing the complaint. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner argued that Kingston, N.Y., Code ch. 332 was unconstitutional because it did not require a warrant if the property owner did not consent to an inspection and because he was penalized for trying to exercise his right to refuse to consent to an inspection. The appellate court disagreed. As the plain language of Kingston, N.Y., Code ch. 332 required either the consent of the owner or a valid search warrant in order for the Building Safety Division to inspect property, it was facially constitutional. Nor was there any evidence that a refusal to consent to an inspection was considered a violation of the ordinance. Similarly, the evidence proffered by the owner did not establish the application of criminal sanctions on the basis of a refusal to consent to an inspection, as opposed to a failure to keep or cancel a scheduled inspection. Thus, on its face, the statute did not unconstitutionally penalize a property owner for refusing to consent. Since the owner's right to refuse to consent to a warrantless search was not penalized, his consent was voluntary and not vitiated under these circumstances. Further, the owner's 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim for damages was without merit.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law--Validity of Ordinance.--Ordinance dealing with registration and inspection of rental properties was not unconstitutional--inasmuch as plain language of ordinance requires either consent of owner or valid search warrant in order for Building Safety Division to inspect property, it was facially constitutional--there was no evidence that owner's refusal to consent to inspection was considered violation of ordinance, as reinspection fee is imposed only when property owner fails to notify Building Safety Division of his or her unavailability on date scheduled; thus, on its face, statute does not unconstitutionally penalize property owner for refusing to consent--plaintiff failed to demonstrate that ordinance was unconstitutional as applied to him; plaintiff admitted that he consented to inspection of his property after building inspector informed him that she would seek warrant; since his right to refuse to consent to warrantless search was not penalized, his consent was voluntary and not vitiated under these circumstances.

 

492 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


BLF Assoc., LLC v. Town of Hempstead


2007-06330


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


59 A.D.3d 51; 870 N.Y.S.2d 422; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9906; 2008 NY Slip Op 10111



December 23, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Leave to appeal denied by BLF Assocs., LLC v Town of Hempstead, 12 NY3d 714, 911 NE2d 860, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 2452, 883 NYS2d 797 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant town appealed an order by the Nassau County Supreme Court (New York) that granted plaintiff owner's motion for summary judgment and denied it cross-motion for summary judgment in the owner's action for a judgment declaring that Town of Hempstead, N.Y., Building Zone Ordinance art. XXXVIII (the article) was ultra vires, void, and unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: In furtherance of its interest in acquiring certain property from the United States Army pursuant to the Federal Base Closure and Realignment Act of 1990, the town formed a local redevelopment agency to develop a usage plan for the property. When the town chose not to purchase the property, the Army sold it to the owner. Thereafter, the town enacted the article to implement it reuse plan for the property. It created a planned residence district, which limited the number and type of homes that could be built on the property and required development of a community recreational facility. The appellate court found, inter alia, that rezoning the property to implement a specific project that the town had intended to construct if it acquired the property was not a consideration or purpose embodied in Town Law §§ 261, 262. The article was unnecessarily and excessively restrictive. It was not enacted for legitimate zoning purposes. The district and its specific form of mixed-usage development was inconsistent with the surrounding area. Therefore, the trial court properly denied the town's cross-motion and granted the owner's motion for summary judgment.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed, and the matter was remitted for the entry of a judgment declaring that the article was ultra vires.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Rezoning for Implementation of Specific Redevelopment Plan

Defendant Town's enactment of a zoning ordinance in order to implement a redevelopment plan for property previously owned by the United States, which plan included a mix of single-family and senior dwellings and a community recreational facility, was ultra vires of the Town's statutory zoning authority (Town Law § 261 et seq.) and, therefore, void as a matter of law. Plaintiff purchased the property through a competitive bidding process after the Town declined to exercise its right to acquire the property and redevelop it for a public purpose. Rezoning of the property for implementation of a specific project that the Town had intended to construct if it acquired the property was not a proper consideration or purpose embodied in the enabling statutes. While those statutes empower a town to regulate and restrict lot sizes and permitted uses, they do not empower a town to create a zoning ordinance that specifies the exact number and type of dwellings allowed. Zoning ordinances may go no further than determining what may or may not be built. The fact that the ordinance herein was unnecessarily and excessively restrictive supported the conclusion that it was not enacted for legitimate zoning purposes. Moreover, the provisions of the ordinance requiring that the recreational facility be owned by a homeowners' association and that the senior citizen dwellings be cooperative units was clearly ultra vires and void. Plaintiff's knowledge of the redevelopment plan before it closed title did not preclude its challenge to the ordinance.

 

493 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Walton v. New York State Dept. of Correctional Servs.


504552


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


57 A.D.3d 1180; 869 N.Y.S.2d 661; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9558; 2008 NY Slip Op 9876



December 18, 2008, Decided 
December 18, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Walton v. New York State Dep't of Corr. Servs., 13 NY3d 785, 916 NE2d 429, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3569, 887 NYS2d 535 (2009)
Motion granted by Walton v. New York State Dep't of Corr. Servs., 13 NY3d 785, 916 NE2d 429, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3568, 887 NYS2d 535 (2009)
Motion granted by Walton v. New York State Dep't of Corr. Servs., 13 NY3d 785, 916 NE2d 429, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 3573, 887 NYS2d 535 (2009)
Affirmed by Matter of Walton v. New York State Dept. of Correctional Servs., 13 NY3d 475, 921 NE2d 145, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 4081, 893 NYS2d 453, 2009 NY Slip Op 8665 (N.Y., Nov. 23, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, recipients of collect telephone calls from inmates, sued respondents, the New York State Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) and a telephone company, alleging a commission on such calls collected by DOCS from the company was an unconstitutional taking of their property and violated their free speech and equal protection rights. The Supreme Court, Albany County, New York, dismissed the complaint. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: To make phone calls, inmates had to place collect calls from a telephone system maintained by the company under its exclusive contract with DOCS. Under that contract, the company remitted over half of its revenues to DOCS, which used the commissions for programs to benefit inmates. The appellate court held the "filed rate" doctrine did not bar appellants' constitutional claims, as they were not challenging the reasonableness of the rate, only the commissions paid to DOCS. That the company passed on the expense of the commissions to its customers did not transform the commissions into unlawful taxes. Appellants' First Amendment rights to free speech and association were not violated, as they did not allege that the charged rates were so exorbitant as to deny them the ability to communicate with inmates by phone. As a prospective recipient of a collect call was in complete control over whether to accept the call, there was no unconstitutional taking. Appellants' equal protection claim failed because they did not demonstrate either that their fundamental rights had been infringed or that they were treated differently from any persons who were similarly situated.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Public Utilities--Rate Making--Filed Rate Doctrine--Challenge to Inmates' Collect-Call Telephone System.--Filed rate doctrine, which holds that rate approved by governing regulatory agency is per se reasonable and unassailable in judicial proceedings brought by ratepayers, did not bar constitutional claims by recipients of collect calls from inmates at facilities of Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) who sought to enjoin DOCS from collecting commission on its contract with telephone corporation that provided subject telephone system and service, inasmuch as Public Service Commission had determined that it lacked jurisdiction to review challenged portion of rate and, thus, declined to consider whether that portion of rate was just and reasonable.

Public Utilities--Rate Making--Challenge to Inmates' Collect-Call Telephone System.--Where petitioners, recipients of collect calls from inmates at facilities of Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) sought to enjoin DOCS from collecting commission on its contract with respondent telephone corporation, which provided subject telephone system and service, court properly determined that commission was not tax unrelated to use of respondent's telephone system and for which petitioners were legally liable to state or any other governmental entity; commission was legitimate business expenses paid to gain access to telephone users, and fact that respondent passed these expenses on to its customers did not transform such commissions into taxes.

Constitutional Law--Freedom of Speech--Challenge to Inmates' Collect-Call Telephone System.--Constitutional claims by petitioners, recipients of collect calls from inmates at facilities of Department of Correctional Services (DOCS) who sought to enjoin DOCS from collecting commission on its contract with telephone corporation that provided subject telephone system and service, failed to state cause of action--imposition of portion of charged rate corresponding to DOCS commission did not violate petitioners' rights to free speech and association in absence of indication that petitioners' constitutional rights had been infringed; because inmates retain their First Amendment rights and may exercise those rights insofar as is consistent with their incarcerated status, state must provide reasonable opportunity for inmates to communicate with outside world; petitioners herein asserted simply that they were not able to speak on phone with their friends and relatives "as much as they would like."

Constitutional Law--Taking of Property--Challenge to Inmates' Collect-Call Telephone System.--Where petitioners, recipients of collect calls from inmates at facilities of Department of Correctional Services (DOCS), sought to enjoin DOCS from collecting commission on its contract with telephone corporation that provided subject telephone system and service, petitioners' claim of unconstitutional taking was properly dismissed--inasmuch as prospective recipient of collect call is in control over whether to accept call and thereby relinquish his or her money to pay for it, there was no taking.

Constitutional Law--Equal Protection of Laws--Challenge to Inmates' Collect-Call Telephone System.--Where petitioners, recipients of collect calls from inmates at facilities of Department of Correctional Services (DOCS), sought to enjoin DOCS from collecting commission on its contract with telephone corporation that provided subject telephone system and service, petitioners' equal protection claim was properly dismissed; petitioners failed to demonstrate that their fundamental rights had been infringed or that they were treated differently from any persons who were similarly situated.

Matter of Doe


3355


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


58 A.D.3d 186; 868 N.Y.S.2d 40; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8943; 2008 NY Slip Op 9293



November 25, 2008, Decided 
November 25, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by In re Doe, 12 NY3d 865, 909 NE2d 579, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 1770, 881 NYS2d 656 (2009)
Affirmed by, Certified question answered by Matter of Doe, 14 NY3d 100, 923 NE2d 1129, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 19, 896 NYS2d 741 (2010)
Motion to strike denied by Doe v E.R.J., 14 NY3d 748, 925 NE2d 84, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 3837, 898 NYS2d 81 (N.Y., Feb. 16, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent alleged adoptive mother sought review of an order of the Surrogate's Court, New York County (New York), which granted petitioner alleged adoptive father's application for immediate visitation and for vacatur of a prior order, which granted the mother's application to re-adopt the minor child at issue, a Cambodian orphan.


OVERVIEW: The parties were never married. Because of U.S. visa restrictions on Cambodian orphans, the parties decided that the father as a citizen of Trinidad and Tobago would legally adopt the child; the child would then be granted U.S. citizenship because the father was a U.S. citizen as well. The father was granted final approval of the adoption by the Cambodian government in 2004, and an amended birth certificate was issued. However, when Trinidad denied the father's application to re-adopt the child, the mother sought to adopt the child herself. After the father relinquished the permission granted to adopt the child, the Cambodian government granted the mother's request to adopt the child. The surrogate's court had granted a re-adoption but vacated the order because the mother failed to reveal that the father was listed on the child's birth certificate. On appeal, the court affirmed. The act of state doctrine did not apply because the case involved an adoption proceeding in the U.S., a matter of private as opposed to public interests, and a letter and a document from the Cambodian government claiming that the father's adoption was invalid did not constitute acts of state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the surrogate's court.


HEADNOTES

International Law -- Act of State Doctrine -- Application of Doctrine to Foreign Adoption

The act of state doctrine, which generally requires American courts to recognize the validity of acts of foreign sovereigns taken within their own jurisdictions and applicable there, did not apply in a proceeding seeking vacatur of an order of the Surrogate's Court granting respondent's application to readopt a Cambodian orphan, in which the parties invoked the jurisdiction of the New York courts and all of the parties including the child were New York domiciliaries. In an effort to circumvent United States visa restrictions on Cambodian orphans, the parties had agreed to have petitioner legally adopt the child and become his sole legal parent so that the child would be granted United States citizenship by virtue of petitioner's status as a United States citizen. However, the parties then brought the child to New York on a temporary medical visa where he has since resided without legal immigration status. Two documents from Cambodian governmental entities obtained ex parte by respondent, stating that she had been legally granted permission to adopt the child and that Cambodia's prior approval of petitioner's request to adopt the child was considered "null and void," involved an attempt to influence the outcome of the litigation between the parties and did not constitute "acts of state" within the meaning of the doctrine. Moreover, the doctrine's territorial limitation precluded its application to the subject documents. Application of the doctrine to this foreign adoption would improperly allow a foreign sovereign to dictate parental rights and the legal status of New York domiciliaries.

People v. Caswell


1451 KA 06-01104


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


56 A.D.3d 1300; 867 N.Y.S.2d 638; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8744; 2008 NY Slip Op 9241



November 21, 2008, Decided 
November 21, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Caswell, 11 N.Y.3d 923, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 504, 874 N.Y.S.2d 8 (2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Caswell v. New York, 556 U.S. 1286, 129 S. Ct. 2775, 174 L. Ed. 2d 278, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 4273 (2009)
Subsequent civil proceeding at Caswell v. Green, 424 Fed. Appx. 44, 2011 U.S. App. LEXIS 11612 (2d Cir. N.Y., 2011)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by, Without prejudice Caswell v. Racetti, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 142423 (W.D.N.Y., Dec. 12, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), which convicted defendant, upon a jury verdict, of robbery in the second degree under Penal Law § 160.10(2)(b), burglary in the second degree (two counts) under Penal Law § 140.25(1)(d), (2), and attempted robbery in the third degree under Penal Law §§ 110.00, 160.05.


OVERVIEW: On the date of the preliminary hearing, defendant objected to his trial counsel's waiver of defendant's rights pursuant to CPL 30.30 and 180.80; the hearing was adjourned to resolve defendant's request. A grand jury indictment was obtained during the four-day adjournment. On appeal, the court affirmed. The trial court was not required to address defendant's request to proceed pro se when it was first made because the request was made in the context of dissatisfaction with defense counsel based on his trial tactics and was not unequivocal. The adjournment of the preliminary hearing was appropriate as defendant had no constitutional or statutory right to such a hearing. Defendant was not denied the opportunity to testify before the grand jury as he received notice of the time and place of the grand jury presentation under CPL 190.50(5)(b) but declined the offer to testify. Admission of clothing worn by one of the victims was not improperly admitted in violation of Brady, although defendant failed to preserve that claim for review, because his claim that DNA testing of the blood on the shirt, if performed, would have proved to be exculpatory in nature was purely speculative.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Right to Representation Pro Se.--Even if defendant did not waive contention that court erred in failing to address his request to proceed pro se when it was first made, by consenting to adjournment, request was made in context of claim of dissatisfaction with defense counsel and was not unequivocal; because only ground advanced by defendant for dissatisfaction with defense counsel was based on his trial tactics, court properly declined to inquire further into defendant's complaints.

Crimes--Preliminary Hearings

Grand Jury--Right to Appear before Grand Jury

Crimes--Evidence.--Court did not err in admitting in evidence clothing worn by one of victims during incident, which was purportedly stained with defendant's blood--contention that clothing should not have been admitted in evidence because it had not been tested for DNA was rejected; defendant's claim that DNA testing, if performed, would have proved to be exculpatory in nature was purely speculative and, inasmuch as no such testing was conducted, defendant could not reasonably argue that he was denied exculpatory material; furthermore, because identification was not at issue, defendant was not denied fair trial based on failure to perform DNA testing.

People v. Smith


2005-05984


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


56 A.D.3d 695; 871 N.Y.S.2d 159; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8616; 2008 NY Slip Op 9104



November 18, 2008, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Upon defendant's guilty plea to criminal possession of a controlled substance in the third degree, the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), adjudicated defendant a second felony offender and imposed a sentence of 4 1/2 to 9 years' imprisonment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The People served and filed a statement pursuant to CPL 400.21 alleging that defendant had been subjected to a predicate felony conviction in federal court. Defendant claimed that the conviction had been unconstitutionally obtained because, when he pled guilty, he was under the influence of drugs and had said so to the federal judge in open court. On the date of the hearing, defendant reported that he had not yet received the minutes of the plea in the prior case. The trial court, however, revealed that its chambers had contacted defendant's attorney and the judge in the prior federal case and, based on these communications, found that defendant was not under the influence of any drugs or alcohol at the time of the federal court plea. Accordingly, based on what it termed "overwhelming evidence," the trial court rejected defendant's claim, adjudicated him a second felony offender, and imposed sentence. The appellate court found that defendant was not properly adjudicated a second felony offender based on the reported results of the trial court's own investigation and the information privately provided to it by a federal judge and a defense attorney.


OUTCOME: The sentence was reversed, the adjudication of defendant as a second felony offender was vacated, and the matter was remitted to the trial court for resentencing of defendant before a different justice.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Sentence--Second Felony Offender.--Defendant was not properly adjudicated second felony offender (CPL 400.21)--defendant presented transcribed minutes of his federal plea proceeding which, he claimed, would show that his conviction had been unconstitutionally obtained because his plea was accepted despite his on-record statement that he was under influence of drugs; court improperly rejected defendant's claim based on reported results of its own investigation and information privately provided to it by federal judge and defense attorney; moreover, in addition to fact that defendant had no opportunity to confront federal judge or his prior attorney, to extent that their statements were considered by court to prove truth of assertion that defendant was not under influence of drugs at time of his federal plea, statements were hearsay and not, as required by applicable statute, "evidence admissible under the rules applicable to a trial of the issue of guilt" (CPL 400.21 [7] [a]).

 

498 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Maron v. Silver


504084


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


58 A.D.3d 102; 871 N.Y.S.2d 404; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9745; 2008 NY Slip Op 8573



November 13, 2008, Decided 
November 13, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended November 14, 2008
As Corrected November 14, 2008.

As Corrected January 26, 2009.
Appeal denied by Maron v. Silver, 12 N.Y.3d 909, 912 N.E.2d 1067, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 2554, 884 N.Y.S.2d 686 (2009)
Modified by, Remanded by Matter of Maron v. Silver, 14 N.Y.3d 230, 925 N.E.2d 899, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 39, 899 N.Y.S.2d 97 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, current and former justices, filed a mandamus petition against respondents, New York State and various state officials. The Supreme Court, Albany County, New York, dismissed all claims against the state officials, and all other claims except the allegations that the failure to increase judicial compensation violated the New York Constitution's Compensation Clause and separation of powers principles. All parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The justices' salaries had not been increased in nine years. They sought to compel disbursement of appropriated funds and an adjustment of their salaries for inflation. They argued that the system of setting their salaries violated the New York Compensation Clause, N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 25(a), and separation of powers principles, and denied them equal protection. The appellate court held that although it was an interested party to the dispute, as no other body could decide the issue, it had to hear the appeals under the Rule of Necessity. The effect of inflation was not a sufficient basis for a claim for an increase in salaries under the Compensation Clause. As the justices did not allege mass resignations by judges, or a plan by the other branches to attack the independence of the judiciary, they failed to state a separation of powers claim. As they did not allege they were selectively treated compared with others who were similarly situated, their equal protection claim failed. As N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 7 required payment within two years after passage of an appropriation act, and over two years had passed, mandamus was not available to compel payment of appropriated funds.


OUTCOME: To the extent the trial court denied respondents' motion to dismiss the justices' claims based on the Compensation Clause and separation of powers principles, it was reversed. Its orders were otherwise affirmed and the justices' petition was dismissed in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Justiciable Questions -- Constitutional Challenge to Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Compensation -- Rule of Necessity 

1. A proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78 brought by current and former justices of various courts of the Unified Court System challenging the constitutionality of the Legislature's failure to have increased judicial compensation since January 1, 1999 presented a justiciable controversy as to whether the legislative and executive branches complied with constitutional limitations placed on their authority that was not precluded by separation of powers principles. Although the State Constitution grants the Legislature and Governor the power to establish judicial compensation (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [a]), and expressly grants budgetary and appropriation powers to the legislative and executive branches (NY Const, art VII, § 7), the judicial branch retains the inherent power to protect itself from the impairment of its ability to function as an independent, coequal branch of government. In addition, although the Justices hearing the appeal had an interest in the outcome of the case, the rule of necessity required consideration of the constitutional challenges raised.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Constitutionality of Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Salaries under Compensation Clause of NY Constitution 

2. The failure of the Legislature to have provided the Judiciary with a salary increase since January 1, 1999 to offset the effects of inflation was not violative of that portion of the Compensation Clause of the State Constitution providing that the "compensation of a judge ... shall not be diminished during [his or her] term of office" (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [a]). The New York Compensation Clause, like its federal counterpart (see US Const, art III, § 1), was intended to protect the independence of the Judiciary, consistent with the doctrine of separation of powers, by prohibiting diminution of judicial compensation while permitting the legislative body to increase judicial salaries as circumstances warranted. The Compensation Clause does not guarantee a real income level or require cost of living adjustments to offset inflation. Rather, the Compensation Clause leaves the power to increase judicial salaries to the discretion of the political branches, as constrained by separation of powers principles which mandate only that the legislative and executive branches refrain from hindering the independence and proper functioning of the judicial branch. Petitioners failed to allege sufficient facts to state a valid claim of a discriminatory attack on the judicial branch that had impaired or imminently threatened the Judiciary's independence and ability to function.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Constitutionality of Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Salaries under Separation of Powers Doctrine 

3. The failure of the Legislature to have provided the Judiciary with a salary increase since January 1, 1999 to offset the effects of inflation was not unconstitutional under the separation of powers doctrine. The Compensation Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [a]) leaves the power to increase judicial salaries to the discretion of the political branches, as constrained by separation of powers principles which mandate only that the legislative and executive branches refrain from hindering the independence and proper functioning of the judicial branch. A constitutional violation would arise out of legislative failure to raise judicial salaries only if there was proof of a discriminatory attack on the Judiciary that had impaired or imminently threatened the independence of the Judiciary and the proper functioning of the judicial system. Here, petitioners' allegation that a number of judges had resigned or intended to do so for financial reasons and petitioners' speculative claim that the Legislature failed to act on the issue of judicial pay raises in retaliation for certain court decisions were insufficient to state a cause of action based upon a separation of powers violation. Petitioners' claim that the failure to enact a salary increase was designed to influence the Judiciary was undercut by the fact that legislators and senior executive branch officials had also been denied pay raises and by petitioners' assertions that respondents acted unconstitutionally in linking judicial pay raises to legislative and executive pay raises, along with other matters unrelated to judicial compensation. Moreover, the Speech or Debate Clause (NY Const, art III, § 11) would bar inquiry into the motives of the Governor or members of the Legislature in failing to take action on judicial pay raises.

Judges -- Judicial Salaries -- Constitutionality of Legislature's Failure to Increase Judicial Salaries under Equal Protection Analysis 

4. The failure of the Legislature to have provided the Judiciary with a salary increase since January 1, 1999 to offset the effects of inflation did not violate petitioners' equal protection rights by placing them in the same class as legislators in terms of salary increases, thereby rendering the Judiciary a "suspect class," and denying petitioners their claimed right contained in the Compensation Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [a]) to maintain the integrity of their salary and compensation. Petitioners failed to state an equal protection claim in the absence of any allegation that they were selectively treated compared with others who were similarly situated. Furthermore, as constitutional officers granted unique salary protection under the Compensation Clause, judges do not bear any of the traditional indicia of a "suspect class" as to warrant extraordinary protection. Thus, a rational basis standard of review would apply to petitioners' equal protection claim, and petitioners failed to negate every conceivable rational basis for the Legislature's inaction in amending article 7-B of the Judiciary Law.

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- Mandamus -- Disbursement of State Funds Appropriated for Judicial Pay Raises 

5. In a proceeding pursuant to CPLR article 78 brought by current and former justices of various courts of the Unified Court System seeking a declaration that the State Legislature unconstitutionally deprived the Judiciary of a salary increase since 1999 and seeking to require respondents to maintain judicial salaries apace with inflation, mandamus did not lie to compel the State Comptroller to release funds appropriated in the 2006-2007 state budget "to fund adjustments in the compensation of state-paid judges and justices of the unified court system" in anticipation of the passage of a subsequent chapter law adjusting judicial salaries that was never enacted. Since more than two years had passed from the passage of the appropriation bill, mandamus was not an available avenue of relief to obtain funds from the state treasury (see NY Const, art VII, § 7). In any event, the appropriation bill did not satisfy the constitutional requirement that judicial compensation be "established by law" (NY Const, art VI, § 25 [a]) so as to provide a basis for the relief sought by petitioners. Rather, the "dry appropriation" merely reflected the legislative intent that funds be made available for an event that did not occur, and did not by implication effectively amend Judiciary Law article 7-B to provide pay raises for the Judiciary.

 

499 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Anonymous v. City of Rochester


796 CA 07-02672


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


56 A.D.3d 139; 865 N.Y.S.2d 804; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7585; 2008 NY Slip Op 7724



October 10, 2008, Decided 
October 10, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Anonymous v City of Rochester, 55 AD3d 1425, 864 NYS2d 376, 2008 NY App Div LEXIS 7573 (NY App Div 4th Dep't, 2008)
Motion to vacate denied by, Motion granted by, Motion dismissed by Jiovon Anonymous v City of Rochester, 11 NY3d 908, 901 NE2d 1274, 2009 NY LEXIS 258, 873 NYS2d 522 (2009)
Motion granted by Anonymous v City of Rochester, 12 NY3d 792, 906 NE2d 1071, 2009 NY LEXIS 645, 879 NYS2d 37 (2009)
Motion granted by Anonymous v City of Rochester, 12 NY3d 792, 906 NE2d 1071, 2009 NY LEXIS 653, 879 NYS2d 38 (2009)
Affirmed by Anonymous v City of Rochester, 13 NY3d 35, 915 NE2d 593, 2009 NY LEXIS 2010, 886 NYS2d 648 (NY, June 9, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a father and his son, appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), which denied their motion for a judgment declaring that a curfew ordinance was unconstitutional. Plaintiffs brought suit against defendants, a city, its mayor, and its police chief, seeking the declaration. Plaintiffs also sought to enjoin defendants from enforcing the ordinance. The trial court granted defendants' motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The curfew ordinance provided that during designated hours, it was unlawful for a minor to be in a public place unless the minor could prove that one of the six enumerated exceptions set forth in the ordinance applied. Plaintiffs alleged that the ordinance was inconsistent with several state statutes and violated a number of rights guaranteed to plaintiffs under the New York and United States Constitutions. The court agreed and held that the ordinance was inconsistent with State law insofar as it applied to minors under the age of 16 and imposed an unconstitutional restriction upon the rights of parents and minors. The court found that, insofar as the ordinance provided that a minor under the age of 16 who violated the curfew ordinance committed a violation, the ordinance was inconsistent with Penal Law § 30.00. The court further held that the ordinance violated the equal protection clauses of U.S. Const. amend. 14, § 1 and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11, since the city failed to show a substantial relationship between the burdens imposed on juveniles by the curfew and the achievement of its objectives. The ordinance also violated free speech protections.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment. As such, the court denied defendants' motion to dismiss, reinstated the complaint, and granted plaintiffs' motion for a judgment declaring the ordinance unconstitutional. The court further ordered that defendants were enjoined from enforcing the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Ordinance -- Juvenile Curfew Ordinance 

A city ordinance establishing a nighttime curfew for juveniles under the age of 17 years during certain designated hours in public places unless one of six enumerated exceptions applied, enacted in response to the violent deaths of three minors over a four-year period, was unconstitutional as inconsistent with the general laws of the state and with the State and Federal Constitutions. The ordinance was inconsistent with Family Court Act § 305.2 and Penal Law § 30.00 to the extent that it authorized police officers to detain and take juveniles under the age of 16 into custody for a curfew violation that did not constitute a crime. In addition, the ordinance was violative of equal protection under a strict scrutiny standard of review with respect to juveniles between the ages of 16 and 17 years old by unconstitutionally infringing upon those juveniles' right of free movement. Although a heightened level of scrutiny was appropriate in view of the fundamental nature of the right of free movement, the ordinance could not withstand even intermediate scrutiny. The City failed to demonstrate a substantial relationship between the burdens imposed on juveniles by the curfew and the achievement of the City's otherwise legitimate objectives of reducing youth victimization and crime. Furthermore, the ordinance imposed an unconstitutional burden on the First Amendment rights of juveniles, and violated the fundamental substantive due process rights of parents to direct and control the upbringing of their children without undue governmental interference.

501 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Jonathan V.


3764, 3765, 3766


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


55 A.D.3d 273; 865 N.Y.S.2d 44; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7018; 2008 NY Slip Op 7176



September 30, 2008, Decided 
September 30, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by In re Michael B., 11 NY3d 713, 901 NE2d 762, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 3978, 873 NYS2d 268 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Family Court, New York County (New York), entered orders which adjudicated appellants, juveniles, delinquent, upon determinations that they each committed an act which, if committed by an adult, would have constituted second degree criminal possession of a weapon and that they each committed the act of unlawful possession of a weapon by a person under 16. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Police found a gun in a charter bus which had been occupied by the three appellants. Although earlier in the day, the bus was used on a commuter route, after the route was completed, the bus was taken from the bus company yard by the driver to take the bus company's dispatcher home. The driver and the dispatcher then traveled around, picking up members of the dispatcher's family. Eventually the bus was driven to the scene of a shooting and then to a hospital. The appellate court found that the Penal Law § 265.15(3) statutory presumption of possession was properly applied by the trial court. Although the bus was a "public omnibus" when it ran its route earlier, it was no longer being used as such when the gun was found. The absence of a definition of "public omnibus" in the Penal Law did not render § 265.15 impermissibly vague, since the term was given its legal meaning as defined in New York jurisprudence. The statute was sufficiently clear to apprise a person of ordinary intelligence of the sort of conduct prohibited. The application of this permissive presumption was constitutional because there was a rational connection between the facts proved and the ultimate fact presumed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Possession of Weapon -- Automobile Presumption -- Application of "Public Omnibus" Exclusion 

1. The statutory presumption of constructive possession of a firearm by the occupants of an automobile (Penal Law § 265.15 [3]) was properly appL Edin a juvenile delinquency proceeding to establish weapon possession charges against three respondents, who were riding on a charter bus that was being used by two bus company employees, without authorization, to transport a group of their family and acquaintances to a private rendevous where a shootout occurred. The statute's "public omnibus" exclusion applies only to buses that are being used to transport passengers for hire. Consequently, the automobile presumption applied because the charter bus was no longer being used as a "public omnibus" when the loaded weapon was found on the floor of the bus. Furthermore, the absence of a definition of the term "public omnibus" in the Penal Law does not render the statute unconstitutionally vague, since the term is given its ordinary legal meaning (see Vehicle and Traffic Law §§ 104, 126) so that a person of ordinary intelligence is apprised of the prohibited conduct. The evidence presented at the fact-finding hearing otherwise provided a sufficient basis for Family Court to have drawn an inference of possession in finding that each respondent was on the bus from the time it left the scene of the shooting until it arrived at the hospital where two of them were treated for gunshot wounds.

Crimes -- Suppression Hearing -- Reopening Hearing 

2. In a juvenile delinquency proceeding, Family Court did not err in reopening the suppression hearing to allow a police officer to testify. In the absence of a showing of bad faith by the presentment agency or prejudice to respondent, the suppression court had discretion to reopen the suppression hearing before rendering a decision in order to allow the presentment agency to elicit additional evidence to cure a deficiency in proof.

Crimes -- Confession 

3. Family Court properly denied respondent's motion to suppress his statements in a juvenile delinquency proceeding charging him with possession of a firearm. The court properly determined that despite reasonable efforts, the authorities were unable to notify a parent and acted reasonably, under all the circumstances, in interviewing respondent in the presence of a woman who identified herself as his aunt (see Family Ct Act § 305.2). The evidence also supported the court's conclusion that respondent's gunshot wound and accompanying medical treatment did not undermine the voluntariness of his statement.

People v. Smith


2006-01396


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


54 A.D.3d 974; 863 N.Y.S.2d 612; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6939; 2008 NY Slip Op 7163



September 23, 2008, Decided

As a second felony offender, defendant was sentenced to a term of seven years in prison and five years of post-release supervision. On direct appeal, the court held that defendant was improperly adjudicated a second felony offender as the predicate conviction had been obtained in violation of defendant's constitutional rights. 504 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cheatham


3694, 3695, 4290/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


54 A.D.3d 297; 863 N.Y.S.2d 407; 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 6540; 2008 NY Slip Op 6675



August 26, 2008, Decided 
August 26, 2008, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Cheatham, 11 NY3d 854, 900 NE2d 559, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 4047, 872 NYS2d 76 (2008)
Appeal denied by People v. McDowell, 11 NY3d 899, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 4342, 873 NYS2d 275 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The People sought review of the order of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which granted the motions to suppress physical evidence filed by defendants one and two, granted defendant two's motion to suppress statements, and dismissed the indictment.


OVERVIEW: Police stopped the vehicle in which defendants were riding as passengers after the driver committed a traffic violation. Police recovered cocaine from the right front door pocket of the car. In asserting his standing to challenge the search of the car, defendants relied solely on Penal Law § 220.25(1), which provided that, where a defendant was charged with possession solely on the basis of the statutory presumption that allowed a defendant to be convicted of possession based on his mere presence in the vehicle where the contraband was found, the defendant had automatic standing to challenge the search. However, this exception applied only where the criminal possessory charge was rooted solely in the statutory presumption. This was not the case here; thus, neither defendant had automatic standing. With respect to defendant two, the State, in proving possession, intended to rely on defendant two's statements made after his arrest. With respect to defendant one, the State intended to rely on testimony that, when officers activated the siren, defendant one looked at the police, dipped his body down so that his hands were by his feet area, and popped back up as the officers approached.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, denied defendant one's motion to suppress, denied defendant two's motion to suppress except to the extent that it sought to suppress statements as involuntary, reinstated the indictment, and remitted the matter to the trial court for it to determine the voluntariness of defendant two's statements. The court otherwise remanded the matter for further proceedings on the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Unlawful Search and Seizure--Standing.--Defendants were not entitled to suppression of cocaine found in vehicle in which they were riding as passengers; police lawfully stopped vehicle after driver changed lanes without signaling, removed occupants, then found cocaine in door pocket; defendants were not entitled to automatic standing to challenge search since defendants' possession of narcotics was not based solely on statutory presumption of possession, which allows conviction based on mere presence in vehicle in which contraband is found; rather, one defendant's statements and his close proximity to drugs, and other defendant's movements in back seat after looking at police car, was evidence of defendants' actual or constructive possession of contraband.

Crimes--Confession
