523 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


State of New York v. Town of Horicon


502371


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


46 A.D.3d 1287; 848 N.Y.S.2d 770; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13227; 2007 NY Slip Op 10442



December 27, 2007, Decided 
December 27, 2007, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, a town and others, sought review of an order and judgment entered by the Supreme Court, Warren County (New York), which, in a combined proceeding pursuant to CPLR art. 78 and an action for declaratory judgment, among other things, granted a motion for summary judgment filed by petitioners, the State and others, annulling Horicon, N.Y., Local Law No. 2 (2002).


OVERVIEW: The local law opened eight routes traversing state forest lands for use by all-terrain vehicles (ATVs). Petitioners claimed, inter alia, that respondents' adoption of the local law failed to comply with the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA), ECL art. 8. The trial court granted petitioners' motion for summary judgment on the basis that the State owned the land underlying the routes in question and that respondents failed to submit sufficient proof to raise a question of fact as to whether the subject routes were "highways by use" within the meaning of Highway Law § 189. On appeal, the court affirmed although for different reasons. Instead, the court held that respondents failed to comply with SEQRA as the adoption of the local law that could affect the environment constituted an "action" within ECL 8-0105(4) and required the town to ascertain whether the action might involve one or more other agencies. The town failed to designate the land manager for the underlying forest preserve as the lead agency or confer with the manager. Respondents also failed to take the required "hard look" at the various environmental impacts. Therefore, the local law had to be annulled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order and judgment.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws--Validity.--Local law which opened eight routes traversing state forest lands for use by all-terrain vehicles (ATVs) was annulled due to respondents' failure to comply with State Environmental Quality Review Act (ECL art 8 [SEQRA]) and/or Vehicle and Traffic Law § 2405 (1)--local law clearly affected environment, and Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC), as land manager for underlying forest preserve, qualified as "involved agency"; respondents made no effort to confer with DEC on matter, designate lead agency or otherwise undertake coordinated review process; strict compliance with SEQRA's procedural requirements was mandated; in any event, respondents failed to take requisite "hard look" at various environmental impacts and/or provide "reasoned elaboration" for negative declaration issued; perfunctory review process undertaken by respondents was devoid of any studies or analyses--Vehicle and Traffic Law § 2405 (1) permits agencies to open highways to ATVs when "it is otherwise impossible for ATVs to gain access to areas or trails adjacent to the highway"; although local law contained recital to this effect, there was nothing in record to substantiate Town Board's findings in this regard.

 

524 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. VanPatten


100315


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


48 A.D.3d 30; 850 N.Y.S.2d 213; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13258; 2007 NY Slip Op 10408



December 27, 2007, Decided 
December 27, 2007, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. VanPatten, 10 NY3d 845, 889 NE2d 91, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 1783, 859 NYS2d 404 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was found guilty of making a terroristic threat and sentenced as a second felony offender to the maximum of seven years in prison, appealed the entry of judgment from the County Court of Madison County (New York).


OVERVIEW: The court rejected defendant's argument that Penal Law § 490.20 was unconstitutionally vague as applied to him. Defendant could not have reasonably contended that in threatening a district attorney (DA), his family and others with murder unless the DA ceased prosecuting certain types of cases, he did not intend to influence the policy of government by intimidation or coercion or affect the conduct of government by murder, assassination or kidnapping, pursuant to Penal Law § 490.20(1). Defendant also could not have reasonably claimed that he did not thereby cause a reasonable expectation of fear. It was error, however, for the trial court to deny defendant's motion to suppress his statements to a police officer because the statements were solicited when defendant was in custody and in violation of his Miranda rights. The finding that defendant's initial statement was spontaneous was not supported by the record. Defendant's warned statements were obtained as part of a single continuous chain of events and also needed to be suppressed. The error was not harmless as there was a reasonable possibility that the statements contributed to defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction and ordered a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Making Terroristic Threat- - Statute Not Unconstitutional as Applied 

1. In the Penal Law § 490.20 prosecution of defendant for making a terroristic threat by threatening the lives of the District Attorney and several other people unless the District Attorney ceased any prosecutions involving violations of Penal Law article 490, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague as applied to defendant. The statutory language of Penal Law § 490.20 sufficiently apprises persons of ordinary intelligence of the type of conduct that is forbidden and provides law enforcement officials with clear standards for enforcement. Defendant could not reasonably contend that in threatening to murder several people unless the District Attorney ceased prosecuting certain cases, he did not intend to "influence the policy of a unit of government by intimidation or coercion[ ] or affect the conduct of a unit of government by murder, assassination or kidnapping" (Penal Law § 490.20 [1]). Moreover, defendant, having conceded that the District Attorney could have feared that defendant would have an accomplice pursue his threats in the letter to murder an extensive list of people "by the end of the year," could not reasonably claim that he did not "thereby cause[ ] a reasonable expectation or fear of the imminent commission of such offense" (Penal Law § 490.20 [1]).

Crimes -- Confession -- Miranda Warnings -- Questioning of Prison Inmate

2. In the prosecution of defendant for making a terroristic threat in a letter to the District Attorney, statements made by defendant to a police investigator in the detention facility where defendant was being held on unrelated charges were elicited in violation of defendant's Miranda rights and should have been suppressed. The interrogation, which took place in a classroom with correction officers standing outside the door while defendant was not permitted to leave on his own, was custodial in nature, and the investigator was obligated to inform defendant of his rights. Defendant admitted to writing the letter after the investigator explained why he was there and described the questions he intended to ask defendant regarding the letter, but before the investigator read him his rights. The investigator's explanation to defendant regarding why he wanted to speak with defendant was the functional equivalent of interrogation, and defendant's statement that he "wrote it" was not spontaneous. Although the investigator read defendant his rights immediately after defendant admitted to writing the letter, the incriminating statements defendant made after the warnings were administered were also inadmissible, as there was not a definite, pronounced break in questioning so as to provide defendant with sufficient time to reflect upon the situation and return him to the status of one who is not under the influence of questioning.

Crimes -- Harmless and Prejudicial Error -- Admission of Statements Elicited in Violation of Defendant's Miranda Rights

3. In the prosecution of defendant for making a terroristic threat in a letter to the District Attorney, the improper admission during trial of incriminating statements elicited in violation of defendant's Miranda rights in which defendant admitted to writing the letter in response to the District Attorney's prosecution of defendant's father was not harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. At trial, defendant's father testified that he wrote the letter himself and that defendant had nothing to do with the letter beyond putting it in an envelope and mailing it at his behest. Although defendant admitted during his parole revocation hearing that he threatened the safety of his parole officer and a deputy sheriff in the letter, he presented evidence that he was on Lithium and Paxil at the time of that hearing. Moreover, the People highlighted defendant's confession to the police investigator in urging the jury to reject the testimony of defendant's father, and the jury had requested a read-back of the investigator's trial testimony regarding defendant's confession.

 

525 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Seasia D.


2006-06882, (Docket No. A-17771-04)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


46 A.D.3d 878; 848 N.Y.S.2d 361; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13348; 2007 NY Slip Op 10531



December 26, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Matter of Seasia D. (Anonymous), 10 NY3d 785, 886 NE2d 782, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 546, 857 NYS2d 20 (2008)
Motion granted by In re Seasia D., 10 NY3d 857, 889 NE2d 497, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 1418, 859 NYS2d 618 (N.Y., May 6, 2008)
Reversed by, Remitted by Matter of Seasia D. v. Mr. & Mrs. Anonymous, 10 NY3d 879, 890 NE2d 875, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 1465, 860 NYS2d 760 (N.Y., June 3, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an adoption proceeding pursuant to Domestic Relations Law art. 7, appellants, prospective adoptive parents, an adoption agency, and the nonparty birth mother, separately sought review of an order entered by the Family Court, Queens County (New York), which, after a hearing, in effect, denied the petition and dismissed the proceeding on the basis that respondent biological father's consent was required for the adoption.


OVERVIEW: The birth mother, who was 14 years old at the time of the subject child's birth, had executed an extrajudicial consent to the adoption. The family court had determined that the birth mother's extrajudicial consent was invalid and that the biological father's consent was required because, inter alia, the child was under six months old at the time of placement. On appeal, the court affirmed and remitted. While the New York Court of Appeals had found Domestic Relations Law § 111(1)(e), regarding a biological father's consent to adoption, unconstitutional in its entirety, judicially-created interim criteria for consent had been established. Because the evidence indicated that the biological father contacted the birth mother's family about four months prior to the child's birth and acknowledged paternity, asked to be present for the child's birth, was incorrectly told that he could not commence a paternity proceeding until after the child's birth, and actually commenced a paternity proceeding under Family Ct Act § 517 and a custody proceeding within a month after the child's birth, the court held that the biological father had met the judicially-created criteria for consent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the family court's order and remitted the matter to the family court to set a date for the production of the child by the prospective adoptive parents, for a determination of where the subject child should reside pending resolution of the biological father's custody proceeding, and for a determination of the biological father's custody proceeding.


HEADNOTES

Adoption--Consent.--Biological father's consent to adoption was mandated by statutory and due process requirements (see Domestic Relations Law §111 [1] [e])--during relevant six-month period, biological father publicly acknowledged paternity and exhibited willingness to assume full parental responsibilities; however, those efforts were rebuffed by birth mother's family--since biological father was minor at time he had sexual relations with birth mother, his conduct could be classified as sexual misconduct on ground that birth mother was under 17 years old, but it could not be classified as statutory rape; thus, there was no basis to conclude that biological father's concerns were more penal than paternal--fact that biological father involved his own mother in process militated in his favor since it demonstrated public acknowledgment of paternity and intent to secure custody with her help--although paternity proceeding and custody proceeding were commenced after child was placed for adoption, biological father's conduct prior to child's placement for adoption was sufficient to establish that his consent was required for placement of child for adoption; since he was not informed of birth mother's plans or child's birth until after child was surrendered for adoption, his failure to immediately assume custody could not be considered waiver--because birth mother's medical expenses were paid by Medicaid, biological father's failure to contribute to those expenses was not determinative--in addition, relocation of biological father's family during birth mother's pregnancy could not be deemed to constitute waiver.

Adoption--Surrender of Child.--Extrajudicial surrender by birth mother, who was 14 years old at time, was invalid since she was acting under duress imposed upon her by maternal grandmother, who threatened to return her to foster care if she did not execute surrender; moreover, she did not have independent counsel, and her wishes for open adoption were not explicitly provided for.

 

526 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Christopher Anthony M.


2006-10362, (Docket Nos. NA-21035/04, NA-21036/04)


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


46 A.D.3d 896; 848 N.Y.S.2d 711; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13344; 2007 NY Slip Op 10536



December 26, 2007, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two child protective proceedings under Family Ct Act art. 10, appellant father sought review of so much of an order of disposition of the Family Court, Queens County (New York), which was entered upon a prior fact-finding order, entered upon his default, finding, inter alia, that he had abused the first child and had derivatively neglected the second child, and upon an order denying his motion for summary judgment dismissing the petitions.


OVERVIEW: While the father was making the bed in a bedroom adjoining the kitchen in his apartment, the first child crawled out of the bedroom and into the kitchen. When he heard the first child scream a few minutes later, the father ran to the kitchen where he observed a woman who also lived in the apartment putting water on the first child's face. The first child was treated for first and second degree burns. After a Family Ct Act § 1028 hearing, the family court determined that neither the father nor the mother harmed the first child as the mother was at the laundromat when the injury occurred and the woman was alone in the room with the first child when he was injured. On appeal, the court reversed the denial of summary judgment to the father. While Family Ct Act § 1046(a)(ii) permitted an inference to establish a prima facie case of abuse of neglect against the father because the first child suffered an injury which would not ordinarily occur in the absence of any act of omission of the caretakers, the evidence was sufficient to rebut the statutory inference of fault. No evidentiary proof was submitted by petitioner agency to establish the existence of a triable issue of fact.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal from the order of disposition except as it brought up for review the prior order denying summary judgment. The court reversed the order of disposition insofar as reviewed, granted the father's motion for summary judgment, dismissed the petitions as asserted against the father, modified the order denying the motion, and vacated so much of the fact-finding order and order of disposition as pertained to the father.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child--Abused or Neglected Child.--Father established that his conduct was neither negligent nor abusive--father's credible testimony, as corroborated by other evidence discovered by investigators, was sufficient to rebut statutory inference of fault against father and to establish that injury could reasonably have occurred accidentally; moreover, evidence provided sufficient factual basis for father's inability to explain how child was injured; he did not know how child was injured because he was making bed in bedroom and was not in presence of child at moment child was injured in kitchen.

 

527 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of East End Prop. Co. #1, LLC v. Kessel


2006-08816


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


46 A.D.3d 817; 851 N.Y.S.2d 565; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12832; 2007 NY Slip Op 10153



December 18, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by, Motion denied by In re East End Prop. Co. # 1, LLC v. Kessel, 10 NY3d 926, 892 NE2d 394, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 1921, 862 NYS2d 328 (2008)
Related proceeding at Matter of East End Prop. Co. # 1, LLC v. Town Bd. of Town of Brookhaven, 56 AD3d 773, 868 NYS2d 264, 2008 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9034 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, Nov. 25, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant civic associations challenged respondent Long Island Power Authority's (LIPA's) order adopting a findings statement under the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA), ECL art. 8, and authorizing agreements to construct and operate a dual-fuel, combined-cycle turbine generator. The Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), denied the petition and dismissed certain causes of act. The associations appealed.


OVERVIEW: The associations lacked standing to allege a violation of Public Authorities Law § 1020-f(aa) because their status as ratepayers did not show a sufficient potential injury in fact. The LIPA's request for proposals and memorandum of understanding for a feasibility study did not trigger SEQRA review as they did not commit the LIPA to a specific project plan. The LIPA's failure to complete parts 2 and 3 of the environmental assessment form did not nullify the entire environmental review procedure where a draft and final environmental impact statement had been compiled. The LIPA satisfied its SEQRA obligations where it thoroughly analyzed the environmental impacts, and its conclusion was supported by accepted governmental guidelines and scientific authorities. Moreover, it had not engaged in improper segmentation by failing to analyze an interstate natural gas pipeline as environmental review of that pipeline was preempted by the Federal Energy Regulatory Commission's regulatory authority. Finally, the State Finance Law § 123-b claim was properly dismissed as the conclusory allegations were insufficient to show that the challenged expenditures were an illegal use of state funds.


OUTCOME: The order and judgment were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties--Standing.--In matter challenging determinations of Long Island Power Authority (LIPA) authorizing power purchase agreement and other related agreements regarding construction and operation of turbine generator, appellants did not have standing to assert cause of action alleging violations of Public Authorities Law § 1020-f; individual appellants failed to demonstrate sufficient potential injury in fact to sustain their burden of establishing standing to challenge LIPA's power to enter into contract without approval from Public Authorities Control Board; Public Authorities Law § 1020-f (aa) mandates that "[n]otwithstanding any other provision of law to the contrary," LIPA "shall not undertake any project without the approval of the public authorities control board"; in absence of injury in fact, "zone of interest" test did not confer standing on individual appellants merely because they were customers of utility; appellant civic associations failed to allege sufficient injury in fact concerning alleged failure of LIPA to obtain approval from Public Authorities Control Board.

Environmental Conservation--Environmental Quality Review.--Challenges to determinations of Long Island Power Authority (LIPA) authorizing power purchase agreement and other related agreements regarding construction and operation of turbine generator (project) were dismissed--LIPA, as lead agency, properly commenced review pursuant to State Environmental Quality Review Act ([ECL art 8] SEQRA) before "any significant authorization [was] granted for a specific proposal" to require filing of environmental impact statement (EIS); neither issuance of request for proposals nor memorandum of understanding entered into between LIPA and gas company pursuant to which gas company would conduct feasibility study exploring possible construction of natural gas pipeline to project site committed LIPA to "specific project plan" or "definite course of future decisions"--failure of LIPA to complete Parts 2 and 3 of full environmental assessment form (EAF) did not require nullification of entire environmental review procedure--LIPA took "hard look" at potential environmental impacts of project in final EIS (FEIS), and adequately analyzed reasonable range of alternatives to project--LIPA's segmentation of natural gas pipeline from environmental review of project was not improper; although project and natural gas pipeline to supply fuel to project were part of integrated and cumulative development plan, environmental review of pipeline under SEQRA was preempted by regulatory authority of Federal Energy Regulatory Commission; LIPA was prohibited from engaging in "concurrent site-specific environmental review"--"new information" that appellants brought to attention of LIPA after issuance of FEIS was not of type that would require further environmental review of project.

Public Authorities--Long Island Power Authority--Taxpayer's Action

529 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Whitehead


2006-03438


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


46 A.D.3d 715; 848 N.Y.S.2d 657; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12586; 2007 NY Slip Op 9811



December 11, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Whitehead, 10 N.Y.3d 772, 883 N.E.2d 1269, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 945, 854 N.Y.S.2d 334 (2008)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Whitehead v. Artus, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 101374 (E.D.N.Y., Sept. 8, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following the denial of defendant's motion to suppress physical evidence, the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), convicted defendant of robbery in the first degree and robbery in the second degree (two counts), upon a jury verdict. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the denial of his motion to suppress. The appellate court concluded that the hearing court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress a handgun. Defendant, by his words and conduct, consented to the limited warrantless search conducted by the detective at his house. Thus, defendant's contention regarding whether there were exigent circumstances to justify the warrantless search were rendered academic. Viewing the evidence in the light most favorable to the prosecution, the appellate court found that it was legally sufficient to establish defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The appellate court was satisfied that the verdict of guilt was not against the weight of the evidence. Contrary to defendant's contention, he was not deprived of the effective assistance of counsel. Defendant's contention that CPL 270.10 was unconstitutional was unpreserved for appellate review under CPL 470.05(2), and, in any event, was not properly before the appellate court due to defendant's failure to notify the Attorney General that he was challenging the constitutionality of a state statute.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Unlawful Search and Seizure.--Hearing court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress handgun; defendant, by his words and conduct, consented to limited warrantless search conducted by detective at his house.

 

530 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Aspen Cr. Estates, Ltd. v. Town of Brookhaven


2006-03815


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


47 A.D.3d 267; 848 N.Y.S.2d 214; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12308; 2007 NY Slip Op 9583



December 4, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Matter of Kaywood Props. Ltd. v. Forte, 19 Misc 3d 1143A, 867 NYS2d 17, 2008 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3285 (2008)
Affirmed by Matter of Aspen Cr. Estates, Ltd. v. Town of Brookhaven, 12 NY3d 735, 904 NE2d 816, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 17, 876 NYS2d 680 (N.Y., Feb. 17, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner developer filed an action to review a determination by respondent town, made after a public hearing, to acquire the developer's property by condemnation.


OVERVIEW: The developer purchased a 39-acre parcel of farmland located in a "farmland protection area" for residential development. Thereafter, the town sought to condemn the property to preserve it as farmland and maintain open space and scenic vistas. At the public hearing the property's agricultural history was disclosed, as well as the town's unsuccessful attempts to acquire the property through a negotiated sale. The court found, inter alia, that the record fully supported the town's decision to condemn the property. The town satisfied the requirements of EDPL 202(A), which also satisfied the due process requirements of the federal and state constitutions. The developer failed to assert any significant potential for environmental harm that might result from condemnation. The town's stated reasons for acquiring the property were all legitimate public purposes. The mere fact that condemnation would provide incidental benefits to private individuals did not invalidate the town's determination since the public purpose was dominant. The developer failed to sustain its burden of establishing that the taking was excessive.


OUTCOME: The determination was confirmed, the petition was denied, and the proceeding was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Eminent Domain -- Judicial Review -- Notice of Public Hearing 

1. The determination of respondent Town to acquire by condemnation a 39-acre parcel of land located in a 500-acre "Farmland Protection Area" that petitioner had purchased for residential development was confirmed. Petitioner received adequate notice of the purpose of the proposed condemnation, which was to ensure the continuation of the property as farmland, and a reasonable opportunity to testify and present evidence at the required public hearing (see EDPL 202 [a]). The statutory scheme was not unconstitutional as applied due to the failure to afford parties adversely affected by a proposed taking the right to an adversarial hearing at which government officials could be cross-examined. The statutory procedures followed by respondent satisfied all due process requirements. Petitioner's allegations that proponents of the condemnation were biased and/or acting in bad faith did not warrant judicially transforming the public hearing required by statute into a full-fledged adversarial proceeding.

Environmental Conservation -- Environmental Quality Review -- Preservation of Farmland 

2. Respondent Town complied with the requirements of the State Environmental Quality Review Act in determining to acquire by condemnation a 39-acre parcel of land located in a 500-acre "Farmland Protection Area" that petitioner had purchased for residential development. Respondent's stated purpose in acquiring the property was to ensure its continuation as farmland. Since the acquistion would have a beneficial impact on the environment, respondent's issuance of a negative declaration with regard to any adverse environmental effects was appropriate, and the preparation of an environmental impact statement was unnecessary (see 6 NYCRR 617.7 [a] [2]).

Eminent Domain -- Public Use -- Preservation of Farmland 

3. The determination of respondent Town to acquire by condemnation a 39-acre parcel of land located in a 500-acre "Farmland Protection Area" that petitioner had purchased for residential development was confirmed. Respondent's acquisition of the property in order to preserve its use as farmland and maintain open space and scenic vistas served a legitimate public purpose, especially in view of the State's express public policy to "promote, foster, and encourage the agricultural industry" (Agriculture and Markets Law § 3) and the approval by town residents of bond acts to finance the acquisition of "open space, farmland and wildlife habitats." Petitioner failed to demonstrate that the proposed condemnation was merely a pretext for improperly conferring benefits upon private persons. The property had been continuously farmed for more than a century before petitioner purchased it for development purposes, with full knowledge that it had been targeted for preservation as farmland. Since allowing farming to continue on the property was fully consistent with the purpose of the condemnation, the fact that one or more individual farmers might benefit was merely incidental, and did not render the public benefit to be achieved by condemnation illusory.

532 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Kessler v. Hevesi


1117, 104482/05


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


45 A.D.3d 474; 846 N.Y.S.2d 56; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12069; 2007 NY Slip Op 9332



November 27, 2007, Decided 
November 27, 2007, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by Kessler v. Hevesi, 10 NY3d 849, 889 NE2d 489, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 1367, 859 NYS2d 611 (N.Y., May 1, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wireless telephone users appealed an order by the New York County Supreme Court (New York) that granted defendant State Comptroller's motion to dismiss the complaint, and denied the users' cross-motion for summary judgment in the users action to find County Law § 309 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court rejected the wireless telephone users' argument that the monthly surcharge on wireless telephones for, inter alia, enhanced 911 services was a taking because it was a user fee rather than a general tax, and because the State had not used the revenue effectively to implement enhanced 911 services. The surcharge assessed under County Law § 309 paid for services received by, and for the benefit of, the general public. The benefits flowed to the general public because everyone, not just wireless telephone users, benefited from the enhancements to 911 service. The extension to wireless telephone users of a surcharge that was initially placed on land-line telephone users to support public safety programs could hardly be said to impose an unfair burden. The users' remaining arguments were considered and found to be unavailing.


OUTCOME: The order was unanimously modified to declare County Law § 309 constitutional; as so modified, the order was otherwise affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law--Validity of Statute 

Constitutional Law--Taking of Property.--Statutory monthly surcharge on wireless telephones for enhanced 911 services is not taking, since extension to wireless telephone users of surcharge that was initially placed on land-line telephone users to support public safety programs cannot be said to impose unfair burden on few.

 

533 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Windy Ridge Farm v. Assessor of Town of Shandaken


502500


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


45 A.D.3d 1099; 845 N.Y.S.2d 861; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11711; 2007 NY Slip Op 8824



November 15, 2007, Decided 
November 15, 2007, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Windy Ridge Farm v Assessor of Town of Shandaken, 10 NY3d 706, 886 NE2d 803, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 670, 857 NYS2d 38 (2008)
Affirmed by Windy Ridge Farm v Assessor of Shandaken, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 2514 (N.Y., Aug. 28, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner owners of property brought a CPLR art. 78 action against respondent assessor, contending that the methodology employed during a specific tax year was unconstitutional. Later, the owners filed an amended petition adding respondents county and school district as parties. The Supreme Court, Ulster County (New York), dismissed the action. The owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: It was undisputed that the original petition named only the assessor as a respondent and failed to name either the county or the school district as a party. By the time an amended petition adding these entities was filed, the statute of limitations had expired. The trial court dismissed the proceeding on two distinct grounds, namely, statute of limitations and failure to join necessary parties. The appellate court found that the owners' failure to timely join the county and the school district, which were clearly necessary parties, mandated dismissal of the proceeding. The failure to name the county and the school district in the first instance was a mistake of law not encompassed by the relation back doctrine. In other words, the owners were clearly "fully aware" of the identity and existence of both the county and the school district but, apparently, failed to appreciate that these entities were legally required to be named in proceedings of this type, which was a mistake of law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions--Defendants United in Interest--Relation Back Doctrine.--Petitioners' failure to timely join County and School District, necessary parties, mandated dismissal of proceeding challenging town's assessment methodology; petitioners were not entitled to benefit of relation back doctrine since their failure to name County and School District could not be viewed as mistake as to identity of proper parties, but rather mistake of law not encompassed by doctrine.

 

534 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Uhlfelder v. Weinshall


8444, 109890/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


47 A.D.3d 169; 845 N.Y.S.2d 41; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11141; 2007 NY Slip Op 8390; 36 Media L. Rep. 1231



November 8, 2007, Decided 
November 8, 2007, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, licensed newsstand operators and a trade association, appealed an order by the New York County Supreme Court (New York) that denied their motion for a preliminary injunction and granted summary judgment to defendants, a city, its officials, and agencies, in the operators' action challenging New York City, N.Y., Loc. Laws No. 64 (2003) regarding the installation and maintenance of newsstands and the placement of advertising thereon.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Local Law 64, all newsstands in the city were to be replaced by structures to be built, owned, and maintained by a corporate franchisee. The newly constructed newsstands were to be operated by the individual licensees who owned the newsstands that were replaced. The city would receive an annual payment from the franchisee in the form of a franchise fee and from revenue received through advertising on the newsstands. The court found, inter alia, that although the city officials had discretion in determining whether a particular newsstand was in a "hazardous condition," the condition had to present a threat to public safety that was sufficiently comparable to the statutory examples. Nothing in Local Law 64 required that the advertisements it authorized be placed on the front portion of the newsstands. The operators could display a sign that they had no control over the content of the advertising panels. Neither the operators nor the trade association had standing to challenge the reimbursement requirement of Local Law 64. The plaintiffs' nonconstitutional claims were without merit or barred by the statutes of limitations.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law--Freedom of Speech--Validity of Statute Regulating Location and Operation of Sidewalk Newsstands--Preliminary Injunction

1. Plaintiffs, licensed newsstand operators within the City of New York, were not entitled to preliminary injunctive relief on First Amendment grounds preventing the City of New York from implementing a 2003 local law which required replacement of all existing newsstands with newsstands owned, constructed and maintained by a single franchisee, required that the new newsstands be designed and built in compliance with the Americans With Disabilities Act (42 USC § 12131 et seq.), and imposed siting and display space requirements for new licensees (Administrative Code of City of NY § 20-241.1). Plaintiffs failed to demonstrate that the local law would impermissibly infringe upon First Amendment rights by giving city officials unfettered discretion to issue and revoke licenses based upon an unconstitutionally vague criterion of avoiding "hazardous conditions." Rather, the term "hazardous condition" was given content by the specific and nonexclusive objective examples enumerated in the statute (see Administrative Code § 20-231 [d]). Moreover, any nonenumerated circumstance found by the Department of Transportation (DOT) to constitute a "hazardous condition" was required to present a threat to public safety that was sufficiently comparable to the statutory examples to allow courts to police DOT determinations on an "as applied" basis. Furthermore, plaintiffs raised only the mere and abstract possibility of constitutional injury to the exercise of their First Amendment rights with respect to the franchisee's placement of advertisement panels on the upper front portion of newsstands.

Parties--Standing--Validity of Statute Regulating Location and Operation of Sidewalk Newsstands--Failure to Raise Standing Issue on Appeal No Bar to Dismissal of Constitutional Claim

2. Plaintiffs, licensed newsstand operators within the City of New York, lacked standing to challenge on First Amendment grounds the implementation of portions of a 2003 local law requiring replacement of all existing newsstands with newsstands owned, constructed and maintained by a single franchisee (see Administrative Code of City of NY § 20-241.1). Plaintiffs lacked standing to challenge the reimbursement requirement of the law requiring the operator of a newsstand "that is not a replacement newsstand and that has been constructed and installed by the franchisee . . . [to] reimburse the franchisee for the cost of such construction and installation" where the individual plaintiffs would be receiving "replacement newsstands from the franchisee free of charge" (Administrative Code § 20-228 [e]), and thus could not show that they would actually be harmed by the reimbursement requirement for standing purposes. Furthermore, the trade association of licensed newsstand operators likewise lacked standing to challenge the reimbursement requirement as an "impermissible levy" on First Amendment rights, since it failed to demonstrate that any of its individual members would in fact be required to reimburse the franchisee for a "replacement newsstand." Moreover, the City's failure to pursue the standing issue on appeal did not preclude dismissal of this constitutional claim on standing grounds, since the lack of standing in the context of the constitutionality of a statute cannot ordinarily be waived by the parties.

Constitutional Law--Freedom of Speech--Validity of Statute Regulating Location and Operation of Sidewalk Newsstands

3. Plaintiffs, licensed newsstand operators within the City of New York, were not entitled to preliminary injunctive relief on First Amendment grounds preventing the City of New York from implementing a 2003 local law relating to the ownership, installation and maintenance of, and placement of advertisements on newsstands (see Administrative Code of City of NY § 20-241.1). The law required the replacement of all existing newsstands with newsstands owned, constructed and maintained by a single franchisee. Plaintiffs' claim that the local law would violate their First Amendment rights by compelling them to disseminate messages placed on the newsstands they would operate, over which they would have no control, lacked merit. Under the law, plaintiffs would not own the new newsstands or have any legally cognizable property rights in the exterior portions of the structures on which advertisements would be placed. Plaintiffs would thus have no right to control the advertising on the structures. The actual owner of the new newsstands, the franchisee, could not be required to display on its property only those messages with which the operators agreed. Moreover, there was nothing in the law that would prohibit the operator of a newsstand from using a portion of the available space in the front of the newsstand to display a sign making clear that the operator had no control over the content on the messages on the advertising panels.

536 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Airtran N.Y., LLC v. Midwest Air Group, Inc.


1220


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


46 A.D.3d 208; 844 N.Y.S.2d 233; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10907; 2007 NY Slip Op 8021



October 25, 2007, Decided 
October 25, 2007, Entered



DISPOSITION:  The trial court's judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded to set an appropriate timetable and mechanics for production.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff domestic corporation sued defendant foreign corporation in the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), to compel the foreign corporation to produce its shareholder lists under Business Corporation Law § 1315(a). The trial court denied the domestic corporation's motion to compel, and it appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held the foreign corporation did not meet the heightened standard of "doing business" applicable to Business Corporation Law § 1312. The appellate court held "doing business," under Business Corporation Law art. 13, was interpreted under two standards. A heightened standard applied to statutes raising concerns about interfering with the Commerce Clause authority reserved to Congress, and a less strict standard, in CPLR 302, applied when such concerns were not present. The standard applicable to Business Corporation Law § 1315 was the less strict standard because it raised no Commerce Clause concerns. Under this standard, the foreign corporation, through its wholly owned subsidiary and acting as its principal, did business in New York. The foreign corporation, a shell holding company, which functioned in New York through its sole operational subsidiary, was, thus, subject to § 1315's obligation to make available its shareholder list. It was unnecessary to show, in addition to dominion and control, that the subsidiary was used as an instrumentality of wrongdoing.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was reversed, and the matter was remanded to set an appropriate timetable and mechanics for production.


HEADNOTES

Corporations -- Inspection of Books and Records -- Inspection of Record of Shareholders of Foreign Corporation -- Improper Application of Heightened "Doing Business" Standard

Plaintiff, a wholly owned New York subsidiary of a foreign corporation formed in connection with an attempt by its parent company to acquire control of defendant, a Wisconsin holding company whose sole operational subsidiary was a commercial air carrier registered to do business in New York, was entitled to relief, as a shareholder, compelling defendant to produce its current shareholder lists pursuant to Business Corporation Law § 1315. Defendant was "doing business" in New York through the activities of its wholly owned subsidiary for purposes of the shareholder record inspection statute. Supreme Court, in denying plaintiff's motion to compel the production of defendant's shareholder lists, erred in determining that the statute required a higher level of "doing business" than the traditional jurisdictional standard for "doing business" (see CPLR 302) in order to avoid an unconstitutional infringement on interstate commerce. In view of the long-standing precedent requiring that the statute be liberally construed in favor of the stockholder, there was no basis for Supreme Court to apply the heightened "doing business" standard of Business Corporation Law § 1312 to Business Corporation Law § 1315, which merely provided an in-state procedural device for New York residents to obtain a shareholder list and in no way imposed an unjustified burden on interstate commerce. Supreme Court also erred in considering the availability of similar relief in Wisconsin and whether other means of communication with defendant's shareholders were available to plaintiff.

 

537 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Pendleton v. City of New York


2006-04748


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


44 A.D.3d 733; 843 N.Y.S.2d 648; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10575; 2007 NY Slip Op 7677



October 9, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, in part Pendleton v. City of New York, 2008 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 2978 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action, inter alia, to recover damages for false arrest and malicious prosecution, plaintiff sought review of an order of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which granted those branches of a cross-motion pursuant to CPLR § 3211(a)(5) to dismiss, as time barred, the fifth cause of action of the amended complaint, a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim, insofar as asserted against defendants, a city and its police department (the city).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff also sought review of the dismissal of so much of the § 1983 claim as was predicated upon claims of false arrest and false imprisonment insofar as asserted against defendant detective. Plaintiff had been arrested on, inter alia, a second degree murder charge and was incarcerated for over a year before the charges against him were dismissed. After filing an initial complaint that asserted that the city had failed to properly train and supervise its officers, plaintiff was permitted to amend to allege, inter alia, the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim that his constitutional rights were violated as a result of the city's policies and customs. The § 1983 claim against the city should not have been dismissed because the amended complaint related back under CPLR § 203(f) to the negligent hiring and training allegations in the original complaint. While the state law claims for false arrest and imprisonment had been dismissed, that did not vitiate the related allegations in the original complaint, and notice of claim requirements under General Municipal Law § 50-e did not apply to 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims. Thus, the fifth cause of action was improperly dismissed as to the detective.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order insofar as appealed from. The court denied defendants' cross-motion to dismiss, as time-barred, the fifth cause of action insofar as asserted against the city and to dismiss so much of the fifth cause of action as was predicated on claims of false arrest and false imprisonment insofar as asserted against the detective.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions--Claim in Amended Pleading.--In action to recover damages for false arrest and malicious prosecution, Civil Rights Act cause of action, insofar as asserted in amended complaint against municipal defendants and based on improper training, related back to negligent hiring and training allegations contained in original complaint (see CPLR 203 [f]), and since original complaint was filed within three-year statute of limitations applicable to causes of action pursuant to 42 USC § 1983, it was error to dismiss Civil Rights Act cause of action of amended complaint against municipal defendants; amended complaint alleged that plaintiff's rights were violated by numerous specific policies or customs of municipal defendants, all of which were tied to their alleged improper training of police employees; those allegations were amplifications of original allegation that municipal defendants failed to properly train police on "appropriate rules applicable to the duties, activities and behaviors" of police employees, and were based on same transactions and occurrences as were at issue in original negligent-training cause of action and were "mere expansion" of original allegations--with respect to so much of cause of action as was asserted against detective, Supreme Court erred in limiting it to violations based only on malicious prosecution.

 

538 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Paniagua


977-978


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


45 A.D.3d 98; 841 N.Y.S.2d 506; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9511; 2007 NY Slip Op 6578



September 6, 2007, Decided 
September 6, 2007, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Paniagua, 9 NY3d 992, 878 NE2d 1026, 2007 N.Y. LEXIS 3864, 848 NYS2d 610 (N.Y., Nov. 7, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order by the New York County Supreme Court (New York) that, inter alia, denied his petition for resentencing on his convictions for first- and second-degree criminal sale of a controlled substance under the 2005 Drug Law Reform Act (DLRA), L 2005, ch. 643.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first- and second-degree criminal sale of a controlled substance (a class A-I felony and class A-II felony, respectively). Thereafter, defendant was resentenced on the A-I count to a determinate term of 8 years, to be followed by 5 years of post-release supervision. In order to qualify for resentencing on his A-II conviction, defendant requested reinstatement of his original sentence on the A-I conviction. The appellate court found that defendant received the minimum sentence allowed under the 2004 DLRA, L 2004, ch. 738, which was precisely the sentence that he requested. Defendant failed to meet the "eligibility requirements" for receiving merit time set forth in Correction Law § 803(1)(d) because he committed two serious disciplinary infractions while incarcerated. Consequently, he did not qualify for resentencing under the 2005 DLRA, L 2005, ch. 643. Defendant's other contentions were without merit.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sentence -- Drug Law Reform Act -- Failure to "Specify and Inform" Defendant of Proposed Sentence

1. Defendant, having been resentenced in July 2005 on his 2002 class A-I felony drug conviction to the minimum sentence allowed under the 2004 Drug Law Reform Act (DLRA) (L 2004, ch 738, § 23; Penal Law § 70.71), was not entitled to have the resentence vacated on the ground that the resentencing court did not "specify and inform" him of the proposed sentence as required by the 2004 DLRA so that he could obtain the temporary reinstatement of his original sentence on his class A-I felony for the purpose of becoming eligible for resentencing on his class A-II felony conviction under the 2005 DLRA (L 2005, ch 643) to the same sentence previously imposed by the resentencing court. Where, as here, the resentencing court imposes a sentence that is more favorable than the one originally imposed, the imposition of that sentence without first "specifying and informing" the defendant of the proposed sentence, even if a technical violation of the statute, does not entitle the defendant to any relief on appeal.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Drug Law Reform Act -- "Eligibility Requirements" for Resentencing

2. Defendant, who was convicted in October 2002 of both class A-I and A-II drug felonies and was resentenced on the class A-I felony to the minimum sentence allowed under the 2004 Drug Law Reform Act (DLRA) (L 2004, ch 738, § 23; Penal Law § 70.71), was properly denied resentencing on his A-II conviction under the 2005 DLRA (L 2005, ch 643), since he did not meet the "eligibility requirements" for resentencing under that statute (see Correction Law § 851 [2]). Although defendant was more than three years from being parole eligible at the time he filed his second resentencing motion, he failed to meet a second prerequisite for resentencing since he was disqualified from obtaining a merit time allowance by virtue of having committed two serious disciplinary infractions while incarcerated (see Correction Law § 803 [1] [d]; 7 NYCRR 280.2 [b] [3]). Defendant's argument that the phrase "eligibility requirements" referred only to the requirements for earning a merit time allowance, and not also to those for being granted one, lacked merit. Further more, application of the statutory scheme did not violate defendant's equal protection or due process rights. The disparate treatment of A-II felons depending on their disciplinary record and other behavior while incarcerated is rationally related to the valid state objective of granting early release from prison and early termination of parole supervision to those defendants best prepared for reentry into society. Nor was the statutory scheme rendered irrational because certain defendants, like defendant, would be able to petition the courts to convert their indeterminate sentences to a determinate term on their class A-I convictions, but unable to obtain relief from their lifetime terms on their class A-II felony convictions.

 

539 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Vil. of Chestnut Ridge v. Town of Ramapo


2005-08757


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


45 A.D.3d 74; 841 N.Y.S.2d 321; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9059; 2007 NY Slip Op 6434



August 14, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Remanded by Vill. of Chestnut Ridge v. Town of Ramapo, 2008 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 76881 (S.D.N.Y., Sept. 30, 2008)
Subsequent appeal at, Dismissed by Matter of Village of Chestnut Ridge v. Town of Ramapo, 12 N.Y.3d 793, 906 N.E.2d 1072, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 639, 879 N.Y.S.2d 38 (2009)
Subsequent appeal at, Dismissed by Matter of Village of Chestnut Ridge v. Town of Ramapo, 12 N.Y.3d 793, 906 N.E.2d 1072, 2009 N.Y. LEXIS 634, 879 N.Y.S.2d 39 (2009)
Subsequent appeal at, Leave to appeal dismissed by Matter of Village of Chestnut Ridge v. Town of Ramapo, 15 N.Y.3d 817, 934 N.E.2d 882, 2010 N.Y. LEXIS 2261, 908 N.Y.S.2d 149 (2010)
Related proceeding at Village of Wesley Hills v. Planning Bd. of the Town of Ramapo, 32 Misc. 3d 1216A, 2011 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3545 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, four villages and two residents, appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), which granted the cross-motions to dismiss filed by respondents, a town, its town board, planning board, and board of appeals (the town). Petitioners had brought suit, pursuant to CPLR art. 78, challenging the town's approval of a resolution and a site plan permitting adult student housing.


OVERVIEW: The litigation arose from the town's amendment of its zoning code to permit adult student living facilities. Only four sites in the town were at issue, with each adjacent or near one or more of the villages. The court held that the villages had standing to assert their claims and that the bar to judicial review of zoning changes by adjoining municipalities established by Town Law § 264(4) did not apply to challenges under the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA), ECL 8-0101 et seq. The court held that the right of a municipality, such as the four villages, to challenge the acts of its neighbors must be determined on the basis of the same rules of standing that applied to other litigants. The court held that the residents also had standing to make similar challenges, but had no standing to challenge the town's approval of a site plan for the construction of one of the adult student housing developments. However, the residents did have standing to challenge the town's alleged failure to comply with the procedural particulars required for a zoning change since they owned property in close proximity to the area subject to the zoning change, but the four villages did not.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment granting respondents' cross-motions to dismiss and held that the four villages had standing to sue on the claims asserted except the challenges to the alleged procedural irregularities. Likewise, the residents had standing to assert some, but not all, of their claims. The court reinstated the case and remitted it to the trial court for a determination, on the merits, of the claims that the court reinstated.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Capacity to Sue -- Challenge by Adjoining Municipalities to Zoning Change Adopted by Town by Local Law

1. Petitioner Villages had the legal capacity in a hybrid CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action to challenge on environmental and certain other grounds respondent Town's adoption of a local law that permitted adult student living facilities as a conditional use in certain residential zones. Petitioners were expressly authorized "to sue and be sued" in "any action or proceeding in any court" (Village Law § 1-102 [5]) and needed no other specific authorization to challenge the adoption of the adult student housing law. Petitioners' ability to sue was not limited by the rule that a municipality, as a creature of the State, may not sue the State, since that rule did not bar an action by one creature of the State against another. Furthermore, Town Law § 264 (4), which provided that a village, although entitled to be heard at a public hearing held by a neighboring town with respect to a proposed zoning change, had no right to seek judicial review of the town's zoning decision, did not otherwise deprive petitioners of the capacity to sue, since the challenged zoning change was the product of a local law, not a zoning enactment. The bar to judicial review of zoning changes by adjoining municipalities did not preclude all statutory claims by a municipality with respect to a zoning enactment of a neighboring municipality, without regard to the source of the authority by which the action was taken. Nor did the statutory bar apply to challenges under the State Environmental Quality Review Act, which contemplated that an adjoining municipality that had a concern about an action had the same right to judicial review as any other "interested" party (see 6 NYCRR 617.2 [s], [t]).

Parties -- Standing -- Zoning Changes -- Adoption of Adult Student Housing Law -- Environmental Review Process

2. Petitioners, as owners of property in close proximity to the area subject to a zoning change, had standing in a hybrid CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action to challenge on environmental and certain other grounds respondent Town's adoption of a local law and revised comprehensive zoning law that permitted adult student living facilities as a conditional use in certain residential zones. Petitioners had an interest in assuring that respondent complied with notice, hearing, voting, filing and publication requirements for the enactment of a local law (see Municipal Home Rule Law § 20). Petitioners also had standing to challenge the adult student housing law as being inconsistent with the Town's comprehensive zoning plan, thereby constituting impermissible spot zoning, and also to assert that respondent failed to comply with statutory regional land use review requirements (see General Municipal Law § 239-m). Furthermore, petitioners had standing to challenge the adoption of the local law as being violative of the State Environmental Quality Review Act, since it could be inferred from their close proximity to the proposed site of an adult student living facility that they would suffer environmental injury different from that of the public at large. However, petitioners, a married couple, lacked standing to raise equal protection or due process claims based upon the exclusion of unmarried adult student housing from the new zoning district.

Parties -- Standing -- Standing of Villages to Challenge Town's Adoption of Adult Student Housing Law -- Compliance with Regional Land Use and Environmental Review Processes

3. Petitioner Villages lacked standing in a hybrid CPLR article 78 proceeding and declaratory judgment action to challenge respondent Town's adoption of a local law and revised comprehensive zoning law that permitted adult student living facilities as a conditional use in certain residential zones on the ground that respondent failed to comply with notice, hearing, voting, filing and publication requirements for the enactment of a local law (see Municipal Home Rule Law § 20). Petitioners had no interest in the respondent's compliance with either its comprehensive plan or the procedural requirements regarding the enactment of a local law. Since petitioners exercised their own zoning authority with respect to property located within their geographical jurisdiction (see Village Law § 7-700), they were beyond the bounds of the mutuality of restriction and benefit underlying the comprehensive plan requirement. Petitioners also lacked standing to raise equal protection or due process claims based upon the exclusion of unmarried adult student housing from the new zoning district. However, petitioners, as adjoining municipalities, had standing to assert that respondent failed to comply with statutory regional land use review requirements (see General Municipal Law § 239-m). Furthermore, petitioners established standing to seek judicial review of the negative declaration under the State Environmental Quality Review Act (SEQRA) that resulted in the law's adoption, where they sufficiently demonstrated that the development permitted by the adult student housing law could have a substantial detrimental effect on the character of their communities, protected by SEQRA, which specifically defined "environment" as including "existing community or neighborhood character" (ECL 8-0105 [6]; 6 NYCRR 617.7 [c] [1] [iv], [v]).

 

540 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


St. Joseph Hosp. v. Novello


799.4 CA 07-00587


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


43 A.D.3d 139; 840 N.Y.S.2d 263; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8546; 2007 NY Slip Op 6090



July 18, 2007, Decided 
July 18, 2007, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal dismissed by St. Joseph Hosp. v. Novello, 9 NY3d 988, 878 NE2d 606, 2007 N.Y. LEXIS 3692, 848 NYS2d 22 (2007)
Appeal denied by St. Joseph Hosp. v. Novello, 10 NY3d 702, 883 NE2d 371, 2008 N.Y. LEXIS 197, 853 NYS2d 544 (N.Y., Feb. 12, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hospital appealed an order and judgment by the Erie County Supreme Court (New York) that denied its motion for summary judgment, granted defendant health commission's cross-motion for summary judgment, declared that L 2005 ch. 63, § 31(E) (the enabling legislation) was constitutional, and dismissed the hospital's amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: The purpose of the enabling legislation was for the commission to examine the current supply of hospital and nursing home facilities and to reconfigure the supply to align with the demand or need. The hospital commenced an action seeking injunctive, declaratory, and other relief to prevent its closure. The appellate court found that although the hospital had a protected property interest in its operating certificate, the enabling legislation satisfied the minimum procedural and substantive due process requirements. The hospital was given the opportunity to appear at a public hearing and to submit various documents. The commission's recommendation was not without legal justification, nor was it outrageously arbitrary. Even if the legislative veto provision was unconstitutional, it was severable. The hospital failed to raise an issue of fact whether the enabling legislation was an unreasonable interference with religious freedom. In addition, the enabling legislation did not violate the Federal Contract Clause or the state Presentment Clause, N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 7. Nevertheless, the trial court erred in dismissing the hospital's action seeking a declaratory judgment.


OUTCOME: The order and judgment were modified by vacating the provision dismissing the cause of action seeking a declaratory judgment; and as so modified, the order and judgment were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission 

1. The statute creating the New York State Commission on Health Care Facilities in the 21st Century (Commission), which was charged with review of the State's health care delivery system infrastructure and authorized to recommend hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations based on consideration of nine specific factors, and requiring the Health Commissioner to implement the Commission's recommendations unless those recommendations were rejected by the Legislature (L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) did not violate plaintiff health care providers' substantive or procedural due process rights. Plaintiffs were afforded procedural due process by having been given the opportunity to appear before the Commission's regional advisory committee at a public hearing and to submit unlimited documents. Furthermore, the Commission's recommendation to close plaintiffs' health care facility did not deprive plaintiffs of their right to substantive due process. Since the enabling legislation contemplated that some facilities should be closed and others resized, consolidated, converted or restructured, it could not be said that the Commission's action in recommending the closure of plaintiffs' facility was without legal justification or "outrageously arbitrary."

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission -- Severability of Legislative Veto Provision 

2. The statute creating the New York State Commission on Health Care Facilities in the 21st Century (Commission), which was charged with review of the State's health care delivery system infrastructure and authorized to recommend hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations based on consideration of nine specific factors, and requiring the Health Commissioner to implement the Commission's recommendations unless those recommendations were rejected by the Legislature (L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) is not un constitutional under the Presentment Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art IV, § 7) for failing to provide for the enactment of legislation with presentment to the Governor. Even if the legislative veto is unconstitutional, that provision of the enabling statute may be severed from the other substantive provisions of the statute in view of the statute's comprehensive severance clause and the absence of strong evidence that the Legislature would not have enacted the statute without the legislative veto. In view of the Legislature's specific directives to the Commission and appointments to the Commission, it was not inconceivable that the Legislature intended the Commission to make its recommendations without those recommendations being subject to subsequent legislative review.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Religion -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission

3. The statute creating the New York State Commission on Health Care Facilities in the 21st Century (Commission), which was charged with review of the State's health care delivery system infrastructure and authorized to recommend hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations based on consideration of nine specific factors, and requiring the Health Commissioner to implement the Commission's recommendations unless those recommendations were rejected by the Legislature (L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) did not unconstitutionally interfere with religious freedom. The Commission's recommendation that plaintiffs' religiously affiliated health care facility be closed as part of the Commission's comprehensive review of the State's health care delivery system infrastructure (see L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) did not violate the Free Exercise Clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art I, §3). The enabling legislation did not target any religious hospitals for closing, and there was nothing in the legislation itself that imposed any restrictions on religious freedom.

Constitutional Law -- Contract Clause -- Hospital Closings Recommended by State Commission 

4. The statute creating the New York State Commission on Health Care Facilities in the 21st Century (Commission), which was charged with review of the State's health care delivery system infrastructure and authorized to recommend hospital and nursing home closings and reorganizations based on consideration of nine specific factors, and requiring the Health Commissioner to implement the Commission's recommendations unless those recommendations were rejected by the Legislature (L 2005, ch 63, part E, § 31) did not violate the Contract Clause (US Const, art I, § 10 [1]) with respect to the Commission's recommendation to close plaintiffs' health care facility. The substantial impairment of plaintiffs' contracts with various vendors, suppliers and employees did not violate the Contract Clause in view of the statute's legitimate public purpose. The task of the Commission in recommending facilities to close, downsize or consolidate was both necessary and reasonable to accomplish that purpose.

 

541 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


New York State Assn. of Small City School Dists., Inc. v. State of New York


501998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


42 A.D.3d 648; 840 N.Y.S.2d 179; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8139; 2007 NY Slip Op 5822



July 5, 2007, Decided 
July 5, 2007, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an association of small school districts, several of the member districts, and others, filed suit, challenging the constitutionality of the education funding system utilized in New York State. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), concluded that only the individually named parent and student plaintiffs had standing to bring the action, and then, dismissed the amended complaint on the merits. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The association lacked standing to bring the action. Where no member of the association had the substantive right to bring suit against the State (local governmental corporate entities and their officers lacked capacity to mount constitutional challenges to acts of the State and State legislation), the association could not rely on its interests to predicate its right to bring the action. Also, the allegations of under funding in the complaint fell outside the scope of State Finance Law § 123-b. Additionally, the individual school board members failed to assert "proof of special harm different in kind or degree from the community in general" and, thus, they were not eligible for common-law taxpayer standing. Finally, plaintiffs' N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 1 claim was properly dismissed. Although district-wide deficiencies were alleged in plaintiffs' complaint, such allegations were premised on accumulated data. Without information regarding whether plaintiffs were actually experiencing the problems reflected by the aggregate statistics, it was impossible to determine whether plaintiffs were actually aggrieved.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the supreme court was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties--Standing--Capacity to Sue--Challenge to Education Funding System.--Plaintiff Association lacked standing to bring action challenging constitutionality of State's education funding system--Association's individual member school districts lacked capacity, and Association's apparent capacity to sue as private entity did not afford it capacity to bring instant suit on behalf of its members; even if Association had legal capacity to bring instant suit, it lacked organizational standing.

Parties--Standing--Citizen Taxpayer Standing--Challenge to Education Funding System.--Individual school board members lacked standing pursuant to State Finance Law § 123-b to bring action challenging constitutionality of State's education funding system; allegation of under funding fell outside scope of section 123-b, which offers means for citizen taxpayers to challenge illegal or improper disposition of state funds or property, but provides no avenue for taxpayers seeking allocation of additional funds--nor did individual school board members have common-law standing as individual taxpayers; State's action was not insulated from judicial scrutiny since parents and students of individual school districts can challenge constitutionally of their school's funding; in any event, individual school board members failed to assert proof of special harm different in kind or degree from community in general and, thus, they were not eligible for common-law taxpayer standing.

Schools--State Aid to School Districts.--No cause of action was stated in action challenging constitutionality of State's education funding system, seeking declaration that represented school districts were so substantially underfunded that they were unable to provide sound basic education to students as required by NY Constitution, article XI, § 1--while amended complaint alleged deficiencies in quality of teaching, facilities and resources in small city school districts, that these deficiencies had resulted in poor outputs and that such failings were caused by funding of small city schools at lower per-student rate than noncity schools, it failed to include any factual allegations specific to school districts at issue.

McKinney v. Commissioner of the N.Y. State Dept. of Health

People v. Tucker


1330-1331, Ind. 3616/04


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


41 A.D.3d 210; 839 N.Y.S.2d 15; 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7360; 2007 NY Slip Op 5318



June 14, 2007, Decided 
June 14, 2007, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by People v. Tucker, 9 N.Y.3d 882, 874 N.E.2d 761, 2007 N.Y. LEXIS 2873, 842 N.Y.S.2d 794 (2007)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Tucker v. New York, 128 S. Ct. 1094, 169 L. Ed. 2d 829, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 851 (U.S., 2008)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Tucker v. Marshall, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 76704 (S.D.N.Y., Aug. 27, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted, after a jury trial, in the Supreme Court, New York County (New York) of robbery in the third degree, pursuant to Penal Law § 160.00. The trial court sentenced defendant, as a persistent felony offender, to a term of 15 years to life. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The evidence showed that defendant pushed the victim down as he stole money from the victim's pocket. Defendant then fled into a nearby hotel where, while still in possession of the victim's property, he struggled with other persons. On appeal, the court found that the prosecutor did not add a new theory of robbery when he argued in summation that defendant's use of force included his efforts to forcibly retain the property immediately after taking it, and the prosecutor was entitled to argue that the requisite element of physical force was established as the two uses of force by defendant constituted an unbroken chain of events in the robbery. Further, the trial court properly (1) declined to submit petit larceny as a lesser included offense; (2) declined to reopen the suppression hearing, under CPL 710.40(4), based upon trial testimony by a hotel security guard that allegedly conflicted with hearing testimony by the police; and (3) sentenced defendant as a persistent felony offender, under CPL 400.20(9), as the court based the sentence solely on prior convictions, facts found by the jury, and the court's discretionary evaluation of the seriousness of defendant's criminal history.


OUTCOME: The conviction and sentence were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Argument and Conduct of Counsel.--Prosecutor did not add new theory of robbery when he argued that defendant's use of force included his efforts to forcibly retain property immediately after taking it; People were entitled to argue that requisite element of physical force was established both by defendant having pushed victim as he stole his money, as well as by defendant's physical resistance to being held, thus endeavoring to retain possession of stolen property; two uses of force constituted unbroken chain of events completing robbery.

Crimes--Lesser Included Offense

Crimes--Suppression Hearing

Crimes--Sentence--Persistent Violent Felony Offender
