Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 6 NY3d 878, 850 NE2d 21, 817 NYS2d 207, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1283 (2006)
Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 6 NY3d 880, 850 NE2d 23, 817 NYS2d 209, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1281 (2006)
Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 6 NY3d 877, 850 NE2d 21, 817 NYS2d 206, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1269 (2006)
Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 6 NY3d 879, 850 NE2d 23, 817 NYS2d 208, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1261 (2006)
Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 6 NY3d 877, 850 NE2d 20,, 817 NYS2d 206 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1280 (2006)
Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 6 NY3d 879, 850 NE2d 22, 817 NYS2d 208, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1247 (2006)
Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 7 NY3d 728, 850 NE2d 1158, 818 NYS2d 183, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1426 (2006)
Motion granted by Hernandez v. Robles, 7 NY3d 728, 850 NE2d 1159, 818 NYS2d 184, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1419 (2006)
Affirmed by Hernandez v. Robles, 7 NY3d 338, 855 NE2d 1, 821 NYS2d 770, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1836 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city clerk appealed a grant of summary judgment in favor of plaintiff same-sex couples by the New York County Supreme Court (New York); the trial court declared, inter alia, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 10, 12, 15(a) violated N.Y. Const. art. 1, §§ 6, 11 and permanently enjoined the clerk from denying marriage licenses to the couples.


OVERVIEW: The city clerk denied the same-sex couples' applications for marriage licenses on the ground that state law did not authorize him to do so. The motion court held, inter alia, that the Domestic Relations Laws (DRL) violated the equal protection and due process provisions of the New York State Constitution and that the words "husband," "wife," "bride," and "groom" applied equally to men and women. The appellate court held that the motion court erred in granting summary judgment to the couples and in finding the provisions of the DRL unconstitutional to the extent that they did not permit same-sex marriage. The relevant provisions of the DRL (N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 10, 12, 15(a)) clearly did not contemplate such unions. The definition of marriage in the DRL expressed an important, long-recognized public policy supporting, among other things, procreation, child welfare, and social stability--all legitimate state interests. The motion court's revision of statutory language impermissibly replaced the legislative intent with that of the court. The DRL provisions regarding marriage did not violate the due process and equal protection provisions of N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 6, 11.


OUTCOME: The grant of summary judgment was vacated, the same sex couples' motion for summary judgment was denied, the city clerk's cross-motion for summary judgment was granted, and a declaration was issued that the relevant sections of the Domestic Relations Law were constitutional and valid.


HEADNOTES

Marriage -- Same Sex Marriage -- Constitutionality of Marriage Laws 

New York's marriage laws (see Domestic Relations Law §§ 10, 12, 15 [1] [a]) are not unconstitutional to the extent that they do not permit same-sex marriages and do not violate the due process and equal protection provisions of the New York State Constitution. Supreme Court thus erred in declaring the statutory scheme unconstitutional and creating a new constitutional right to same-sex marriage, thereby impermissibly intruding upon the legislative domain. The power to regulate marriage and make policy decisions as to which type of family unit works best for society lies with the Legislature, not the judiciary. Plaintiffs failed to demonstrate that the reservation of marriage to heterosexual couples violates their rights to due process and equal protection. New York's marriage laws are rationally related to the legitimate state interest in fostering traditional heterosexual marriage as the social institution that best forges a linkage between sex, procreation and child rearing.

 

592 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Baby Boy C. v. Tohono O'odham Nation


6451


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


27 A.D.3d 34; 805 N.Y.S.2d 313; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13717; 2005 NY Slip Op 9259



December 6, 2005, Decided 
December 6, 2005, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Counsel Amended January 19, 2006.
Reargument denied by In re C. v Tohono O'Odham Nation - St. Regis Mohawk Tribe, 2006 NY App Div LEXIS 4699 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Apr. 18, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an Indian Tribe, sought review of an order from the Family Court, New York County (New York), which adopted the "existing Indian family" (EIF) exception to the Indian Child Welfare Act of 1978, 25 U.S.C.S. § 1901 et seq., and then denied the Tribe's motion to intervene in an adoption proceeding. The family court directed the adoption in favor of respondent adoptive parents to proceed.


OVERVIEW: The child's mother was a member of the Tribe, and she and the father executed consents in Arizona for termination of their parental rights and to adoption by the qualified adoptive parents. The Tribe sought to intervene in the adoption proceeding. The family court denied intervention under the Act and adopted the judicially created EIF exception, based on its finding that the child was not part of an EIF. The family court determined that the EIF exception was necessary in order to uphold the constitutionality of the Act where the child and his family lacked significant ties to a particular tribe. On appeal, the court held that the EIF exception was in direct conflict with the express language and purpose of the Act, as well as the rationale of the U.S. Supreme Court's judicial precedent. Accordingly, it declined to accept that exception as the law in New York. The court also held that although the Act was generally applicable to the proceeding, intervention by the Tribe was not expressly authorized in adoption proceedings as a matter of right under 25 U.S.C.S. § 1911(c). However, due to the Tribe's significant interest, permission to intervene was proper under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 1013.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the family court by holding that the EIF exception was not the applicable law of New York, and it granted the Tribe's motion to intervene in the proceeding. The matter was remanded for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Private Placement Adoption -- Adoption of "Indian Child" -- Applicability of Indian Child Welfare Act of 1978--Rejection of "Existing Indian Family" Exception

1. The Indian Child Welfare Act of 1978 (ICWA) (25 USC § 1901 et seq.) generally applied to a private placement adoption proceeding involving an "Indian child" born of a one-half Native American Indian mother and a non-Indian father, irrespective of whether the child or his parents had significant connections to his mother's tribe. Family Court, in declining to apply the ICWA and rejecting the tribe's motion to intervene as a matter of right, erred in adopting the judicially created "existing Indian family" (EIF) exception to the ICWA. That exception precludes application of the ICWA when an Indian child is not being removed from an existing Indian family or environment. Acceptance of the EIF exception would be fundamentally inconsistent with both the plain language of the ICWA (see 25 USC § 1903 [1]), and its goal of preserving and protecting the interests of Indian tribes in their children. Furthermore, application of the ICWA without the EIF exception abridges no fundamental constitutional right, especially in view of the statutory "good cause" exception (25 USC § 1915) that provides state courts with the flexibility to deviate from the statute's placement preferences in circumstances where the interests of the parent or child outweigh the tribe's interest in the strict application of those preferences.

Parties -- Intervention -- Right of Indian Tribe to Intervene in Adoption Proceeding Involving "Indian Child"

2. Although the Indian Child Welfare Act of 1978 (ICWA) (25 USC § 1901 et seq.) applied to a private placement adoption proceeding involving an "Indian child" born of a one-half Native American Indian mother and a non-Indian father, irrespective of whether the child or his parents had significant connections to his mother's tribe, the statute did not afford the tribe standing to intervene in the proceeding as a matter of right. The ICWA expressly permits tribal intervention in foster care placement and of termination of parental rights proceedings, but does not similarly authorize tribal intervention in adoption and preadoptive placement proceedings (25 USC § 1911 [c]).

Parties -- Intervention -- Right of Indian Tribe to Intervene in Adoption Proceeding Involving "Indian Child"

3. Although an Indian tribe was not entitled to intervene as of right in an adoption proceeding involving an "Indian child" under the Indian Child Welfare Act of 1978 (ICWA) (25 USC § 1901 et seq.), Family Court erred in denying the tribe's motion to intervene pursuant to CPLR 1013. The tribe should have been permitted to intervene pursuant to CPLR 1013 since it had a valid interest in presenting its placement preferences (see 25 USC § 1915 [a]), and otherwise assuring that the adoption proceeding was conducted in accordance with the ICWA.

Adoption -- Private Placement Adoption -- Adoption of "Indian Child"

4. In a private placement adoption proceeding involving an "Indian child", Family Court's error in applying an "existing Indian family" (EIF) exception to the Indian Child Welfare Act of 1978 (ICWA) (25 USC § 1901et seq.) to deny intervention could not be considered harmless where the tribe was permitted to intervene only for the limited purpose of the EIF hearing. Application of the ICWA would not have been "useless" merely because the mother threatened to revoke her consent and retain custody if the adoption by the prospective adoptive parents failed, since a parent might ultimately change his or her mind and since application of ICWA protects the important interests of the child and tribe during pendency of the proceeding.

 

593 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brown


1303 KA 00-01807


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FOURTH DEPARTMENT


23 A.D.3d 1090; 804 N.Y.S.2d 209; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 12867; 2005 NY Slip Op 8491



November 10, 2005, Decided 
November 10, 2005, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal denied by People v. Brown, 6 N.Y.3d 810, 845 N.E.2d 1280, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 722, 812 N.Y.S.2d 449 (2006)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Brown v. Graham, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 33640 (W.D.N.Y., Apr. 6, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court, Monroe County (New York), convicting him, upon a jury verdict, of murder in the second degree (N.Y. Penal Law § 125.25(2)), criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree (N.Y. Penal Law § 265.03(2)), and criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree (N.Y. Penal Law § 265.02(4)).


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the evidence was legally insufficient to support the conviction of depraved indifference murder and that the verdict was against the weight of the evidence with respect to depraved indifference murder. The appeals court disagreed. The jury was entitled to discredit defendant's testimony that intruders entered his home and shot the victim, who was defendant's close friend, particularly in view of the fact that defendant admitted to the police that he owned the gun used in the shooting. Defendant further contended that the Supreme Court erred in failing to charge manslaughter in the second degree as a lesser included offense of murder in the second degree, but defendant failed to preserve his contention for review. The appeals court agreed with defendant that the trial court erred in permitting the People to impeach their own witness on redirect examination of that witness. However, that error was harmless. The trial court did not err in refusing to suppress statements defendant made to the police before receiving his Miranda warnings. His statements were not the product of interrogation, and thus the court properly refused to suppress them.


OUTCOME: The judgment was unanimously affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - Murder--Depraved Indifference Murder.--Evidence was legally sufficient to support conviction of depraved indifference murder--jury was entitled to discredit testimony of defendant that intruders entered his home and shot victim, who was defendant's close friend, particularly in view of fact that defendant admitted to police that he owned gun used in shooting; People presented evidence establishing that bullet responsible for victim's fatal injuries took highly unusual route, entering chest cavity of victim through his forearm.

Crimes--Witnesses--Impeachment of Own Witness.--Court erred in permitting People to impeach their own witness on redirect examination of that witness; although pursuant to CPL 60.35 (1) People may impeach their own witness with prior inconsistent statements, testimony sought to be impeached must be elicited during direct examination by prosecutor; however, error was harmless. .Crimes--Confession

Crimes--Instructions.--It was not error to deny defendant's request to include concept of "moral certainty" in circumstantial evidence charge.

 

594 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Matter of Hebel v. West


97432, 97433


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


25 A.D.3d 172; 803 N.Y.S.2d 242; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11467; 2005 NY Slip Op 7887



October 27, 2005, Decided 
October 27, 2005, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal denied by Hebel v. West, 7 NY3d 706, 868 NE2d 662, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 2087, 837 NYS2d 1 (N.Y., July 6, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee village resident filed two N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceedings in the Supreme Court, Ulster County (New York), to enjoin appellants, the village mayor and two village marriage officers, from solemnizing marriages without duly issued marriage licenses. The trial court denied the mayor's motion to dismiss one proceeding, issued a temporary restraining order in one proceeding, and granted the applications. The mayor and the officers appealed.


OVERVIEW: When same-sex couples requested marriage licenses from the town clerk, under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §§ 13, 14, and 15, the clerk refused to issue the licenses. The mayor then drafted his own affidavit of marriage and contract of marriage, and used those documents instead of duly issued licenses to solemnize the couples' marriages in a ceremony. The mayor also had the marriage officers appointed to solemnize same-sex marriages. The resident asserted that the mayor and the marriage officers could not violate the New York Domestic Relations Law and legally marry a couple without a license duly issued by the town clerk. On appeal, the court found that (1) the mayor, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5701(b), could not appeal the denial of his motion to dismiss; (2) the resident had standing to bring the action because in matters of great public interest a citizen could maintain a mandamus proceeding to compel a public officer to do his or her duty; (3) the mayor was without authority under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 17 to perform the marriages without duly issued licenses; and (4) the mayor, under N.Y. Const. art. XIII, § 1, should have sought judicial intervention before disregarding the marriage laws.


OUTCOME: The appeal of the interlocutory order was dismissed. The final judgments in the two proceedings were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing -- Enjoining Performance of Same Sex Marriages -- Public Significance of Local Official's Actions

Petitioner had standing, as a village resident and a member of the village's Board of Trustees, to maintain CPLR article 78 proceedings seeking to permanently enjoin respondent Mayor or any designated marriage officer from performing unlicensed marriages. Respondent, after determining that he was "constitutionally prohibited" from refusing to solemnize same-sex marriages, drafted his own set of documents for licensing marriages. Petitioner had standing to seek preventive relief in view of the public significance of respondent's unprecedented action, and the fact that respondent's conduct was "in contravention of a clear mandate of law" that a marriage be performed only where a marriage license has been issued (Domestic Relations Law § 17). The controlling statutes afford a local official no discretion in determining whether a marriage license should be issued or in solemnizing a marriage in which no valid license has been issued. Rather, the law unequivocally provides that it is unlawful to knowingly solemnize a marriage where no proper license has been issued.

Marriage -- Same Sex Marriage -- Village Mayor's Improper Solemnization of Same Sex Marriages

Respondent Mayor was permanently enjoined from performing same-sex marriages. Respondent clearly exceeded his role as a village mayor and violated the separation of powers doctrine by determining that he was "constitutionally prohibited" from refusing to solemnize same-sex marriages and then drafting his own set of documents for licensing marriages. The controlling statutes (see Domestic Relations Law §§ 13-15, 17) afford a local official no discretion in determining whether a marriage license should be issued or in solemnizing a marriage in which no valid license has been issued. Moreover, respondent's acts did not fall within the scope of his constitutional oath of office (see NY Const, art XIII, § 1). A local official generally does not have the authority to refuse to comply with a ministerial duty imposed by statute on the basis of the official's opinion that the statute is unconstitutional. 

 

595 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


Langan v. St. Vincent's Hosp. of N. Y.


2003-04702


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


25 A.D.3d 90; 802 N.Y.S.2d 476; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10922; 2005 NY Slip Op 7495



October 11, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal dismissed by Langan v. St. Vincent's Hosp., 6 N.Y.3d 890, 850 N.E.2d 672, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 1214, 817 N.Y.S.2d 625 (2006)
Related proceeding at Matter of Langan v. State Farm Fire & Cas., 48 A.D.3d 76, 849 N.Y.S.2d 105, 2007 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 13242 (N.Y. App. Div. 3d Dep't, 2007)
Related proceeding at Langan v. St. Vincent's Hosp. of N.Y., 64 A.D.3d 632, 882 N.Y.S.2d 500, 2009 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5682 (N.Y. App. Div. 2d Dep't, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant hospital appealed an order of the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which denied that the hospital's motion to dismiss a wrongful death suit filed by plaintiff same-sex partner, and granted the partner's cross-motion for summary judgment as to his standing to recover damages for wrongful death.


OVERVIEW: The partner argued that he and the decedent had entered into a civil union in Vermont and that precluding same-sex spouses as potential wrongful death distributees violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the federal and state constitutions. The appellate court held that the partner failed to show that the denial of wrongful death benefits to same-sex couples did not serve a legitimate governmental purpose. Confining marriage and all laws pertaining to the marital relationship to heterosexual couples did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of either the federal or state constitutions. The fact that since the perfection of the appeal, Massachusetts had judicially created such a right for its citizens was of no moment since the partner and the decedent were married in Vermont. Neither Vermont nor the couple equated civil union relationships with heterosexual marriage. The decedent did not state that he was married when he entered the hospital. Moreover, the partner did not state that he was married in the probate papers. The Full Faith and Credit and comity theories did not apply. Only the legislature was empowered to act on the issue of same-sex marriages.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Death -- Wrongful Death -- Standing of Same-Sex Life Partner to Maintain Wrongful Death Action 

Plaintiff, the same-sex life partner of decedent pursuant to a civil union formalized under the laws of Vermont, lacked standing to maintain an action to recover damages for decedent's wrongful death. Under New York law, plaintiff was not a "surviving spouse" entitled to maintain a wrongful death action. Plaintiff failed to establish that application of the wrongful death statute (EPTL 5-4.1) in such a manner as to preclude same-sex spouses as potential distributees is violative of equal protection. Plaintiff failed to demonstrate that the denial of wrongful death benefits to same-sex couples serves no legitimate governmental purpose. Judicial precedent has established that confining marriage and all laws pertaining either directly or indirectly to the marital relationship to different sex couples is not violative of equal protection under either the state or federal constitutions. Furthermore, the theories of full faith and credit and comity were inapplicable to grant plaintiff standing to maintain the wrongful death action. Vermont law, although affording same-sex couples in a civil union relationship the same rights as those afforded married couples, did not equate civil union relationships with traditional heterosexual marriage, and thus did not alter traditional concepts of marriage.

Matter of Seymour v. Nichols


97133


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


21 A.D.3d 1234; 801 N.Y.S.2d 426; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 9542; 2005 NY Slip Op 6974



September 29, 2005, Decided 
September 29, 2005, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, which was initiated under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 506(b)(1), petitioner pistol permit applicant sought review of a determination by respondent judge to deny the applicant's request for a pistol perm under N.Y. Penal Law § 400.00(1)(e).


OVERVIEW: The applicant was denied a pistol permit on the ground that the revocation of his prior pistol permit posed an automatic bar to reissuance pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 400.00(1)(e). He commenced an N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to declare that N.Y. Penal Law § 400.00(1)(e) was unconstitutional. Since the applicant sought a declaration on the constitutionality of the statute, the court converted the proceeding into a declaratory judgment action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 103(c). As to the statute, the court found that as long as the applicant had an opportunity for a hearing on the prior revocation, the statute did not violate due process. Moreover, the State had a legitimate governmental interest in preventing an applicant whose previous permit was revoked from receiving a new one and the statute bore a rational relationship to that legitimate governmental interest.


OUTCOME: The court converted the proceeding to an action for a declaratory judgment, and declared that the statute preventing the permit was not unconstitutional. The court confirmed the determination denying the permit.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Proceeding against Body or Officer--Conversion of Article 78 Proceeding to Declaratory Judgment Action.--Fact that Penal Law §400.00 (1) (e) does not afford hearing to applicants seeking reissuance did not constitute violation of procedural due process; as long as applicant was provided with opportunity to be heard in connection with prior revocation proceeding underlying denial of reissuance, there was no need for additional hearing where application was denied based solely upon prior revocation--Penal Law §400.00 (1) (e) does not violate equal protection by facially discriminating against applicants whose previous permits were revoked; no suspect class or fundamental right was involved, and there was rational relationship to legitimate governmental interest since Legislature's licensing scheme prevents applicant whose previous permit was revoked from receiving new one and insures that only persons of acceptable background and character are permitted to carry handguns.

Hunter v. Bd. of Supervisors


97288


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


21 A.D.3d 622; 800 N.Y.S.2d 231; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 8280



August 4, 2005, Decided 
August 4, 2005, Entered


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, proprietors of companies who were subject to N.Y. Tax Law § 1202-u and Warren County, N.Y., Local Law 4, sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which, inter alia, converted the proprietors' application into an action for a declaratory judgment (DJ), and thereafter dismissed causes of action. The action was brought against defendant county board of supervisors.


OVERVIEW: The board made a municipal home rule request for enactment of a state law, authorizing a tax on the rental proceeds of hotel and motel rooms. In response, the state legislature passed § 1202-u, and the county enacted Warren County, N.Y., Local Law 4, which imposed such tax. The proprietors brought an action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 which challenged the constitutionality of the enabling and the local legislation. The board sought dismissal and the proprietors sought a preliminary injunction to prohibit imposition of the tax. The trial court converted the matter into a DJ action, struck a provision in the local law, and dismissed the remainder of the matter. On appeal, the court found that the payment of the tax due or an undertaking in order to seek judicial review did not render the local law unconstitutional, the Ex Post Facto Clause was inapplicable, and the Contracts Clause of U.S. Const. art. 1, § 10 was not violated. Further, there was no violation of the dormant Commerce Clause, nor of the due process clause or the equal protection clause, based on the similar treatment to in-state and out-of-state tourists. N.Y. Tax Law § 1202-u was not repealed by implication.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

 [***1] Taxation--Hotel and Motel Occupancy Tax--Judicial Review--Condition Precedent.--In action challenging local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax, provisions of local law requiring undertaking or payment of tax due as condition precedent to judicial review were constitutional.

Statutes--Repeal by Implication--Hotel and Motel Occupancy Tax.--There was no evidence that Legislature, by enacting law authorizing occupancy tax for one county, intended to repeal identical authorization for another county and each of laws could be given effect without conflicting with one another.

Constitutional Law--Ex Post Facto Law--Hotel and Motel Occupancy Tax.--Contention that local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax could be retroactively applied in contravention of Ex Post Facto Clause of US Constitution was rejected--Ex Post Facto Clause prohibits legislation that makes criminal act not criminal when committed or increases punishment for previously committed offenses and, thus, had no applicability to legislation at issue.

Constitutional Law--Contract Clause--Hotel and Motel Occupancy Tax.--Local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax did not impair obligation of contracts within meaning of Contract Clause of US Constitution (see US Const, art I, § 10)--Contract Clause does not prevent enactment of legislation that is reasonably necessary to further important public purpose even where such legislation may incidentally affect existing contractual obligations--purpose of legislation was to provide funds for promotion of tourism in county, recognizable public interest benefitting all of citizens of county.

Parties--Standing.--Plaintiffs lacked standing to assert claim that local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax was void for failure to exempt Indian tribes from its coverage in violation of Tax Law § 1116 since they failed to demonstrate any injury that they, as owners and operators of nontribal accommodations, would suffer as result of failure of local law to exempt Indian tribes from its reach.

Taxation--Hotel and Motel Occupancy Tax--Impermissible Tax on Real Estate.--Local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax did not constitute impermissible tax on real estate in violation of NY Constitution; tax on use of real estate is not to be tax on real estate itself.

Taxation--Hotel and Motel Occupancy Tax--Due Process--Equal Protection.--Contention that local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax was violative of due process and equal protection because time limits regarding when taxpayers may seek refund were insufficient was rejected since all taxpayers seeking refund or subject to assessment of additional taxes were subject to same time limitations.

Constitutional Law--Commerce Clause--Hotel and Motel Occupancy tax.--Contention that local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax violated dormant Commerce Clause, which prohibits state taxation that discriminates or unduly burdens interstate commerce, was rejected; local law was nondiscriminatory on its face and did not accord differential treatment of in-state and out-of-state economic interests that benefitted former and burdened latter; local law impacted in-state and out-of-state tourists in same way and did not operate to disadvantage of any identifiable interstate market.

Taxation--Hotel and Motel Occupancy Tax--Applicability of CPL.--Contention that local law imposing hotel and motel occupancy tax was unconstitutional with respect to penalties it imposed upon those found to be in violation of law, in that it did not expressly provide for application of Criminal Procedure Law in enforcement and imposition of criminal sanctions provided therein, was reject; provision of Criminal Procedure Law are exclusively applicable to any and all criminal actions prosecuted in state.

 

598 of 1817 DOCUMENTS


People v. Conger


14555, 14854


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


19 A.D.3d 938; 798 N.Y.S.2d 169; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 7227



June 30, 2005, Decided 
June 30, 2005, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal denied by People v. Conger, 5 NY3d 827, 837 NE2d 740, 2005 N.Y. LEXIS 3008, 804 NYS2d 41 (N.Y., Sept. 30, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The County Court of Chemung County, New York, convicted defendant of three counts of burglary in the second degree and three counts of petit larceny. Two prior felony convictions were proved at a hearing. The trial court adjudicated defendant a persistent felony offender, and he was sentenced as such to concurrent prison terms of 15 years to life. The trial court denied defendant's motion to set aside the sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the statute pursuant to which he was adjudicated a persistent felony offender, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law art. 400.20, was unconstitutional because it permitted enhancement of a sentence based on facts not found by a jury. Any fact, other than a prior conviction, that increased the penalty for a crime beyond the statutorily prescribed maximum had to be found by a jury and proved beyond a reasonable doubt, absent a waiver by a defendant. The instant court found that this rule was not violated in New York because a defendant's prior felony convictions were the sole determinant of whether a defendant was subject to enhanced sentencing as a persistent felony offender. Although the trial court had discretion not to impose a sentence within the enhanced range, N.Y. Penal Law § 70.10(2), it was the prior convictions rather than the trial court's finding that the history and character of defendant and the circumstances of his criminal conduct warranted extended incarceration that initially subjected defendant to enhanced sentencing. Defendant failed to show that his sentence was unconstitutional. Defendant's remaining arguments were unpersuasive.


OUTCOME: The judgment and the order were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes--Sentence--Persistent Felony Offender.--Contention that statute pursuant to which defendant was adjudicated persistent felony offender is unconstitutional because it permits enhancement of sentence based on facts not found by jury (see CPL 400.20) was rejected--while any fact, other than prior conviction, that increases penalty for crime beyond statutorily prescribed maximum must be found by jury and proven beyond reasonable doubt, absent waiver by defendant, defendant's prior felony convictions were sole determinant of whether he was subject to enhanced sentencing as persistent felony offender.

Leskinen v. Fusco


5251, 5252


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, FIRST DEPARTMENT


18 A.D.3d 387; 796 N.Y.S.2d 54; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 5718



May 31, 2005, Decided 
May 31, 2005, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal granted by, in part Leskinen v. Fusco, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 10772 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Oct. 6, 2005)
Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Leskinen v. Fusco, 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 11783 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Nov. 3, 2005)
Appeal dismissed by Leskinen v. Fusco, 6 N.Y.3d 807, 845 N.E.2d 1276, 2006 N.Y. LEXIS 247, 812 N.Y.S.2d 445 (2006)
Sanctions allowed by Leskinen v. Fusco, 2006 N.Y. Misc. LEXIS 3109 (2006)
Related proceeding at Matter of Shearer, 2012 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 1733 (N.Y. App. Div. 1st Dep't, Mar. 13, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Bronx County, New York, in a dispute over attorney fees in plaintiff's medical malpractice action, (1) denied appellant law firm's motion and cross-appellant lawyer's motion for relief, (2) continued a prior order directing that the fee be deposited with a county clerk pending further court order, and (3) pursuant to N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, pt. 130, imposed financial sanctions on them. The firm and lawyer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The lower court's order was not an unconstitutional attachment or other improper restraint on the payment of the firm's fee. The firm attempted to appropriate the entire fee through the subterfuge of a proposed compromise order that did not disclose the lawyer's claim to half of the fee and that such claim was then being actively litigated before another justice. Once the court learned of the lawyer's claim shortly after signing the compromise, it appropriately exercised its discretion to grant relief from an improperly obtained order, under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5015(a)(3), by temporarily staying payment of attorney fees and disbursements. The record did not support the firm's claim that the lawyer was not entitled to share in the fee because he violated N.Y. Jud. Law § 478 by engaging in the unauthorized practice of law in New York. The award of sanctions against the lawyer was vacated since he did nothing to impair or impede payment of the settlement funds to plaintiff. Under N.Y. Jud. Law § 475, the lawyer was to receive 50 percent of the legal fee deposited with the clerk since the fee-sharing agreement clearly provided for an equal split of the legal fee recovered in the action.


OUTCOME: The appeals court modified the order (1) by vacating the sanctions and vacating the award of interest on the amount deposited to plaintiff and (2) by ordering that 50 percent of the deposited fee and accrued interest was to be paid to the lawyer. The court remanded the case to the lower court for further proceedings and to decide whether sanctions were to imposed and interest awarded against the firm and affirmed the remainder of the order.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and Client--Compensation.--Appellant's claim that orders on appeal constituted unconstitutional attachment or other improper restraint on payment of its fee was rejected--appellant attempted to appropriate entire fee in medical malpractice action through subterfuge of proposed infant's compromise order that did not disclose cross appellant's claim to half of fee and fact that such claim was being litigated before another justice; once court learned of cross appellant's claim shortly after signing infant's compromise, it granted relief from improperly obtained order by temporarily staying payment of portion of infant's compromise constituting attorneys' fees and disbursements.

Attorney and Client--Unauthorized Practice of Law.--In connection with dispute between attorneys over division of fee earned in medical malpractice action, record did not support appellant's claim that cross appellant was not entitled to share in fee because he engaged in unauthorized practice of law in New York, in violation of Judiciary Law § 478; at no time did cross appellant ever appear in court, conduct deposition or hold himself out as entitled to practice in New York, and retainer agreement was clear that New York attorney would have to be retained in event of litigation.

Attorney and Client--Division of Fees.--Cross appellant was entitled to receive 50% of legal fee at issue--fee-sharing agreement between appellant and cross appellant clearly provided for 50/50 split of legal fee recovered in action; there was no evidence to support appellant's contention that division of fee depended on nature and extent of services performed.

Novara ex rel. Jones v. Cantor Fitzgerald, LP


96513


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, THIRD DEPARTMENT


20 A.D.3d 103; 795 N.Y.S.2d 133; 2005 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 4861



May 5, 2005, Decided 
May 5, 2005, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal denied by Novara ex rel. Jones v. Cantor Fitzgerald, LP, 5 NY3d 710, 837 NE2d 733, 2005 N.Y. LEXIS 2574, 804 NYS2d 34 (N.Y., Sept. 20, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The New York Workers' Compensation Board (Board) affirmed an order granting death benefits to appellee, decedent's domestic partner, after the attacks of September 11, 2001, pursuant to then pending legislation related to N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 4. However, the Board declined to reach the constitutional issues raised by the other appellants, the daughter's mother, the employer, and its carrier. This appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: The mother challenged the judgment entered. However, although the appeals court agreed with the mother that N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 4 created an inequity in the law, such disparate treatment did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Constitution. Second, § 4 did not violate N.Y. Const. art. I, § 18, as the legislature was within its discretion to include domestic partners in the class of persons entitled to share in a decedent's workers' compensation death benefit. Third, because the reduction in benefits was prospective only, nothing that the mother's daughter already possessed was taken from her. Moreover, even if the mother were correct that her daughter had a vested property interest in the continued receipt of the decedent's death benefits in a specified amount, due process required nothing more than that a claimant be given notice and an opportunity to be heard, which was provided. Fourth, because nothing more than an expectancy interest was at issue, no unconstitutional taking occurred. Finally, the mother's assertion that N.Y. Workers' Comp. Law § 4 constituted an invalid delegation of legislative power lacked merit.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Workers' Compensation Death Benefits Resulting from Employee's Death in September 11, 2001 Terrorist Attacks

Workers' Compensation Law § 4, which afforded the domestic partners of employees killed in the September 11, 2001 terrorist attacks the same death benefits available to surviving spouses, was not unconstitutional as applied to claimant, whose daughter's workers' compensation death benefit resulting from decedent's death in the terrorist attacks had to be shared with decedent's domestic partner. The disparate treatment of dependent children based upon whether their fathers were killed in the September 11, 2001 terrorist attacks was not violative of equal protection under the "rational basis" test. The challenged statutory classification bears a rational relationship to the State's legitimate governmental interest in aiding its citizens in a time of crisis and preventing further inflation of the welfare rolls. Nor was the inclusion of domestic partners in the statute violative of the "trade off" underlying the workers' compensation provisions of the State Constitution. New York Constitution, article I, § 18 grants the Legislature the power to create an exclusive system of compensation for injuries sustained by employees, including the power to define who should be entitled to relief for damages. Furthermore, the prospective reduction in the benefits awarded to claimant's daughter did not result in either a due process violation or a Takings Clause violation.
